Title: Floating offshore wind energy: Techno-socio-economic 
perspectives and their influence on wind farm design and optimization 
DTU Wind Energy-M-0467 
July 2021 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical University of Denmark 
with a unique integration of research, education, innovation and public/private 
sector consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities develop new 
opportunities and technology for the global and Danish exploitation of wind 
energy. Research focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are central 
for the development, innovation and use of wind energy and provides the 
basis for advanced education at the education. 
Project period:  
January – July 2021

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
As countries around the world aim to increase their renewable energy content, ﬂoating
wind farms will allow the oﬀshore wind industry to expand into deeper waters. The
development of ﬂoating wind energy at commercial scale is a complex, multidisciplinary
challenge that the industry is just beginning to face. This thesis deconstructs the ways
local context inﬂuences the formation of a ﬂoating wind farm’s site and layout, and how
it in turn aﬀects the overall proﬁtability of the project. An interdisciplinary lens is used
to examine wind farm development through both a case study of the planning process
for oﬀshore wind in Morro Bay, California, and a quantitative model for wind farm site
choice and layout optimization.
Morro Bay, located oﬀthe coast of Central California, has been chosen as a case study,
because it is the state’s largest area currently being considered for oﬀshore wind energy
and it has been entrenched in political gridlock. Actor Network Theory, a framework
used in Science and Technology Studies, is applied to reveal a wind farm siting process
that prioritizes a few central actors, as well as a wind farm layout design network that is
made more complex with the introduction of ﬂoating wind technology.
The latter part of the thesis moves from the socio-technical to the techno-economic
frame to explore the perspective of a developer. A model integrating newly-developed
wake and cost models minimizes the Levelized Cost of Energy of a 1 GW ﬂoating wind
farm in the 2018 Morro Bay Call Area. This study highlights the importance of mooring
system design, which in deep waters (> 600 m) both drives cost and inﬂuences the
distances between turbines due to their footprint on the seabed.
Stakeholder-motivated scenarios link the socio-technical and the techno-economic
analysis, and give key insights into the synergies and trade-oﬀs between those sites and
layouts that are technically optimal and those that are desirable to key actors in the wind
farm planning process. It is found that in the case of Morro Bay regular optimization
techniques may allow for both economic and social gains.
This thesis lies at the boundary where local stakeholders, technology, regulation, and
economics of a ﬂoating wind farm intersect. This study concludes that to viably and
economically develop ﬂoating wind farms in California, there must be a coordinated eﬀort
between the socio-technical analysis and techno-economic design and optimization – two
ﬁelds that are traditionally separate but must converge in the emergence of a wind farm.

Preface
This thesis was prepared at the department of Wind Energy at the Technical University
of Denmark in fulﬁllment of the requirements for acquiring a Master of Science degree in
Wind Energy.
The research described in this thesis was conducted under the supervision of Katherine
Dykes and Niels-Erik Clausen at DTU and in collaboration with Cecilie Langhans at
Ørsted.
Copenhagen, July 2, 2021
Isabelle Anthony Bauman & Paula Emilie Velmans

Acknowledgements
This thesis would not have been possible without our incredible army of supporters. First,
we’d like to extend a massive thank you to our supervisors at DTU, Niels-Erik Clausen
and Katherine Dykes, who have not only provided guidance and invaluable insights but
also went out of their way to connect us to others who have helped us along the way.
Next, we’d like to thank those who have supported us from Ørsted, namely Cecilie
Langhans, Max Cohen, and James Sun, who have given us an industry perspective that
helped shape our thesis. A special thank you to Rachel Meyer and Maria Hoﬀman, who
were instrumental in the brainstorming phase for this project.
We’d also like to extend our gratitude to Julia Kirch Kirkegaard and Tom Cronin, who
taught us about Actor-Network Theory, gave valuable feedback for our project, inspired
us to study the social impact of renewable energy in the ﬁrst place.
Next we’d like to thank Mads Pederson and Rafael Rodrigues for their time and assistance
in setting up and debugging our project in TopFarm. We’d also like to thank Sebastian
Sanchez Perez-Moreno and Matthew Shields for their time and assistance in developing
our optimization model.
Finally, we’d like to thank all twenty of the individuals that we interviewed for this thesis
– our project would not be what it is without your input and perspectives!
From Isabelle: I would like to thank my friends and family for their everyday support
throughout this process – in particular I’d like to thank Itzel for being a fantastic ﬂatmate,
Gülin for all our days at the library, and my wonderful parents, Jill and John Bauman,
for their endless love, guidance, and inspiration.
From Paula: Thank you to my friends and family for their support throughout the
last months. Especially, I’d like to extend a massive thank you to Doro, for always
cheering me up, my sister Clara and my parents Caroline and Frank Velmans for their
never-ending support and love.
Most of all, Isabelle and Paula would like to thank each other for not killing one another
in the process1.
1We both are very grateful to have worked as a team that functioned so well – it made the process
all the more enjoyable, even in lockdown!

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
In the international eﬀort to abate climate change, wind energy has emerged as an
essential contributor to a future sustainable energy mix. Global wind energy deployment
has rapidly increased in recent years from 120 GW in 2008 to 743 GW installed capacity in
2020 (Lee and Zhao, 2021). The development of oﬀshore wind energy in 2008 is expanding,
as it brings several advantages, including increased, more stable wind resources as well
as reduced noise and visual impacts (Musial and Ram, 2010).
Many countries face a lack of shallow water sites suitable for bottom-ﬁxed oﬀshore
wind development. The current industry standard bottom-ﬁxed technologies become
uneconomical at waters deeper than 60 m (Walter Musial, Beiter, Tegen, et al., 2016).
Floating wind technology – wind turbines mounted on ﬂoating substructures anchored
to the seabed with mooring lines – provides means to access wind resources at greater
depths. Yet ﬂoating oﬀshore wind is not as mature an industry as its ﬁxed-bottom
counterpart – the largest ﬂoating wind project yet commissioned is Hywind Scotland,
which has a total installed capacity of 30 MW (Floating oﬀshore wind in Equinor -
equinor.com 2021).
In the United States (US) the highest oﬀshore wind energy potential (in GW) is
found in waters deeper than 60 m, as seen in Figure 1.1. Notably, California (CA) has
588 GW oﬀshore wind energy potential of which 98% is over deep water (over 60 m).
Subsequently, is it clear that ﬂoating wind technology is critical to commercially develop
and capture the wind energy potential in CA.
Figure 1.1: US Oﬀshore Wind Potential classiﬁed by water depth (Musial and Ram,
2010)
==PAGE:20==
2
1 Introduction
In this thesis, we explore the potential for the growth of ﬂoating oﬀshore wind oﬀ
the coast of CA through a multidisciplinary lens. A wind farm is a heterogeneous
socio-techno-economic system – its existence can be expressed as a network of technical,
economic, political, and social actors and forces. At each stage of development, from
conception to decommissioning, a wind farm is shaped by and impacts myriad actors,
from the engineers who design its turbines to the local communities for whom it provides
electricity.
Hence, to fully understand and describe the emergence of a ﬂoating wind farm in CA
we must examine its planning and development from multiple perspectives. The Bureau
of Ocean Management (BOEM) Morro Bay Call Area oﬀthe coast of Central CA is
chosen as a case study for socio-technical and techno-economic analysis that provides
cross-disciplinary insights into the construction and impact of a wind farm’s site and
layout.
1.1
Project Scope
We begin the thesis with a socio-technical analysis of potential development space. This
analysis employs Actor-Network Theory (ANT), a theoretical framework from Science
and Technology Studies (STS), to explore the relationships between the actors active
in CA’s wind energy planning process. This section maps socio-technical networks that
arise from supporting and opposing socio-technical actors using interview data from
industry professionals involved with and knowledgeable about ﬂoating wind farms and
the CA West Coast as a wind energy development region.
The second half of the thesis examines the Morro Bay Call Area with a techno-
economic lens – a wind energy developer’s perspective is further explored through
optimization of a 1 GW wind farm for Levelized Cost of Energy (LCOE)1. The model
integrates cutting-edge cost and energy production modeling tools developed by the
National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) and DTU for an LCOE model that
introduces cost impacts of novel ﬂoating wind technology. In the construction of a
techno-economic model for the case study, socio-technical controversy is mapped to the
quantitative design space through the introduction of siting and layout design scenarios
motivated by stakeholder perspectives.
Crucial to this thesis is the discussion of the reciprocal inﬂuence of socio-technical
and techno-economic analyses. A transition chapter demonstrates the process of mapping
social networks to a techno-economic frame through the choice of LCOE as an optimizer
and the designation of physical constraints on wind farm site and layout informed by
local stakeholders. Finally, optimization results are analyzed for their impact on the
actors considered in the initial case study – we reﬂect upon the trade-oﬀs in design that
may be necessary to achieve results that are both socially acceptable and ﬁnancially
feasible.
1LCOE is a metric broadly used to reﬂect the cost to the developer of producing electricity over the
lifetime of the wind farm, and is often employed by researchers and developers as a metric for assessing
novel technology.
==PAGE:21==
1.1 Project Scope
3
1.1.1
Research Questions
This thesis aims to answer the following overarching research question and sub-questions:
How does local context inﬂuence the site and layout of a ﬂoating wind farm and hence its
ability to be cost-competitive in future energy systems?
The following sub-questions are introduced to answer the question above and explore the
resource space:
• What are relevant (technical, regulatory, economic) forces supporting and con-
straining the development of ﬂoating wind?
• How do these constraints impact an optimization of ﬂoating wind farm layout for
proﬁtability?
• What changes in wind farm layout with the introduction of ﬂoating wind technology?
– How will the costs associated with wind farm development change with ﬂoating
technology?
1.1.2
Limitations
In both socio-technical and techno-economic analysis, the scope of the thesis is limited
by time constraints, data availability, and model development, notably the following:
Socio-technical Analysis
• Interview perspectives: twenty industry professionals were interviewed to inform
the stakeholder analysis. It is acknowledged that those who were interviewed all
work in the oﬀshore wind industry and may have a bias towards the development
of ﬂoating wind in CA.
• Interview perspectives: many of the key stakeholders discussed in this thesis, for
example, the ﬁshing and shipping industries and local citizens, were not interviewed
due to time constraints (although included in analysis through comments they have
submitted to the regulating entity). The absence of those opinions may cause the
analysis to inadvertently privilege the perspectives of those who were interviewed.
• Authors’ bias: both authors of this thesis study and work in the wind industry,
which may bring inherent bias towards oﬀshore wind development.
==PAGE:22==
4
1 Introduction
Techo-economic optimization
The second half of this thesis includes an optimization of wind farm macro- and
micro-siting (depicted in Figure 1.2 below).
Figure 1.2: Cost eﬀective Oﬀshore Wind Farm Optimization (Hou et al., 2019). Compo-
nents in blue are altered in this thesis. Components in purple are not included.
• Timing: As this predicts a wind farm that might be built in 5-10 years, it is
anticipated that many values used in this thesis will change with time, including
associated system and installation costs as well as the power output of turbines.
• Mooring Model: the mooring model employed is conservative from a cost-perspective,
as hydrodynamic interactions between wave and wind and ﬂoater and mooring
design are not included; information about soil conditions are not suﬃcient for a
grounded anchor selection; also load to anchors are not considered.
• Electrical System: this thesis does not optimize the electrical system layout (array
and export cables). Figure 1.2 demonstrates that electrical components and voltage
levels are selected, but outside of the optimization loop. In addition, High Voltage
Alternating Current (HVAC) export cables are chosen as the costing model has not
yet been developed to support High Voltage Direct Current (HVDC) cable cost.
• Oﬀshore Substation (OSS): this thesis does not optimize for the location of the
OSS near the wind farm. In addition, the costing model is not equipped to model
ﬂoating OSS costs, resulting in high uncertainty for OSS costs.
• Floating Sub-structure: the ﬂoating substructure is only included in cost modeling
and not the wake model. The impact of ﬂoating substructures on energy yield must
be explored in future research.
• Operational Expenditures (OpEx): the annual operational expenditures are taken
as a constant value, and sensitivity analysis is conducted. Hence, LCOE values do
==PAGE:23==
1.2 Thesis Structure
5
not reﬂect the impact of site and layout on operational expenditures.
• Weather data for Capital Expenditures (CapEx): Monthly-averaged weather data
from the years 2011-2019 is used, which may smooth out signiﬁcant weather events
and decrease the modeled installation CapEx.
1.2
Thesis Structure
• Chapter 2 introduces the case study of Morro Bay, CA, painting a picture of the
area, its environment, wind resource, and status in the development timeline of
oﬀshore wind in CA.
• Chapter 3 applies Actor-Network Theory to explore and map the socio-technical
construction of a wind farm oﬀthe coast of CA. For this analysis, exploratory
stakeholder interviews with industry professionals and researchers with insights
into the prospects of the region are conducted. Some key interview groups include:
– Members of the Task Force for Oﬀshore Wind in CA, who serve as key
decision-makers and planners
– Leading researchers in oﬀshore wind at diﬀerent research centers including,
with expertise in wind resource and cost modeling
– Floating substructure developer
– Representatives from Oﬀshore Wind CA: developers and lobbyists with an
interest in the development of oﬀshore wind in CA
Interview ﬁndings and public comments are used to describe the actors within and
dynamics of the socio-technical networks that shape a wind farm’s site and its
layout – two key components of its social and technical construction.
• Chapter 4 reﬂects on the process of mapping socio-technical networks to a quanti-
tative techno-economic design space that centers the lens of a wind farm developer.
We highlight and critique the choice of LCOE as a metric for optimization as it
prioritizes the voices of some groups over others, and oﬀer a method of including
stakeholders’ voices through spatial constraints in a model.
• Chapter 5 provides the background of ﬂoating wind farms as well as the base
technological and cost assumptions for the model development.
• Chapter 6 describes the model formulation including the energy yield model,
mooring line model, and CapEx model. In addition, optimization scenarios moti-
vated by stakeholder perspectives are introduced and the optimization procedure is
detailed.
• Chapter 7 displays and analyzes optimization results and the results of technical
and socially-motivated scenarios are examined for techno-economic impact.
• Chapter 8 reﬂects on the impacts of wind farm layout design in the previous
==PAGE:24==
6
1 Introduction
chapter on the social networks presented in ANT analysis.
• Chapter 9 concludes with a reﬂection on the importance of the integration of the
social, technical, and economic when evaluating the potential for the development
of renewable energy systems. Proposed directions for multidisciplinary study of
ﬂoating wind farms are included.
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2
Case Study: Morro Bay,
California
This chapter introduces the case of a ﬂoating wind farm in Morro Bay, CA through
tracing the development of a wind farm, from its ﬁrst stages as the aim of federal and state
renewable energy ambitions to the environmental impacts of its physical manifestation.
It provides the reader the regulatory, geographical, and environmental landscape that
underpin the relationships that are further explored in Chapter 3.
2.1
Political Climate and Planning Process
The emergence of a wind farm begins at the federal and state level, where political support
and renewable development targets spur on the federal and state planning process for
oﬀshore wind. In this section, we explore the ambitions of the US and CA that might
support the growth of oﬀshore wind on the West Coast and, introduce the current status
of BOEM’s planning process in the region.
2.1.1
Federal and State Policy
A precondition for the development of wind energy is the support of political bodies.
Oﬀshore wind in CA has demonstrated support at both the federal and state level.
2.1.1.1
Federal Ambitions
The US federal government introduced an energy policy shift in the US that places
increased focus on supporting the growth of the oﬀshore wind industry (Digges, 2021).
The administration has set a target to reach 100% renewable electricity generation, to
achieve a carbon pollution-free power sector by 2035, and to reach a net-zero emissions
economy in the entire U.S. at the latest by 2050 (White House, 2021).
Additionally, the federal government set interim targets for oﬀshore wind energy
development. In March 2021 the administration announced to deploy 30 GW oﬀshore
wind in the united states waters by 2030 (Digges, 2021).
2.1.1.2
California State Goals
The state of CA also sets an aim of 100% renewable energy production. “The 100 Percent
Clean Energy Act of 2018,” Senate Bill 100 (SB 100, De León) was approved in September
==PAGE:26==
8
2 Case Study: Morro Bay, California
2018 (Newsom, 2021). SB100 is a policy landmark that requires a 100% renewable energy
and zero-carbon resources supply of electric retail sales by 2045 (Newsom, 2021).
Moreover, in February 2021 a new Assembly Bill (AB) 525 was proposed and is
currently in the approval process (Chiu, Cunningham, and Friedman, 2021). AB 525
would require CA to produce 10 GW of electricity by oﬀshore wind facilities by 2040. The
bill also includes an interim goal to install 3 GW oﬀshore wind by 2030 in federal waters
oﬀthe coast of CA (Renews, 2021). The proposed AB 525 would require the States
Energy Commission to evaluate and establish strategic planning for oﬀshore wind oﬀthe
coast of CA. Although it is uncertain whether this bill will be passed, it demonstrates
increased conviction to include oﬀshore wind in CA’s energy mix.
2.1.2
Oﬀshore Wind Planning Process
Federal and state oﬀshore wind ambitions are then acted upon by those responsible for
the process of siting, leasing, and regulating oﬀshore wind development.
2.1.2.1
Regulatory Agency - BOEM
The Bureau of Ocean Energy Management (BOEM) is the leasing regulatory agency for
energy development, including renewable energies, in oﬀshore areas in the U.S. Outer
Continental Shelf (OCS)(Winston & Strawn, 2021). The OCS deﬁnes "all submerged lands
lying seaward of state coastal waters (3 miles oﬀshore) which are under U.S. jurisdiction"
(BOEM, 2021c).
BOEM emphasizes responsible energy development and resource
management in the OCS while engaging with the diﬀerent stakeholders throughout the
whole process (Winston & Strawn, 2021).
The framework of BOEM’s Wind Energy Commercial Leasing Process, shown in
Figure 2.1, consists of four distinct phases (Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, 2017).
1. Planning and Analysis: BOEM identiﬁes Call Areas, which are areas suitable for
oﬀshore wind energy development, and publishes the Call for Information and
Nominations, where all stakeholders can submit comments regarding the Call Areas.
Finally, BOEM identiﬁes priority Wind Energy Areas (WEAs) oﬀshore.
2. Leasing: Auctions for the WEAs take place. The leases are issued in either a
competitive or non-competitive process. In competitive leasing, BOEM informs
the public and developers through a Sales Notice followed by a lease sale. In
a non-competitive lease, BOEM will negotiate a lease. After the auction, the
developer has to deploy a plan for the lease area, which needs to be approved by
BOEM.
3. Site Assessment: The phase includes the submission of a Site Assessment Plan
(SAP) and the conduction of site characterizations (meteorological and geographic)
surveys and environmental studies.
4. Construction and Operations: The developer submits a Construction and Operation
Plan (COP) and a decommissioning plan for the oﬀshore wind farm. Finally, after
BOEM’s approval, the developer can begin the installation of the wind farm.
==PAGE:27==
2.1 Political Climate and Planning Process
9
Figure 2.1: BOEMs’ Wind Energy Commercial Leasing Process (Kearns&West, 2018b)
In 2016 BOEM formed the CA Intergovernmental Renewable Energy Task Force,
with members of state, local, and federally recognized tribal governments and federal
agencies. The task force builds a platform for public engagement, to discuss stakeholders’
concerns, exchange environmental data, and encourage and facilitate stakeholder engage-
ment throughout the growth of oﬀshore wind energy in CA (BOEM, 2017). Current
development in the region is between the Planning and Analysis and Leasing phase.
Leases are expected to occur in 2022 (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021).
2.1.2.2
Call Areas
The 2018 Call Areas in CA provide a resource potential of 8.5 GW, assuming an oﬀshore
wind power density of 3 MW/km2 (Collier et al., 2019). The two Call Areas Morro Bay
and Diablo Canyon are located oﬀthe central coast of CA between San Francisco and
Los Angeles. The location of the Humboldt Call Area is in North CA oﬀthe coast of
Eureka, as seen in Figure 2.2.
==PAGE:28==
10
2 Case Study: Morro Bay, California
Figure 2.2: Call Areas in CA in 2018 (Oﬀshore Wind California, 2021)
Detailed data about the Call Areas, including CA marine and coastal energy, manage-
ment, ecology, natural resources, ﬁshing, and traditional uses are provided in the Oﬀshore
Renewable Wind Energy Gateway (Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, California
Public Utilities Commission, and California Energy Commission, 2021). The Gateway
functions as a platform for data and information exchange of biological and physical
resources and ocean uses (Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, California Public
Utilities Commission, and California Energy Commission, 2021). Throughout this thesis,
the tool is used to describe the environmental and commercial activities in the Call Area.
2.2
Morro Bay
Through the planning process, the wind farm’s site becomes increasingly speciﬁc –
evolving from state-level to Call Area and eventually to lease areas. Once the Call Area
location has been chosen, local environmental, technical, and political dynamics can be
analyzed. In this thesis, we explore the 2018 Morro Bay Call Area.
This section thereby provides a geographical, meteorological, and environmental
picture of the chosen area as a backdrop for further socio-technical analysis in Chapter
3. It is noted that this technical analysis constitutes a data-focused lens often used by
researchers and developers when constructing a wind farm (this is further discussed in
Section 3.8).
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Morro Bay
11
2.2.1
Overview
The two Call Areas on the central coast are not only nearby the major population areas,
by implication the major load centers in CA, San Francisco, and Los Angeles, as well as
existing available grid infrastructure onshore, as Collier (2020) describes the transmission
on the central coast a "plug and play" scenario. Therefore, the case study in this thesis is
chosen to be on the Morro Bay Call Area.
Morro Bay is located 38 kilometers oﬀshore from Cambria in central CA. The Call
Area covers 806 km2 and has a potential power capacity of 2.4 GW given a power density
of 3 MW/km2 (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020).
Figure 2.3: Morro Bay Call Area in 2018 (BOEM, 2021a)
2.2.2
Geographic Conditions
The bathymetry describes the geographic conditions of the area and includes the water
depth, seabed (soil) conditions. The water depths in the Morro Bay Call Area vary
between 800 m and 1100 m.
Tajalli Bakhsh et al. (2020) conclude in their report that there is little detailed soil
information about the Call Area available and identiﬁed research needs on site-speciﬁc
seabed conditions oﬀthe central coast of CA. The bathymetry inﬂuences the substructure
design/selection, which will be further elaborated in Section 5.2.4.1. Therefore, based on
Vigara et al. (2020) the soil conditions in the Morro Bay Call Area are assumed to be a
composition of sand and mud and are classiﬁed as medium dense sand/clay in this thesis.
==PAGE:30==
12
2 Case Study: Morro Bay, California
2.2.3
Meteorological Conditions
The meteorological conditions in the Call Area describe the wind and wave characteristics.
The wind rose and the Weibull distribution by sector is shown in Figure 2.4. The
wind data for the Call Area at 150m height is extracted from the Global Wind Atlas
(DTU, Vortex, and ESMAP, 2021). It can be observed that the Call Area has a dominant
wind direction from north-west between 285◦and 345◦. The mean wind speed is found
to be 7.5 m/s. The Weibull parameters A1 and k2 for the mean wind direction are
A = 7.5m/s and k = 1.75.
(a) Wind Rose
(b) Weibull Distribution
Figure 2.4: Wind data Morro Bay
The wave data are based on Station 4602, a buoy operated by National Data Buoy
Center located at 35.770 N 121.903 W (National Data Buoy Centre and National Oceanic
and Atmospheric Administration’s, 2021). The signiﬁcant wave height in the Call Area
is 2.5 m with a mean wave period of 7.5 s.
2.2.4
Environmental Conditions
Before brieﬂy describing the environmental conditions in the Call Area, we give an
overview of the environmental risks associated with ﬂoating oﬀshore wind technology.
2.2.4.1
Environmental Risks
An advantage of ﬂoating wind farms is that substructures are anchored to the seabed. Pile
driving is not needed, reducing the disruptive inﬂuence on the surrounded environment,
1A is the Weibull scale parameter – indicating the characteristic wind speed of the distribution
(Swiss Energy, 2021)
2k is the Weibull shape parameter – characterizing the form of the distribution (Swiss Energy, 2021)
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Morro Bay
13
especially for marine mammals. Moreover, the wind turbines are constructed in the
port and towed to the ﬁnal position, which also reduces the disruption of the marine
environment during the construction of the wind farm (Copping and Grear, 2018).
However, ﬂoating wind technology particularly the mooring system and the dynamic
cables introduces impacts on marine mammals. Copping and Grear (2018) conducted
a study on the potential risk of ﬂoating wind technology on Humpback Whales. The
potential identiﬁed risk for marine mammals "secondary entanglement", where marine life
becomes entangled with items such as ﬁshing nets that might wrap around mooring lines
or ﬂoating cables. Secondary entanglement thereby poses risk to marine mammals, ﬁsh,
and diving seabirds (Benjamins et al., 2014). The main environmental risk for seabirds
and migratory birds with oﬀshore wind turbines is associated with a collision with the
rotating blades (Morandi et al., 2018).
As the largest ﬂoating oﬀshore wind project installed globally is only 30MW and the
existing pilot projects installed are closer to shore, the environmental impact research is
limited (Collier, 2020). In their study on the environmental impacts of ﬂoating oﬀshore
wind in Central CA and Hawaii Morandi et al. (2018) analyzed the potential impacts
on diﬀerent seabird species, marine mammals, and small open-sea ﬁsh and concluded
the environmental sensitivity in the area is low. However, it must be noted that ﬂoating
oﬀshore wind technology is still in its beginning phase and therefore introduces uncertainty.
As a result, Collier (2020) states the need for funding and completion of environmental
impact research and following introducing mitigation strategies for a successful ﬂoating
oﬀshore wind development in CA.
2.2.4.2
National Marine Sanctuaries & Important Environmental
Areas
Figure 2.5 shows the locations of CAs’ National Marine Sanctuaries (NMS), Essential
Fish Habitat (EFH)3, and Habitat Areas of Particular Concern (HAPCs)4 for Paciﬁc
Coast Groundﬁsh. EFH areas and HAPCs are identiﬁed by the National Oceanic and
Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) Fisheries and the Paciﬁc Fishery Management
Council. Both EFH and HAPCs are designated to protect those areas from destructive
activities and maintain a healthy ecosystem (West Coast Regional Oﬃce, 2020b).
Figure 2.5 shows that the Call Area is partly inside an HAPC and an EFH.
3An EFH is described as a "habitat necessary for ﬁsh to spawn, breed, feed, or grow to maturity"
(West Coast Regional Oﬃce, 2020a).
4HAPCs are a discrete subcategory of EFH areas. And are categorized as "high priority areas for
conservation, management, or research because they are important to ecosystem function, sensitive to
human activities, stressed by development, or are rare" (West Coast Regional Oﬃce, 2020b).

2.2.4.3
Marine Mammals
Important areas for marine mammals and distribution maps for diﬀerent marine mammals
oﬀthe coast of central CA are shown in Appendix A.
From Figure A.1 it can be observed that the Call Area is outside of biologically
important areas for Harbor Porpoises, Gray Whales, Blue Whales, and Humpback
Whales. Biological important areas include migratory corridors, reproductive areas,
feeding areas, and areas in which small and resident populations are concentrated
(Conservation Biology Institute, 2017)5.
The Maps A.2, A.3, A.4 and A.5 demonstrate the probability of appearance of an
animal in the Paciﬁc oﬀthe central coast of CA, of Humpback Whales, Blue Whales,
Elephant Seals, and Sea Lions tracked between June 2003 and January 2009 (Conservation
Biology Institute, 2017). The Figures A.2, A.3, A.4 and A.5 show a non-existing to a
small distribution of Humpback Whales and Sea Lions in the Call Area, whereas there is
a slightly higher occurrence of Blue Whales and Elephant Seals.
2.2.4.4
Seabirds
The seasonal seabird density oﬀthe coast of CA in the sample period of 1975 to 2008
is shown in the Appendix A. In fall and winter, shown in the Figures A.8 and A.9,
the seabird density is higher than in spring and summer. However, the seabird density
itself is not signiﬁcant without knowing the speciﬁc species, because diﬀerent species
5data collected in 2015
==PAGE:33==
2.2 Morro Bay
15
react diﬀerently to the wind turbines. Therefore, for an accurate assessment of the
environmental impact on the seabirds, additional data for each species occurrence in the
area is needed.
2.2.5
Grid Interconnection
As previously described in Section 2.2 the grid connection on the central coast of CA is
considered a "plug and play"(Collier, 2020) scenario, based on the two possible existing
options for the high voltage transmission onshore. Both options provide a minimum
transmission strength of at least 230 kV, which will be necessary to interconnect wind
farms with 1 GW capacity (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020). Figure 2.6 shows the
onshore electrical substations along the coast of CA and the transmission lines.
One option is the former substation of the 2014 decommissioned Power Plant Morro
Rock, located 61 km from the outer edge of the Call Area. The substation provides a
capacity of 668 MW (Collier, 2020). The facility is equipped with a cooling discharge
water tunnel with a direct entrance from the ocean that would provide a possible shore
landing point (LLC, 2016).
The Diablo Canyon Power Plant, located 66 km from the Call Area, will be decom-
missioned in 2024. Once the Power Plant is decommissioned the onshore substation
provides another option for the grid interconnection onshore with a capacity of 3 GW
(Collier, 2020).
Figure 2.6: Existing Transmission Infrastructure in CA (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020)
==PAGE:34==
16
2 Case Study: Morro Bay, California
2.2.6
Port Infrastructure
Floating oﬀshore wind turbines are assembled at the construction port and then towed out
to the ﬁnal oﬀshore site (Collier et al., 2019). For both bottom-ﬁxed and ﬂoating oﬀshore
wind turbines enough storage space in the construction and installation port is required
as well as crane facilities. Deep-water facilities and infrastructure are necessary, where
the clearance depends on the chosen substructure technology (the diﬀerent substructures
and their requirements are further elaborated in Section 5.2.2) (Collier et al., 2019).
Finally, from the port to the ﬁnal oﬀshore site there should be no air draft restrictions,
as the fully assembled turbines are then towed to their ﬁnal oﬀshore destination (Collier
et al., 2019).
These requirements pose a challenge for the choice of port for ﬂoating oﬀshore wind
in CA, as the ports in CA are found to be busy and working to capacity (Collier et al.,
2019). Port Hueneme, located in Oxnard, CA, is identiﬁed as the most favorable port for
ﬂoating oﬀshore wind development on the Central Coast despite a lack of availability
and space (Collier, 2020). Collier (2020) proposes to conduct a survey on the space
availability and governance of ports in the whole state of CA, to evaluate the upgrades
for a successful ﬂoating oﬀshore wind development. The challenges associated with the
port infrastructure are further discussed in Chapter 3.
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 3
The Socio-Technical Network
3.1
Introduction
This chapter applies Actor-Network Theory to describe and analyze socio-technical
networks to explore the construction of a ﬂoating wind farm as a resource space. In ANT,
the interactions of actors and their network of relationships form technology (Katherine
Dykes, 2016). In the formation of a wind farm from concept to material technology to a
ﬁnancial asset, the network that surrounds and deﬁnes the wind farm evolves as new
actors enter and exert agency.
In the current paradigm, the conception of a wind farm begins with the characterization
of a wind energy resource (Nadaï and Cointe, 2020) – the ﬁrst step in the "macro-siting"
process of a wind farm. The formation of a wind farm hence begins with its site, which is
socio-materially constructed from controversy1 in a network of actors with a stake in the
region of interest. Canonically, once a wind farm’s site is deﬁned, "micro-siting" of wind
turbines within that site follows. This process, otherwise called wind farm layout design,
introduces individual turbines into the wind farm’s actor-network2 through questions
of access and navigation. In this chapter, we will explore the entangled actor-networks
that deﬁne a wind farm’s site and layout. We ﬁrst examine each network separately as
snapshots in time and eventually discuss how these networks are enmeshed in each other
in the lifetime of a wind farm.
At the time of writing, oﬀshore wind in CA is in an early stage – between the Planning
and Leasing phases in BOEMs’ wind energy commercial leasing process, shown in Figure
3.1 below. Those actors present in today’s dialogue are necessarily those invested in
large-scale marine spatial planning and the siting of the WEAs, including federal agencies,
the Department of Defense (DOD), national organizations concerned with environmental
impact, ﬁshing, and shipping, local municipal governments, and oﬀshore wind energy
developers. Once the ﬁnal leasing process is completed, the network will evolve to include
a new set of actors with new interests – those concerning the wind farm’s layout and
access to the resource space.
1Controversy is a term used in STS to describe "shared uncertainty", characterized by involving many
actors, reﬂecting social dynamics, are "reduction-resistant", debated, and tied to the conﬂict (Venturini,
2010).
2ANT describes socio-technical networks as "actor-networks," also called "actor worlds," – both
terms refer to the same phenomenon. Callon, Rip, and Law (1986) describe the diﬀerence as "the term
actor-world emphasizes the way in which these worlds, built around the entities that create them, are
both uniﬁed and self-suﬃcient. The term actor-network emphasizes that they have a structure and that
this structure is susceptible to change"
==PAGE:36==

3 The Socio-Technical Network
Figure 3.1: Wind farm Site and Layout in BOEM’s Leasing Process
The chapter is structured as follows: the ﬁrst part sets the analytical frame, the
second and third parts introduce the case study and varying stakeholder perspectives
gathered through public comments and semi-structured interviews, and the fourth part
discusses the evolution of the Actor-Network surrounding new oﬀshore wind development
as a wind farm entity materializes through the design of site and layout.
3.2
Actor-Network Theory
ANT provides a framework to investigate the ways that heterogeneous socio-technical
actors3 relate to and construct actor-networks through their relationships4. ANT thus
seeks to map and trace connections, dependencies, and power-relationships among actor-
networks to open the ’black box’ of socio-technical processes (Kirkegaard and Nyborg,
2020). ANT approaches wind energy as a “hybrid problem”: a network of engineering,
economics, politics, and psychologies tied to the emergence of a wind farm (Jolivet and
Heiskanen, 2010) – this study approaches a ﬂoating wind farm as a hybrid problem with a
focus on the engineering, planning, economics, and politics of its early-stage development.
ANT is chosen as a framework for its focus on how socio-technical networks deﬁne
technology through entangled technological, economic, policy-related processes, which are
a part of the complex multidisciplinary system of a wind farm. ANT is chosen over other
social constructivist approaches that focus on the meaning-making of local community
3A socio-technical actor is deﬁned as any entity that has the agency to inﬂuence other actors in a
network. ANT is unique among STS frameworks (and is chosen for this analysis) in that these actors are
not constrained to human actors – socio-technical artifacts are considered to have the same potential to
inﬂuence the network (called generalized symmetry) (Callon, 1990). Heterogeneity refers to the inclusion
of human and non-human actors.
4ANT takes a constructivist, relational approach, which means it avoids all essentialist (true or false)
explanations to a phenomenon, rather than phenomenon are explained by their relationship to others
(Callon, 1990).
==PAGE:37==
3.2 Actor-Network Theory
19
members, who have not yet been heavily involved in or impacted by the wind farm
planning process at the current stage.
In this thesis, we examine the structure of the actor-networks that deﬁne a wind
farm site and layout which evolve as new actors enter and exert agency within these
networks. It should be noted that "an actor-world is a network of simpliﬁed entities which
in turn are other networks" (Callon, Rip, and Law, 1986). For example, a simpliﬁed
actor-network surrounding a wind farm might treat a local municipal government as one
actor, which is in itself a network of many actors.
3.2.1
ANT Deﬁnitions: Intermediaries, Translation, and
Framing/Overﬂowing
In the study of actor-networks, especially those centered on technology, emphasis is
placed on intermediaries – instruments that build and reinforce networks – “actors deﬁne
one another through the intermediaries which they put into circulation” and that “the
social can be read in the inscriptions that mark the intermediaries” (Callon, 1990). An
intermediary by deﬁnition does not have independent agency yet conveys meaning in
the network. In ANT, an intermediary can become an actor – it is co-created by other
actors in the network and, can transform to exercise independent agency.
Technical objects, such as wind turbines or wind farms, are constructed by and
inﬂuence the worlds around them – hence, "we cannot describe technical objects without
describing the actor-worlds that shape them in all their diversity and scope." (Callon, Rip,
and Law, 1986). In the life of a wind farm, the farm itself transforms from intermediary in
the design process to actor in its physical manifestation – in the design phase, regulators,
developers, and other agents contribute to the process of determining the coordinates
on maps where wind turbines might be built, but it is only when the turbines are
physically constructed that it impacts actors, such as the marine environment and ﬁshing
community that cohabit the area or are displaced. This chapter focuses on the design
phase, highlighting actor-networks in which the wind farm acts as an intermediary in
that it is inscribed by the actors that surround it.
A core concept of ANT is the act of translation – the act of exchanging information
between stakeholders. Callon (1990) states “the notion of translation implies deﬁnition”,
indicating that those actors that translate meaning deﬁne that meaning. Callon, Rip,
and Law (1986) state that "all translation works to solidify actor-worlds": translations
allow us to describe the mechanisms by which an actor-network is deﬁned. The actors
who are the authors of intermediaries might then be thought of as "translators" – in this
case, those who inscribe the wind farm site and layout and shape its construction.
Also essential to the following analysis are the concepts of "framing" and "overﬂowing".
Callon (1998) deﬁnes "the frame establishes a boundary within which interactions take
place more or less independently of their surrounding context." For instance, in the
calculation of wind farm’s area, we enter a data-based frame in which all entities can be
measured quantitatively. "Overﬂows" are hence instances where actors interact with their
surrounding context. In a constructivist view (like ANT), "overﬂows are the rule and
framing is a fragile, artiﬁcial result based upon substantial investments" (Callon, 1998).
==PAGE:38==
20
3 The Socio-Technical Network
Hence in the process of deﬁning a wind farm site, it is of interest to identify the frames
that are set and which actors are invested in them.
In the following sections, we trace those actors, intermediaries, and framing devices
used in the actor-networks associated with ﬂoating wind farm development in Morro
Bay, CA to open the ’black boxes’ of a wind farm’s site and layout. A common method
applied in ANT is the "follow the actor" approach (Latour, 2005)– in this chapter, the
actor in question is the not-yet-developed wind farm and is traced through evolving
networks of wind farm site and layout – at this point an intermediary, but if built, an
inﬂuential actor.
3.3
Wind Farm Site and Layout: Deﬁnitions
In this chapter, we examine the design process of a ﬂoating wind farm in its earliest
stages – describing the process of siting a WEA and identifying factors that contribute
to the choice of wind farm layout. Figure 3.2 shows a sample wind farm site, marked
with red lines, and a wind farm layout demonstrated by the wind turbines position.
Figure 3.2: Presentation of a Wind Farm Site and Layout (Nordsee One GmbH, 2021)
A wind farm site and layout are both concepts of "resource space", deﬁned as "space
that is conﬁgured to channel" those materialities of "diﬀuse and changing character,"
such as the wind (Labussière, Nadaï, and Elliott, 2018). Renewable energy transitions
literature claim that "resource space is rather a precarious achievement, ‘a temporary
stabilization at the nexus of political, economic and technical relations that is always
potentially subject to dissolution and challenge,’" (Labussière, Nadaï, and Elliott, 2018)
indicating that the site and layout of a wind farm are achieved at a point of compromise
between stakeholders that is dependent on the time at which the stabilization is achieved.
==PAGE:39==
3.3 Wind Farm Site and Layout: Deﬁnitions
21
Labussière, Nadaï, and Elliott (2018) state that "the making of a ‘space of resource’
calls for speciﬁc operations: calculating a volume, navigating within it, controlling a
material ﬂow". The choice of wind farm site is often reduced to a question of calculating
the volume of resource – a ’volume’ that is not reducible to the material calculation
of wind energy density or power output of a wind farm or the displaced resource but
also takes into account less tangible features such as social acceptance. Similarly, the
question of wind turbine siting in the form of spacing requirements is one navigation
within the space of energy resources. In the case of wind power, navigation "is a matter
of discovering the relationships between the wind turbines and other entities, such as
the landscape (visual), birds (migrations) and local populations (land uses)" (Labussière,
Nadaï, and Elliott, 2018). These coexisting or competing interests then drive the internal
and external geometries of a wind farm.
3.3.1
Wind Farm Site
The ﬁnal footprint of a wind farm is continuously designed over wind farm development
process. First, in the planning and analysis phase, WEAs that are deemed suitable
for oﬀshore wind are selected through a process that takes into account the inputs of
stakeholders including research institutions, local governments, and other industries.
Next, one or more lease areas are designated by BOEM after environmental assessment
and site assessment processes. Developers choose a lease area for which to prepare and
submit a bid. If the bid is successful, the developer then translates the lease area to a
physical wind farm footprint. The footprint a wind farm is not necessarily continuous –
for example, a wide shipping lane may divide a wind farm into two parts.
A wind farm site has "multiple existences as a social, technical, territorial, ﬁnancial,
legal, and regulatory entity" (Nadaï and Cointe, 2020). Wind farm developers conﬁgure
sites as resource spaces used to extract energy from the wind for proﬁt. The footprint
of the wind farm is entrenched in social controversy, as the position of a wind farm
is inherently tied to commercial, military, and other marine activities in the area, the
interest of local residents and industries.
3.3.2
Wind Farm Layout Design
The process of designing a wind farm layout is a process of nested compromises. The
layout of a wind farm is shaped by and holds multiple meanings for diverse stakeholders,
before and after the physical manifestation of that wind farm: "the siting of wind turbines
must be rendered compatible with ongoing uses of land, landscape, and air and with
administrative requirements" Nadaï and Cointe (2020).
In the wind farm development process, the layout is designed in two phases: in a
preliminary form in the wind farm bid, and after the lease is awarded with input from
more stakeholders as the ﬁnalized layout design. In this chapter, we focus on the latter
phase, as in this case, the layout arises from social-technical controversy.
To a wind farm developer, the layout of turbines is tied to the wind farm’s proﬁtability
through technical and ﬁnancial trade-oﬀs. In addition, turbine locations are aﬀected by
==PAGE:40==
22
3 The Socio-Technical Network
geological conditions that determine where anchors can be placed. Finally, other users of
the wind farm, namely the US Coast Guard (USCG), marine life, ﬁshing, and shipping
communities have a stake in the layout of wind turbines within a WEA. In a case study
of a wind farm built to avoid bird migration paths, Nadaï and Cointe (2020) state that
"reactivating [a wind farm] site as an energy productive entity requires an exploration of
the potential for compatibilities between migrating birds and new wind turbines". Hence,
to ’activate’ a wind farm (or, in other words, ’translate’ a wind farm to a resource space),
a variety of stakeholders must contribute to the making of the wind farm as a hybrid
entity.
3.3.2.1
Examples of current Layout Recommendations
The construction of wind farm layout is of interest in the case of the Morro Bay Call Area
largely due to the precedent set by the US Coast Guard (USCG) in New England. So
far, all wind farms in the area are laid out as a simple grid with shipping lanes oriented
north-south, and east-west. For example, a Port Access and Route Study (PARS) in
Massachusetts and Rhode Island WEA speciﬁes the following: (Unites States Coast
Guard, 2020)
• Lanes for vessel transit should be oriented in a northwest to southeast direction,
0.6 nautical miles (NM) 5 to 0.8 NM wide.
• Lanes for commercial ﬁshing vessels actively engaged in ﬁshing should be oriented
in an east to west direction, 1 NM wide.
• Lanes for USCG search and rescue operations should be oriented in a north to
south and east to west direction, 1 NM wide.
51 NM is equal to 1852 m
==PAGE:41==
3.4 Inquiry Framework and Methods
23
Figure 3.3: Sample layout of New England layout according to the current US Coast
Guard standard (Geijerstam et al., 2019).
These layout designations were the product of a compromise between wind energy
developers, the USCG, and local stakeholders (Unites States Coast Guard, 2020), (Oﬃcer
USCG, 2021).
Many stakeholders supported larger corridors (2-4 NM), yet it was
concluded that 1 NM would be suﬃcient for vessel routes and would allow for vessels to
pass without decreasing other routes within the WEA. In addition, the 1 NM spacing
allows for ﬁshing access throughout the wind farm, instead of in selected areas (Unites
States Coast Guard, 2020). It might be expected that the layout requirements on the
Morro Bay site are a result of a similar compromise between stakeholders.
3.4
Inquiry Framework and Methods
This analysis identiﬁes and maps stakeholder perspectives in the networks of wind farm
site and layout through primary source data: the data gathered in this analysis are both
derived from the oﬃcial statements that stakeholders have submitted to BOEM and
stakeholder interviews.
First, oﬃcial comments from stakeholders submitted to the BOEM and the CA Energy
Commission (CEC) upon the announcement of a Call for Nominations and Information
announcing the WEAs detail a landscape of those currently engaged in the region. At this
point, a less-than-representative mass of individuals have submitted comments – many of
those who will be impacted by the development of oﬀshore wind in CA have not voiced
their stake at this stage. Hence the network is skewed toward non-local decision-makers
who have a vested interest in the use of the OCS, whereas local citizens have not gained
signiﬁcant agency.
==PAGE:42==
24
3 The Socio-Technical Network
Simultaneously, stakeholder interviews were conducted to further explore the network.
For the interviews, an exploratory, semi-structured interview style was chosen to establish
trust and to allow for thorough follow-up questions. All interviews were conducted in
English over video calls between 30 and 60 minutes, sound-recorded, and transcribed using
an automatic transcription software. Quotes in the sections below were selected using
transcriptions and sound recording together. Full transcripts are not provided in this
thesis, as requested by those interviewed. In total sixteen interviews were conducted with
twenty interviewees from research institutions and industry to assess the case through
diﬀerent lenses. Table 3.4 presents the interviewees including a short description.

3.5
Oﬃcial Comments: Stakeholders and their
Perspectives
In October 2018 BOEM published three Call Areas for the deployment of oﬀshore wind
energy oﬀthe coast of CA, shown in Figure 2.2. On their call for Information and
Nominations, BOEM received 118 public comments and fourteen nominations from
diﬀerent developers (Thurston-Keller, 2020). The Figure 3.5 categorizes and summarizes
the received comments and describes the stakeholders’ perception of the Call Areas.
Figure 3.5: Summary of the public Comments received by BOEM (Thurston-Keller,
2020)
The organized comments are presented in a stakeholder matrix, shown in Figure 3.5,
where the gray ﬁelds demonstrate a negative perception and the yellow ﬁelds correlate
to positive impacts. The matrix provides an overview of the perception of stakeholders
towards wind energy development oﬀthe coast of CA. A detailed analysis of each
stakeholder group in the matrix is presented in Appendix B.
