• Shipping: One of the strongest opponents of the Morro Bay Call Area is the
commercial shipping industry. They state that the Call Area conﬂicts with the
USCG Marine Planning Guidelines concerning coastwise or coastal shipping routes,
navigation safety corridors, and port approaches. They propose the elimination
of the (large) portion of the Morro Bay Call Area that intersects the easternmost
lane of the Monterey Bay National Marine Sanctuary (NMS) tracks. Moreover,
if an oﬀshore wind farm will be placed, the industry requires collaboration with
the USCG, as they consider the necessary sea space for vessels to maneuver safely
and contain recommended minimum separation distances for the siting of oﬀshore
structures near shipping routes (Costanzo, 2019).
• Fishing Community: The ﬁshing community is also a strong opponent, as they
fear losing potential ﬁshing ground. However, through an engagement in an early
stage in the siting process, a concordance might be reached as they state that "we
are not opposed to the concept of oﬀshore renewable energy projects and express
desire for "inclusive and meaningful ﬁshing industry collaboration" (Johnston and
Hawkins, 2020). In addition, the Morro Bay Commercial Fishermen’s Organization
has reached a consensus with the Castle Wind proposed project, ﬁnalized in 2018,
which agreed upon an area that would have the least impact on the ﬁsheries "and
would mitigate the displacement of our ﬁshermen with this operation" (Hafer, 2019).
• Environmental NGOs: are categorized proponents, given that concerns on
the environmental impact are addressed. Moreover, the NGOs expressed unease
==PAGE:46==
28
3 The Socio-Technical Network
with the siting process, stating "Department of Defense should not be the de
facto siting agency for oﬀshore wind development in CA"(Aylesworth et al., 2019),
claiming that DOD’s role is superseding the priorities of other stakeholders. "By
engaging in private negotiations with oﬀshore wind developers to discover areas
of potential compatibility with oﬀshore wind development on the Central Coast,
BOEM, DOD, and industry become the sole parties to privileged and conﬁdential
information"(Aylesworth et al., 2019).
• Indigenous Tribes: The Chumash tribe in the area is naturally opposed to wind
energy development (Kearns&West, 2018a), which may impede the designation
of the proposed Chumash Heritage National Marine Sanctuary (CHNMS), which
encompasses both the Morro Bay and Diablo Canyon WEAs.
• Local Government: One of the strongest proponents of oﬀshore wind development
in Morro Bay is its local municipal government.
They are motivated by the
opportunity for oﬀshore wind development to spur job creation and to replace
retiring nuclear energy facilities in Diablo Canyon. Put explicitly, the City of Morro
Bay states "The success of an oﬀshore wind project within the Morro Bay WEA
is of critical importance to the City. San Luis Obispo County and the City are
in substantial need for local economic development, and we view oﬀshore wind
as a critical part of the revitalization of the Morro Bay and county economies"
(Headding, 2019).
• Local Citizens: are similarly strong proponents of the Morro Bay Call Area.
Similar to the local government, they are motivated by the opportunities for
the region.
However, they also express concerns regarding the impact of the
ﬁshing industry, on the viewshed and the environment and emphasize the need for
mitigation strategies to reduce those impacts.
• Tourism Industries: is less than enthusiastic about oﬀshore wind development in
CA. The Corporation expresses concerns about impacts to viewshed of the Hearst
Castle, as wind turbines placed in the lease area as currently planned will be visible
from the Hearst Castle, stating that "the Castle’s expansive views of the Paciﬁc
Ocean and the Coast, a panoramic landscape that Hearst entrusted to the State in
1958 and further protected in 2005, is one of its major draws" (O’Toole, 2020).
3.6
Interview Findings: Wind Farm Siting
First, the ﬁndings from the Call for Information and Nominations and stakeholder
interviews are used to construct the network surrounding the siting process of an oﬀshore
wind farm in CA. The wind farm site is an intermediary in the network, constructed by a
network of actors and passed to the developer in the form of a lease area. Also illustrated
in this network is the eventual physical manifestation of the siting process: the wind
farm footprint – which is an actor in a future actor-network surrounding the wind farm.
==PAGE:47==
3.6 Interview Findings: Wind Farm Siting
29
Figure 3.6: ANT Map - Wind Farm Site
In the following sections, each actor in this network is discussed by using the informa-
tion of those interviewed to illustrate its position, perspective, and inﬂuence. It should
be noted that the following section presents interview data largely at face value, where a
more thorough ANT analysis will be detailed in Section 3.8 below.
3.6.1
Actors in the Siting Network
Those interviewed were asked to highlight and oﬀer insights on key stakeholders currently
present in siting discussions in CA.
==PAGE:48==
30
3 The Socio-Technical Network
3.6.1.1
BOEM’s Siting Process
First, those interviewed were asked to comment on the current status of oﬀshore wind
in CA, oﬀering perspective on BOEM’s process. A Member of the BOEM Task Force
(2021) discussed the intergovernmental Task Force, headed by BOEM and the California
Energy Commission (CEC) is comprised of "federal, state, local, and tribal governmental
representatives" and is limited by the Federal Advisory Committee that does not allow
meetings with industry "in a closed setting", notably including the ﬁshing industry. This
Task Force can be viewed in Figure 3.6 as those actors interact with BOEM within the
circle that deﬁnes the wind farm site.
The Member of the BOEM Task Force (2021) presents the upcoming process that
precedes the designation of a WEA: "once we identify the Wind Energy Area we would
conduct an environmental review under the National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA).
We also have to comply with the Coastal Zone Management Act and the National Historic
Preservation Act. Then we would publish a proposed sale notice in the Federal Register."
Researchers interviewed comment upon the evolving nature of the network around
wind farm site "Morro Bay is not going to stay where it is, it’s not going to stay the same
size" (NREL Researcher 1, 2021), and state that "It’ll probably be about two or three
lease areas. And then the lease areas, get auctioned oﬀin a regular auction" (NREL
Researcher 1, 2021).
3.6.1.2
Department of Defense
In the siting process for Morro Bay, no stakeholder looms as large as the US Department
of Defense, whose oﬀshore presence on the West Coast comes into conﬂict with the
proposed areas, "a lot of training and testing that goes on oﬀshore CA" (Member of
the BOEM Task Force, 2021). In addition, the DOD has pressures due to increases
in trade (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021). These assertions are contested by
developers and researchers, who comment "less than 4% of the DOD Navy training areas
are most directly aﬀected by Morro Bay" (Developer 3 (Castle Wind), 2021). A particular
point of interest is the "Vandenberg Air Force Base, which is a spaceport" resulting in
"limited commercial space for access points" (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021).
The exact location of these activities is not always transparent – "because they’re the
military they classify everything" (Researcher (University of Delaware), 2021). This lack
of transparency has resulted in a stagnated siting process – "if [the DOD] say that they
may want to use it in the future is enough to hold up the process" (Consultant (DNV),
2021).
Other stakeholders, including environmental groups and the ﬁshing community, have
expressed their sentiment that the DOD is being unfairly prioritized by the Oﬀshore
Wind Task Force. This comment was responded to with "is ﬁshing more important
or less important than training and testing by the Department of Defense? Certainly
not to a ﬁsherman, but it could be more important depending on what they’re doing
to the United States, from, from a federal perspective" (Member of the BOEM Task
Force, 2021). This sentiment is echoed across research and development spheres, which
==PAGE:49==
3.6 Interview Findings: Wind Farm Siting
31
comments including "the Department of Defense is a powerful stakeholder and has a lot
more legitimacy, let’s say, then the commercial ﬁshermen, because the Navy has legal
precedent, not to mention national security" (Researcher (University of Delaware), 2021).
Some interviewed highlight that the DOD is engaged chieﬂy in the planning phase,
and not necessarily past the announcement of a wind energy area (PNNL Researcher,
2021). A researcher comments "the military has the most power but they’re probably not
the most aﬀected" (NREL Researcher 1, 2021). DOD is projected to remain a stakeholder
in the continued planning of new oﬀshore wind areas – researchers state "their larger
concern is cumulative impacts, so maybe for the ﬁrst few projects it isn’t as much of an
issue but as we see a greater build out this becomes a real issue for them where they
don’t have opportunities to move around" (NREL Researcher 1, 2021).
3.6.1.3
National Marine Sanctuaries
In addition to the DOD, NMS and the indigenous tribes associated with them have been
identiﬁed by those interviewed as stakeholders that pose the most immediate risk to the
emergence of oﬀshore wind in the Morro Bay Call Area. First, the existing Monterey Bay
National Marine Sanctuary (MBNMS) currently forms the eastern barrier of the Call
Area. This is seen by developers as an "unmovable barrier" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind),
2021). It is observed that the DOD proposed areas (Figure B.2b) overlap the existing
sanctuary, indicating that within the federal government barriers might be less rigid.
Those hoping to develop oﬀshore wind are also wary of the currently proposed CHNMS,
which would encompass the entirety of the Morro Bay Call Area. As the proposed
sanctuary is connected to an indigenous tribe, researchers indicate the importance of
considering the use and value of space "indigenous communities that might view a
particular area as something that needs to be protected forever and doesn’t see, for
example, a wind development project as an appropriate use for that space" (LBNL
Researcher 2, 2021).
A developer discusses the internal politics of the proposed sanctuary: "it’s a northern
Chumash tribe – a tribe of 30 people. But then there is a diﬀerent Chumash tribe and
one doesn’t recognize the other" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021), indicating that
the organizing force behind the proposed sanctuary may not have the full resources to
manage an oﬀshore wind project in its waters.
In addition to indigenous groups, there are "environmentalists that actually would
like to see the sanctuary" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021), adding dimension to the
network.
3.6.1.4
Marine Environment
There are environmental groups (in the Task Force, as seen in Figure 3.6 as ’Environ-
mental NGOs’), which have both vested interest in preserving the marine habitats and
environment, as well as in the development of clean energy. Some impacts are known –
for example, "as we get closer to shore we run into ... higher concentrations of whale
migratory corridors" (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021). An oﬀshore wind activist
==PAGE:50==
32
3 The Socio-Technical Network
cites bird migration as a potential research area (Wind Energy Advocate, 2021). At this
point in the siting process, all those interviewed stressed the need for further studies and
analysis on environmental impacts.
Some developers cite the issue of the lease timing "it’s a bit of a conundrum because
until we have at least we don’t have the funding to really do exhaustive California Envi-
ronmental Quality Act (CEQA) and NEPA analysis but the environmental community is
concerned about unknowns and data gaps. But you can’t ﬁll the data gaps and complete
the analysis until you have a lease and lease is in a speciﬁc area" (Developer 2 (Castle
Wind), 2021).
3.6.1.5
Fishing Industry
The ﬁshing industry, one of the most vocal groups in the Call for Information and
Nominations, was highly discussed groups in interviews. A Member of the BOEM Task
Force (2021) comments that the ﬁshing industry, although present in public forums, has
been absent from much of the discussion due to the Federal Advisory Committee Act, and
as noted above, takes a back seat to discussions with the Department of Defense. Yet, the
Task Force member notes "We want to avoid high concentration ﬁshing areas high-value
ﬁshing areas closer to shore" (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021), demonstrating
the secondary nature of the ﬁshing industry’s involvement in the siting network.
Researchers, developers, and federal agencies alike comment that identifying which
grounds are used for ﬁshing is not a straightforward task. For example, "one of the
hardest things that we have to collect data on is the recreational users, the people that
do not have transponders on board their boats so it’s a lot harder to pinpoint who
actually uses this waterway" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021). Some researchers comment upon
the ways that the ﬁshing industry already feels highly squeezed, that they’re concerned
with losing more access, even if it’s an area they may not use a lot" (PNNL Researcher,
2021). This is assumed to be driven by the feeling that "on the coast of CA, there are
already a number of marine protected areas that are now oﬀ-limits for commercial ﬁshing
activity of any kind" (LBNL Researcher, 2021), leading to ﬁshermen to see oﬀshore wind
development as "impinging on their livelihood" (LBNL Researcher, 2021).
Researchers thus propose that "we’d want them really involved in the process of
determining which areas are more or less appropriate" (PNNL Researcher, 2021). A
developer of an unsolicited lease has already begun an iterative process to determine
which areas would be most compatible with ﬁshing interests. The developer reached a
"revenue sharing agreement" with ﬁshermen where "after the wind farm is operational, it
would essentially tax itself above what we’re paying the state and federal governments
to compensate the commercial ﬁsherman" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021). This
agreement was shown to have garnered the support of the ﬁshing community, as expressed
in Appendix B.
==PAGE:51==
3.6 Interview Findings: Wind Farm Siting
33
3.6.1.6
Shipping
The shipping industry, like the ﬁshing industry, has also been absent from the Task
Force but has been vocal in the discussion of the wind farm site. A Coast Guard oﬃcer
comments "Diablo and Morro Bay had the biggest challenges for the shipping industries,
they’re the ones that actually made direct comments asking for a PARS to be completed.
And we were already engaging in that discussion" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021), indicating that
these actors exert agency on the Task Force’s proceedings.
The USCG oﬃcer comments upon a recent shift in shipping activity in the last decade
that impacts the shipping community’s relationship with oﬀshore wind development,
"California changed their sulfur regulations to go out to 24 nautical miles in 2012. And
so now the majority [of ships] are going right through the center of BOEMs Call Areas."
(Oﬃcer USCG, 2021). The current shipping route can be viewed in Figure B.3.
The USCG oﬃcer comments that as a lesson learned from the East Coast where
"BOEM was already leasing out blocks at a time when they wanted to establish fairways"
(Oﬃcer USCG, 2021), leading to extensive negotiation about the use of space. He calls
for the designation of a fairway in California, claiming that this might de-conﬂict the
process of siting oﬀshore wind through avoiding controversy with the shipping industry
(Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
3.6.1.7
Local Need for Oﬀshore Wind in California
Developers argue the local need for oﬀshore wind in the Morro Bay area is driven by
the local economy: "from a stakeholder perspective, the central coast of California is
economically depressed needs to replace Diablo Canyon, both in terms of the power it
generates but also jobs and economic activity" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021). This
statement references the perspective of local citizens, who may not yet be active in the
siting network, but will be impacted by wind energy development.
In addition, those interviewed reference wildﬁres, that in recent years have caused
bottlenecks in onshore transmission, as oﬀshore transmission as a viable safeguard to
major outages (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021) 6.
3.6.1.8
Visual Impact
In the cases of onshore wind and ﬁxed-bottom oﬀshore wind, the visual impact is often a
key stakeholder issue with residents that live near a wind farm. Across those interviewed,
all agree that visual impact is less of a concern in CA. For example, a developer states
"once you get beyond 25 miles to be on the central coast where there’s a marine layer,
80% of the time, you won’t even see the turbines. On a clear day when the sun is on
the east, you might see small little toothpicks on the coast" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind),
2021).
6In 2020 four million acres burned in CA due to wildﬁres caused by transmission lines (LA Times,
2021), resulting in blackouts. Dale, Carnall, and Wei (2018) studied the impact of wildﬁres on the CA
electricity grid. They conclude, that the risks for wildﬁres are the highest in the regions in southern CA
around LA and San Diego.
==PAGE:52==
34
3 The Socio-Technical Network
BOEM has responded to concerns about the visual impact and prepared photomon-
tages to demonstrate the impact for diﬀerent distances to shore. The simulations are
performed from two diﬀerent onshore locations and for 15, 17, and 20 miles distance to
shore of the turbines for both daytime and nighttime. From the photomontages (Ap-
pendix C) it can be observed that the visual impact is small to nonexistent, depending
on weather conditions.
Although in the current Call Area there is little concern about visual impact, a
developer expresses that conﬂict may arise with the proposed DOD areas: "the DOD
proposal to move closer to shore did get some negative feedback from stakeholders and
oﬃcials" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021).
Even though the turbines might in a technical sense be less disturbing, from a local
stakeholder’s perspective, the further distance to shore might cause developers to de-
legitimize local opposition: "with ﬂoating, it’s even more challenging because you don’t
really need to deal with the local community. There isn’t even as much shore-side activity
as the developers – you can’t claim you’re going to see them and you’re going to be
bothered by them" (Researcher (University of Delaware), 2021). This may further mute
local citizens’ voices in the siting network, who are often "not involved until there’s a
problem or you have to do a cable connection" (Researcher (University of Delaware),
2021).
3.6.2
Research and Development: Technical Challenges
A developer’s stake in participating in the siting process is coupled with the proﬁtability of
the resource space (this will be explored in future chapters). This perspective necessitates
that a developer, an actor with techno-economic priorities, focus on the technical barriers
to siting a ﬂoating wind farm in Morro Bay. Those interviewed were asked to speak
to the unique research and development challenges that they anticipate in the siting of
oﬀshore wind oﬀthe West Coast of CA. Many of their responses can be grouped into
four categories: those related to the need for ﬂoating wind technology, hurdles related to
transmission, supply chain, and ﬁnally procurement of oﬀshore wind.
The challenges reﬂect the priorities of wind farm developers and researchers to the
largest extend, two groups depicted in Figure 3.6.
3.6.2.1
Floating Technology
Some of those interviewed cite "technological uncertainty" as "the biggest challenge with
ﬂoating oﬀshore wind", clarifying "it is the perception of technological uncertainty. But I
would also say that developers can’t describe exactly what the sub-structure is going to
look like" (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021).
There is a particular lack of knowledge about dynamic cabling and mooring lines – a
BOEM Task Force member states "I’ve heard the term spaghetti7 so many times", as
well as concerns that turbines might "ﬂoat away" (Member of the BOEM Task Force,
2021). Others engaged in the oﬀshore space cite a need for education of the public about
7In this context the word spaghetti is used to describe the mooring lines and cables.
==PAGE:53==
3.6 Interview Findings: Wind Farm Siting
35
ﬂoating technology, claiming "this concept is not very well socialized yet in the minds of
the public or even certain energy policy leaders or legislators" (Wind Energy Advocate,
2021). Yet to those who have been engaged in the oﬀshore space, "the technology for
ﬂoating platforms is not something new" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021), indicating that there
may be some available knowledge transfer from past oil and gas projects.
In addition, the deployment of ﬂoating structures poses a limitation on the maximum
depth that can be considered for a Call Area. Developers comment that one "immovable
boundary" is Diablo Canyon (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021), where the bathymetry
drops below 1300 m. A previous iteration of the Morro Bay Call Area assumed a maximum
depth of 1000 m (Figure B.1) and has been altered as technology has improved (NREL
Researcher 1, 2021). The depth of the Call Area also inﬂuences substructure choice.
For instance, a developer cites concerns with tension leg platforms (detailed Chapter 5),
including the technological risks associated with the resonance frequency of the tensioned
mooring lines as well as the processing of tensioning in such deep waters (Developer 2
(Castle Wind), 2021). This led this developer to choose a semi-submersible structure,
which has a median mooring footprint when comparing to Tension Leg Platform (TLP)
and spar technologies.
3.6.2.2
Transmission
The roll-out of oﬀshore wind oﬀthe West Coast is necessarily tied to the development of
transmission to accommodate it. In the case of Morro Bay, the question of transmission
is inherently tied to the retiring Diablo Canyon power plant to the south of Morro Bay, as
it is "the last remaining nuclear plant in Californiaand the state is committed to retiring
it in 2025. We’d like to be able to reserve that transmission for use for oﬀshore wind"
(Wind Energy Advocate, 2021).
Those interviewed indicate that transmission presents "a bit of a chicken and egg
situation, you need the transmission to make oﬀshore wind work," meanwhile, "you
need the agency to say we want oﬀshore wind for this to come to pass" (Wind Energy
Advocate, 2021). Transmission is a particularly complex question "because of the scale,
oﬀshore wind requires CAISO because we need their support to pay for the transmission"
(NREL Researcher 1, 2021). There is potential for oﬀshore wind on the Central Coast to
send power to Los Angeles County via subsea cable, which would require considerable
investment from transmission system operators (NREL Researcher 1, 2021).
3.6.2.3
Port Infrastructure
In interviews, the availability of ports for construction and operations and maintenance
(O&M) consistently arose as an area of signiﬁcant uncertainty in the oﬀshore wind
planning process. An oﬀshore wind energy advocate paints the picture:
"First of all, California doesn’t have that many natural ports, to begin with.
Second of all, some of the bigger ones are primarily used for in and out
delivery of container ships and they’re constantly in use, and there aren’t
==PAGE:54==
36
3 The Socio-Technical Network
big areas adjacent to them that are unspoken for." (Wind Energy Advocate,
2021)
Consultants and researchers also bring attention to port infrastructure. Some re-
searchers state: "port infrastructure needs to be signiﬁcantly upgraded in order to host
an oﬀshore wind industry that could scale to the extent that that might be required
for there to be signiﬁcant industry interest" (LBNL Researcher, 2021). Others have
suggested that new ports must be built altogether (NREL Researcher 1, 2021).
Developers tie the development of nearby ports as key to cost-eﬀectiveness: "in
economic analysis on the West Coast, one of the biggest cost area was O&M because the
sea conditions are so challenging on the West Coast" (Consultant (DNV), 2021).
Once ports are built they impact other actors engaged in the OCS. A US Coast Guard
oﬃcer comments, "the port infrastructure is going to impact California tremendously, as
shipping for that port is going to massively increase and navigation will need to improve"
(Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
3.6.3
Reﬂections on the Siting Process
Those interviewed were ﬁnally asked to reﬂect on the siting process for oﬀshore wind
in CA. Researchers encourage BOEM and CEC to conduct "a planning process that
ultimately allows individuals to have an input and ultimately have a say in the outcome of
a project" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021), and facilitate "a transparent dialogue to mitigate
their concerns," (PNNL Researcher, 2021) with the urgency that "one has to really engage
those communities and very early on" (LBNL Researcher, 2021).
The necessity for stakeholder engagement was expressed by many of the parties
interviewed. A developer expresses stakeholder engagement as a means of making a
site less risk-prone, claiming "the more you can de-conﬂict a project by reaching out to
stakeholders building consensus getting support, the easier it will be to ultimately permit
a project." (Developer 3 (Castle Wind), 2021).
Other researchers comment on developers motivation for stakeholder engagement,
illustrating that unlike in Europe, "litigation is a really big issue here. And that’s the fear
of both the federal agencies as well as the certain key stakeholders, like the developers,
they don’t want to be sued" (Researcher (University of Delaware), 2021).
From a regulatory standpoint, the Coast Guard has also expressed commitment
to stakeholder engagement processes, stating "We need to listen to the ﬁsheries, the
recreational waterway users the deep draft vessels when it comes to containers, oil, other
developers that may be looking at waterway use" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
Some researchers have been struck by "how much more sophisticated and engaged
the state was on energy issues" (PNNL Researcher, 2021), demonstrating that BOEM
and the CEC are moving more aggressively towards a lease rather than just gathering
information – actively engaging in the process of forming a wind farm site. One researcher
recommends that "the state could mandate certain requirements and especially now with
environmental justice becoming much more common part of these processes, I could
think that the onshoring of the cable, and where that occurs and what community that
==PAGE:55==
3.7 Findings: Wind Farm Layout Design
37
occurs in, and how that community beneﬁts directly from that project could be part of
the process" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021).
3.7
Findings: Wind Farm Layout Design
Next, those interviewed were asked to comment on how the introduction of ﬂoating
technology might shape the design of a future commercial-scale wind farm layout. This
line of interview questions is speculative, as at this point in time "it’s more about the
marine spatial plan rather than the layout of how the turbines are going to be placed
way down the road" (NREL Researcher 3, 2021). This is echoed by the developer of
Castle Wind, who stated "we do have a turbine layout and we’ve thought about points
of interconnection and transmission capacity. But the most of our eﬀort over the last
three or four years has been trying to push the Navy, and then trying to get political
leadership in Sacramento" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021).
The following network surrounding the wind farm layout design is illustrated to
highlight the actors inﬂuencing the wind farm layout design. The following section
presents interview data at face-value, where a more thorough ANT analysis will be
detailed in Section 3.8 below.
==PAGE:56==
38
3 The Socio-Technical Network
Figure 3.7: ANT Map - Wind Farm Layout
3.7.1
US Coast Guard: Regulatory Actor
The US Coast Guard the central actor responsible for coordinating the navigational
guidelines for the wind farm, which directly informs the layout design process. A member
of the Task Force states "[BOEM] are not navigational safety experts - - we rely on our
federal partner of the United States Coast Guard to determine what would be safest for
vessels" (Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021). Yet the timing of this activity is in the
future: the Coast Guard states that it is a challenge that "we really don’t really dive in
heavily until there’s actually an applicant awarded a lease" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021). Hence
in the following section, the US Coast Guard will be explored as an actor, alongside
those with a stake in the layout of a wind farm.
==PAGE:57==
3.7 Findings: Wind Farm Layout Design
39
To examine the US Coast Guard as an actor, it is of interest to explore past decisions
made by the Coast Guard with regards to wind farm layout. For the ﬁrst wind farms, "the
spacing protocols was more what the applicant provided to the Coast Guard – the Coast
Guard didn’t directly say what the spacing was going to be" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021). This
statement echoes the PARS for Rhode Island and Massachusetts, which details the grid
spacing as a regulatory compromise between the developer, shipping, and ﬁshing interests
(Unites States Coast Guard, 2020). Finally, a representative of the Coast Guard indicates
a desire for consistency, as "the Coast Guard wants to maintain standard navigation
protocols on the East Coast and West Coast" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
3.7.2
Wind Energy Developers and Researchers
The compromise met on the East Coast came with signiﬁcant concessions from wind
energy developers, who seek to maximize the energy produced by the wind farm. One
researcher states, plainly, "there’s the uniform spacing between turbines in New England
– You can’t optimize anything if we’re stuck at a uniform spacing" (Consultant (DNV),
2021).
In the case of the West Coast, both the Coast Guard and developers seem to agree
that the grid pattern also caters to the wind direction, as the Coast Guard oﬃcer states
"grid pattern isn’t just for navigation concerns, it’s mostly for the wind direction" (Oﬃcer
USCG, 2021), and the developer references the "unidirectional wind" as motivation for a
regular layout (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021).
In addition, the layout is inﬂuenced by wake eﬀects – a developer states "if you put
them closer together, then you’ll have shadow eﬀects, and the shadow eﬀects reduce
your yield" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021). In an NREL study, researchers place
turbines at a certain spacing for conservative estimations including wake eﬀects (NREL
Researcher 1, 2021).
3.7.3
Cohabitation of Wind Farms
As the wind farm layout is a subject of compromise, it is of interest to examine which
stakeholders interact with and thereby seek to inﬂuence the layout of a ﬂoating oﬀshore
wind farm. At the moment, Coast Guard does not have the authority to name an oﬀshore
wind farm an "exclusion zone":
"Coast Guard regulations for restricting traﬃc only goes out to 12 nautical
miles. Congress would have to modify the regulation to push our authority
outside of 12 to make an exclusion zone" Oﬃcer USCG, 2021.
If an exclusion zone is not named, it is expected that some stakeholders may have an
interest in the cohabitation of the space occupied by the wind farm. The Coast Guard
has similar questions: "is that the right avenue to make an exclusion zone [so that] only
the ﬂoating oﬀshore wind turbine industry has a right to that water, or should that water
still have other uses?" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
==PAGE:58==
40
3 The Socio-Technical Network
3.7.3.1
Search and Rescue
The US Coast Guard plays two roles in the network: as a regulator at the center of the
network and as an actor with a vested interest in cohabitation. The chief concern of the
US Coast Guard in setting wind farm layout requirements is Search and Rescue (SAR).
This is justiﬁed by previous experience that "on the East Coast, the grid pattern is more
utilized, speciﬁcally for search and rescue capabilities for a search platform to be able to
do its pattern in and out and because we have mandated certain requirements that we
saturate an area" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
The proposed Call Area is signiﬁcantly further from shore than those on the East
Coast, which exposes some gaps in SAR regulation "BOEM’s going to require them to
have lights per FAA standards, but that’s not required because FAA doesn’t even go
up to 12 nautical miles" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021). Thereby, there is the need to "develop
a search and rescue protocol agreement" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021), also citing that the
USCG will also have to develop SAR protocols for workers on the ﬂoating platforms and
turbines.
Others interviewed are skeptical of the need for a grid pattern for SAR, citing wind
farms with irregular layouts in Europe as exceptions to this rule (Consultant (DNV),
2021).
3.7.3.2
Fishing Industry
A notable cohabitant of oﬀshore wind farms on the East Coast of the US is the ﬁshing
community. Hence, a substructure manufacturer highlights that "coexistence is essential
and is a big part of the agenda at the ﬁsheries in the ﬁshing community" (Developer 1
(PPL), 2021). The presence of mooring lines and dynamic cables complicate the issue of
ﬁshing within a wind farm.
Some researchers and developers insist that ﬁshermen will not be able to perform
some actions within the wind farm: "no trawling no netting no long line" (Developer 2
(Castle Wind), 2021). Although they state that these actions might be prohibited, "the
ﬁshing vessels would be able to travel through the wind farm from point A to point B
and get to their ﬁshing grounds" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021). They do not see an
issue with transit through wind farms, as "the updraft [of a ﬁshing vessel] is normally
10 meters so you’re not going to get to the mooring or cables because they started 20
meters and below" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021).
As a result, it might be anticipated that "ﬁshermen are one stakeholder group
that we’re seeing a lot more against ﬂoating wind than bottom ﬁxed" (ORE Catapult
Researcher, 2021). This sentiment is not shared across the industry – a USCG oﬃcer
states "I do not think the mooring lines have been such an issue with commercial ﬁshing
industry directly" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
Those interviewed demonstrate conﬂicting opinions as to whether the ﬁnishing com-
munity will want to cohabit the wind farm space. One researcher comments, "I don’t
think that ﬁshermen generally go within oﬀshore wind farms that much today, but I think
there’s always the option if that’s where the ﬁsh move to then that’s where the ﬁshermen
==PAGE:59==
3.7 Findings: Wind Farm Layout Design
41
could move to" (ORE Catapult Researcher, 2021). The USCG representative believes
that the introduction of ﬂoating structures will "bring in diﬀerent ﬁsh – in the Gulf of
Mexico the platforms wherever they’re established have a new biological environment
that’s established around them. And so these wind turbines are going to have the same
eﬀect" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
One researcher interviewed viewed the development of oﬀshore wind as an oppor-
tunity for the ﬁshing community to develop new means of catching ﬁsh that do not
require traditional trawling techniques "they need to design parallel technology" (NREL
Researcher 2, 2021).
3.7.3.3
Shipping
The USCG is also responsible for shipping vessels passing through the region. On the
East Coast, larger shipping vessels are permitted to pass through the wind farm. From a
developer’s perspective, "large shipping traﬃc would have to be rerouted" as they "don’t
see an easy way to accommodate large vessels" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021). The
USCG oﬃcer agrees that some vessels will not be able to pass through, but includes the
caveat that "as long as we keep on charted as the mariners aware, that might reduce the
risk for navigation" (Oﬃcer USCG, 2021).
3.7.3.4
Marine Environment
It is of interest to many environmental groups to learn how marine life will interact with
a ﬂoating wind farm environment. Many of those interviewed cited the low probability of
primary entanglement as mooring lines and dynamic cables have limited movement and
are under considerable tension. Yet in cases of secondary entanglement, marine species
become ensnared in loose ﬁshing gear or other marine debris that is entangled with a
mooring line (NREL Researcher 3, 2021).
In terms of layout regulation, the USCG comments on the potential for collaboration
with environmental advocates to use wind farm spacing to avoid whale strikes (Oﬃcer
USCG, 2021).
3.7.4
Reﬂections on the Layout Design Process
The layout design of a wind farm is inherently tied to its siting and the size of the Call
Areas – from a technological perspective, the positions of the turbines are limited to
move within the Call Area perimeter. A developer states that in Morro Bay, "we need
about 120 square miles per project. The turbines won’t necessarily ﬁll all of that space
but you need to have space for siting as they need to do some geotechnical surveys and
biological surveys.
A researcher comments on how the wind farm layout design might change over time.
He predicts "I think there’s maybe a short term tendency to want to put more densely
packed projects in because there’s a shortage of places. If sites become more available,
then I think that spacing may be worth the pressure to space service closer will be more
relieved" (NREL Researcher 1, 2021).
==PAGE:60==
42
3 The Socio-Technical Network
3.8
Discussion
Through the exploration of stakeholder perspectives, we can illustrate and examine the
stakeholder networks surrounding the siting and layout design of a wind farm in Morro
Bay, CA. Through these networks, we employ ANT to analyze the collaborative process
of designing a wind farm.
In analysis, we characterize the multidisciplinary emergence of a wind farm design –
Nadaï and Cointe (2020) characterize this collaborative work as "recomposing heteroge-
neous entities and values together so that they advance and contribute to a shared end".
The notion of heterogeneity highlights that human and non-human actors deﬁne the
network – in addition to the human actors detailed above, non-human actors, including
wind farm siting software, cost calculations, and regulations shape the wind farm’s
emergence.
In this chapter, the siting and layout design phases are treated as separate, yet the
two are inextricably linked – the site of the wind farm both predetermines the physical
perimeter for a wind farm layout and shapes the network for who has a stake in wind farm
layout design. Thereby, we will revisit the two diagrams illustrating the actor-networks
involved in siting and layout design, with the understanding that the second network is
necessarily an evolution of the ﬁrst.
3.8.1
Wind Farm Siting
Now, we revisit the constructed actor-network to examine its dynamics. The multidisci-
plinary, often conﬂicting perspectives presented in this chapter form the basis for the
construction of a wind farm site.
3.8.1.1
Mapping the Network of Wind Farm Site
Figure 3.8 below demonstrates key actors in the wind farm site network, highlighting their
relationships to one another. It is noted that not all actors are included in this simpliﬁed
representation – non-human actors, including measurement and calculation devices for
wind resource and environmental conditions, inﬂuence and frame the communication
between actors. Actors and intermediaries in the network are connected using arrows,
indicating the direction of information. Dashed arrows signify communication that is
expected to occur in future stages of wind energy development.
==PAGE:61==
3.8 Discussion
43
Figure 3.8: ANT Map - Wind Farm Site
Figure 3.8 above demonstrates the relative centrality of actors who contribute to the
construction of a wind farm site. It becomes apparent that the center of the network is
BOEM and the CEC – those with the responsibility for choosing the sites for potential
WEAs. BOEM and the CEC are hence the translators of actor-network of wind farm site:
they receive and process stakeholder perspectives and generate the WEA, and thereby
deﬁning the network.
From interview data, it can be observed that there are two macro-scale groupings
of stakeholders: those that are on the Oﬀshore Wind Task Force and those that are
==PAGE:62==
44
3 The Socio-Technical Network
not. As stated earlier, "industries," including ﬁshing, shipping, and local tourism are
not included in the inner circle of the decision-making process. Notably, researchers, the
DOD, and NMS are instrumental members of the Task Force, whose concerns have been
ampliﬁed. Design literature oﬀers some criticism to this approach, cautioning that "certain
technological problems may not be decomposable, and hence require not information
hiding, but richly connected contributions across participating actors" (Tharchen, Garud,
and Henn, 2020), reinforcing claims of exclusion by stakeholders outside the Task Force.
In Figure 3.8, it is necessary to diﬀerentiate between those actors engaged with the
WEA and the eventual wind farm footprint. The WEA is an intermediary, inscribed on
maps and enforced through legislation, conveying where a wind farm may legally be sited.
The inscription of a WEA directly impacts the DOD, which must relocate their military
operations in preparation for the area to be used for wind energy purposes. Developers
interested in submitting bids to a future auction are also beholden to the WEA, which
informs the design of a proposed wind farm. Indigenous groups with a vested interest in
the proposed CHNMS are also directly impacted by the WEA, which may change the
area able to be occupied by this proposal.
The lease area, once awarded, is translated by the wind farm developer to become
a wind farm footprint (an actor) through processes of consultation, design, calculation,
and material construction. It is only after a wind farm is materialized that it exerts
agency and other stakeholders are impacted – for example, shipping and trawling vessels
will have to avoid the wind lease once activity commences. These physical activities
also impact the marine environment, and thereby those concerned with environmental
preservation including NGOs and Tribal organizations. In addition, it is not until the
construction phase commences that onshore stakeholders, including those connected to
ports, landfall, or points of connection as well as those who might have a view of the
project might be impacted.
3.8.1.2
Technical Framing of the Siting Process
BOEM’s timeline (Figure 2.1) presents the process of developing a wind farm as linear
describing the steps to achieve the inevitable emergence of a wind farm. This narrative
is teleological and techno-centric, beginning with research and development and ending
in the construction of a wind farm. In this narrative, researchers at national labs identify
areas of interest based on resource availability and available technology, including metrics
such as the maximum allowable water depth and limits on extreme weather conditions,
using developed calculation tools and practices. This initial step deﬁnes the resource
space in technical terms and sets the frame for the subsequent construction of the WEA.
Within a technical frame, a stakeholder’s inﬂuence in the network is thereby tied to
its presence in maps and data. The previous Chapter 2 presents such data, collected
using masts, buoys, radar, and satellites and presented numerically and visually as maps
and graphs available in the Gateway (Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, California
Public Utilities Commission, and California Energy Commission, 2021). This frame has
been used to bolster some stakeholders: the original Morro Bay Call Area (pre-2018)
covered less area than the 2018 Call Area as it assumed a smaller maximum water depth
==PAGE:63==
3.8 Discussion
45
in which ﬂoating technology might be deployed, and maintained a boundary with the
MBNMS (Figure B.1), which is inscribed on federal maps. Yet interviews show that
certain groups, namely the ﬁshing industry and other recreational users of the OCS often
are ambiguous with the location of their activities due to the lack of radar on smaller
boats. Hence these users are often discounted in the initial siting process. Furthermore,
stakeholders present in the siting process that are competing with the technically ’best’
scenario, contribute to a negative lens towards those who seek to limit the wind energy
yield by restricting the wind farm footprint.
When the wind farm is installed, its interactions with local stakeholders cause this
technical frame to overﬂow, necessitating engagement with the socio-technical network.
As apparent in interviews and comments, there has already been some dialogue between
a potential developer, Castle Wind, and local stakeholders in the Morro Bay Call Area.
This early-stage engagement allows for increased visibility of those stakeholders in the
comment process, as there is evident knowledge sharing between these groups in the
Call for Information and Nomination comments. This process of pre-engagement with
stakeholders traditionally left out of the early planning stages adds depth and complexity
to the siting process, as the site (an intermediary), is linked to its eventual physical
manifestation as an actor.
3.8.1.3
Feedback Loops and Paradoxes in the Network
In Figure 3.8 above we can identify feedback loops early in the design process. One
example is the DOD, who are directly engaged with the Task Force and must shift their
operation once the WEA is designated. Hence groups directly impacted by the WEA
may be motivated by immediacy to participate in the planning process.
In addition, stakeholder interviews expose multiple paradoxes in BOEM’s site desig-
nation process in CA. One such paradox is environmental analysis – it is only after a
lease is sold that thorough environmental analysis is conducted. This situation prevents
environmental NGOs from forming concrete stances on oﬀshore wind. Another notable
paradox is the timing of the USCG’s involvement after the lease is awarded. Late engage-
ment prevents the designation of a fairway that might avoid possible WEAs. A fairway,
if created, would be included in the data used to determine the wind farm sites, thereby
amplifying the voice of the shipping industry in the siting process. Finally, questions of
port, transmission, and supply chain availability are all those that may determine project
feasibility for developers and inevitably impact the turnout of the lease are auctions.
3.8.1.4
2021 Morro Bay Call Area
On May 25, 2021, the ﬁnalized Morro Bay Call Area was advanced for consideration
as a WEA. The amended area is signiﬁcantly larger than the one used in this thesis.
Notable amendments include the addition of a south-eastern expansion (similar to the
proposed DOD South site), the exclusion of a north-eastern portion, and a signiﬁcant
expansion to the west of the Call Area towards the edge of Diablo Canyon. From the
analysis presented above the following might be surmised of these decisions.
==PAGE:64==
46
3 The Socio-Technical Network
• South-east expansion: this decision may be motivated by discussions with the DOD,
who identiﬁed that this region is acceptable for their purposes. In addition, this
addition allows for a shorter export cable route to landfall to the southeast of the
Call Area. This expansion may cause some pushback from local tourism industries
including Hearst Castle, which has the clearest view of this portion of the wind
farm.
• Western expansion: this decision is likely technically motivated, as the western
limit of the 2018 Call Area was determined by maximum depth allowed by ﬂoating
wind energy technology. The inclusion of this zone indicates that DOD has made
concessions to relocate some oﬀshore operations. One signiﬁcant consequence of
this action will be the further diversion of shipping lanes to the west of the eventual
WEA.
• Northern exclusion: the exclusion of this area likely is due to compromise with
the DOD for ﬂight paths to the Vanderberg Air Force Base, demonstrating a
collaborative solution.

3.8.2
From Wind Farm Site to Layout
The multidisciplinary process of determining a wind farm site begins in a techno-economic
frame to characterize a wind resource space. Next, the proposed area then is opened to
the social sphere up for comments and dialogue to collaboratively design a wind energy
==PAGE:65==
3.8 Discussion
47
site, which is oﬃcially designated as WEAs. The wind farm site is then translated to the
technical space through intermediaries such as maps and data basins, which then will be
used to design a wind farm.
The wind farm site is passed to the network that deﬁnes the wind farm layout network
(Figure 3.10 below) as an object that partially deﬁnes the resource space – wind turbines
may only be arranged within the physical space set by the wind farm lease area. Along
with the physical nature of the lease area, its social and economic complexities are also
passed to the question of wind farm layout. Many of the stakeholders present in the
network surrounding leasing remain key actors in the discussion of wind farm layout,
namely those whose marine activities would be diverted or displaced by a wind farm’s
footprint. In translating the wind farm site to this network, complexities of siting are
assumed to be resolved – those actors that are displaced by the WEA, such as the DOD
and large-scale shipping that must divert all activities from the lease area, are not present
in the network surrounding wind farm layout.
==PAGE:66==
48
3 The Socio-Technical Network
3.8.3
Wind Farm Layout
The map of stakeholders involved in the layout design of a ﬂoating wind farm in Morro
Bay is illustrated again in Figure 3.10. This network is predicted – as the lease has not
been awarded the process for determining the layout of a potential wind farm has not
oﬃcially begun. Figure 3.10 represents future dialogue in the form of dashed arrows –
the only communication existing at present is between the USCG, other marine users,
the regulatory agency, and research institutions.
Figure 3.10: ANT Map - Wind Farm Layout
At the heart of the network that constructs a wind farm layout are questions of access
and design. Although the ﬁnal layout approval is conducted by BOEM, the question of
==PAGE:67==
3.8 Discussion
49
wind farm access is driven by the US Coast Guard and wind farm layout design largely
centers the developer.
3.8.3.1
Wind Farm Access
The concept of the layout of wind farm layout is inherently tied to those who must
navigate within its footprint. A precondition for navigation is access – which users are
allowed to operate within the wind farm space. The question of access has been explored
by Nadaï and Cointe (2020), who state "here ’accessing’ has a double meaning. It means
colonizing spaces in the sense of expanding into or occupying spaces, but also colonizing
in the sense of changing ways of perceiving, representing, practicing, and performing".
In other words, the question of who might access a wind farm is not only an issue of
who might be displaced through placing wind turbines in previously utilized space but
also a question of the ways that each of these groups interacts with and relates to the
newly-constructed wind farm.
It is noted that the Morro Bay Call Area exists in a space currently occupied by many
mariners and other groups. Although the proposed Morro Bay area is currently used by
the ﬁshing and shipping industries, the concept of colonization remains pertinent as the
wind farm changes the value of the land in particular to indigenous groups that aim to
designate the area as a NMS, and to those marine and avian species whose migration
patterns will be impacted by the installation of such massive structures in the water.
The USCG translates questions of access to regulation in the development of navi-
gational guidelines. Navigational guidelines, when constructed collaboratively, are the
transcriptions of those physical limits, as expressed in a quantitative frame, that must
be placed on a wind farm layout to mitigate the impact on other marine users.
Regulation, in this case, is likely a PARS, which provides recommendations for the
layout based on a compromise between marine users. Compromise does not require
consensus. In this compromise, the wind farm layout design is used as a medium to allow
for diﬀerent groups to use the space for their needs – for example, the wind turbines are
placed far enough apart to limit wake eﬀects and that most vessels might pass through
(Unites States Coast Guard, 2020). Yet both groups make concessions – the wind turbines
are not placed optimally for energy yield, and some vessels are limited in navigation
through wind farms. As expressed in past PARS and interviews, the USCG indicates
that a grid layout is required for a successful SAR procedure. It is noted that SAR
requirements in the North Sea do not require strict grid layouts, but do require images
and models of proposed layouts for evaluation (Oﬀshore Energy Liaison Oﬃcer, HM
Coastguard, and MCA, 2018), indicating that there may exist multiple solutions that
are discarded in the USCG’s framing.
Like the DOD in the case of wind farm site, the USCG is neither the designer of
the wind farm layout nor gives ﬁnal approval but holds determining inﬂuence as the
translator of marine use to recommendations, as well as an actor with a vested interest
in access to the wins farm for SAR. This is in large part due to the role of the USCG as
a liaison for multiple maritime industries, especially the shipping industry. Through the
PARS, the USCG sets a baseline for the wind farm layout design, such that it "enables
==PAGE:68==
50
3 The Socio-Technical Network
coordination without the need for consensus and intense interactions among individuals
across diﬀerent disciplines" (Tharchen, Garud, and Henn, 2020) – the wind farm layout
allows for other marine actors to utilize the space without spurring controversy.
3.8.3.2
Wind Farm Layout Design
The question of wind farm layout design is shaped by the nature of technology, the tools
used for placing turbines and analyzing costs, the geographical environment, and USCG
recommendations. The wind farm developer produces a wind farm layout that maximizes
proﬁts within the multidisciplinary bounds set by regulators and stakeholders who seek
access to the wind farm. The wind farm layout design is hence an intermediary inscribed
by the wind farm developer, which then transitions to an actor as it is constructed and
exerts agency through impacting access routes, ﬁshing activities, the marine environment,
and potentially those impacted by the wind farm visually.
Floating wind turbines, as indicated in stakeholder interviews, introduce novel design
challenges in constructing a wind farm layout. In the design of ﬂoating wind farms, the
introduction of mooring lines and dynamic cables introduce the possibility of collision
or entanglement with marine life, ﬁshing gear, and debris that might not have been the
case for monopile or other ﬁxed-bottom foundations. This technology complicates the
question of access for ﬁshermen, who on the East Coast have been permitted to trawl
within wind farms but (as according to developers (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021))
will likely not have this possibility in CA. The questions of entanglement and secondary
entanglement with mooring lines and dynamic cables merits further study that must
be coupled to the study of wind farm access of other marine users whose gear might
contribute to this concern.
The introduction of ﬂoating technology introduces a dynamic component to wind
farm layout design – ﬂoating turbines tethered by catenary mooring lines move laterally,
resulting in a wind farm that is not rigid. This variability in wind farm position reminds us
that "any notion of design is always incomplete, and design initiatives are always ongoing
and full of future potentialities" (Tharchen, Garud, and Henn, 2020). Hypothetically, this
concept is reinforced by the comparative ﬂexibility of wind farm layout design – there are
far fewer barriers to re-positioning or removing an under-performing ﬂoating turbine than
its ﬁxed counterpart. Within a technical frame, positioning and re-positioning turbines
are contingent on a variety of factors discussed in this chapter, including the seabed
conditions and the position of other mooring lines to avoid crossings and abrasion.
Within the actor-network of wind farm layout, each turbine is a hybrid actor in part
deﬁned by the space it occupies. The space held by each turbine cannot be separated
from its relationship to the other turbines in the farm as they are connected by dynamic
cables, and inﬂuence each other’s energy production through wake loss. The network
that deﬁnes the layout design will evolve over the lifetime of the wind farm – not only
if the developer moves a turbine – as those who share the resource space continually
redeﬁne it.
==PAGE:69==
3.9 Conclusions
51
3.9
Conclusions
The application of Actor-Network Theory allows us to shed light on the emergence of
a ﬂoating wind farm in Morro Bay, CA. Through mapping stakeholder perspectives
gathered in interviews to a socio-technical network, we can detect the socio-material
work of assembling a wind farm’s site and layout design. Hence, a wind farm is deﬁned
through framing and translation by both human and non-human actors in its evolving
network.
In this analysis, the wind farm site is considered a resource space, constructed by and
inﬂuencing multidisciplinary actors with a stake in its location. The wind farm site is ﬁrst
an intermediary in the network as deﬁned through a collaborative process orchestrated
by BOEM. This process sets a technical frame that prioritizes federal actors such as the
DOD and NMS that have a stake in marine spatial planning. Once the wind farm site is
passed to the developer in the form of a lease, the wind farm can transition from being
an intermediary to an actor and thereby impact those actors who have commercial and
personal interests in the region.
The wind farm layout design adds dimension to the wind farm site – stakeholders that
access the wind farm, from the marine life near the cables, including the USCG’s SAR
operations, ﬁshing and shipping in the area, and marine life contribute to the resource
space.
Through interdisciplinary layout design, the ’best’ layout is not necessarily
synonymous with the most proﬁtable wind farm but is a design emerging out of a hybrid
collaborative network.
Within the current paradigm, the construction of the wind farm layout necessarily
follows the siting of the wind farm. This is explicitly limited as the USCG is not engaged
until the lease is awarded – a point at which some engaged view it as too late. Yet this
evokes the question of whether a "better" solution might be achieved through simultaneous
choice of site and layout.
==PAGE:70==
52
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 4
Transition from the
socio-technical to the
techno-economic
The previous chapters examine the process of determining a wind farm site and layout
from a socio-technical perspective – how hybrid actors construct the resource space of
a wind farm. Analysis to this point has largely treated wind farms as socio-material
objects that take up space that is shared and constructed by multiple actors. Once wind
turbines are installed, they not only have a physical impact but, also begin to generate
proﬁt for the developer and power for consumers, homes and workplaces.
The following sections of this thesis seek to explore a wind farm developer’s techno-
economic perspective through a layout optimization for LCOE. In translation from
socially constructed phenomena of wind farm site and layout to a techno-economic frame,
some stakeholder perspectives are muted and others are ampliﬁed. In ANT, the act of
translation re-deﬁnes a socio-technical network – in this chapter, we seek to map this
translation and comment upon its implications in the networks forming ﬂoating wind
farm design.
4.1
Developer: Translating Wind Farm Site and
Layout to a Socio-technical Actor
The latter part of this thesis simulates stepping into the actor-network described in the
previous chapters as an engineer at a company developing a bid for the Morro Bay Call
Area. The developer is chosen as it is the actor ultimately responsible for the design of a
wind farm layout and for translating that design to an operational wind farm. Figure
4.1 below demonstrates the integration of the siting and layout design networks in the
development process.
==PAGE:72==
54
4 Transition from the socio-technical to the techno-economic
Figure 4.1: ANT Map - Wind Farm Developer
In the preparation of a bid for a wind farm lease, the developer determines both the
site and a preliminary layout. The layout is then used as input to estimate the cost of
energy produced by a wind farm, an indicator of its competitiveness in the energy market
as well as eventual proﬁtability.
BOEM determines leasing criteria, namely the wind farm site and the auction type.
The siting network as described in Chapter 3 treats the Morro Bay Call Area as one site
with which the actor-network interacts. Yet, as stated in interviews, the Morro Bay Call
Area will likely be ﬁlled with two to three wind farms, pre-designated by BOEM (NREL
Researcher 1, 2021). Hence, when preparing a bid for a lease area, the developer will
conduct a nested process of siting to determine which sub-area to pursue. In this process,
the developer translates the WEA, a socially constructed resource space, to a proposed
site and layout deﬁned in a technical frame, and ﬁnally to economic ﬁgures that indicate
a wind farm’s proﬁtability.
BOEM has stated that in its decisions it may take into account the highest bid for the
lease (single-factor) but may also consider whether the bidder holds a Power Purchase
Agreement (PPA) (Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, 2021b). By these criteria, a
developer’s success in the bidding process is tied to its ability to drive down the cost of a
wind farm.
==PAGE:73==
4.2 Wind Farm Assetization
55
4.2
Wind Farm Assetization
Recent research in the ﬁeld of renewable energy development has focused on the process
of turning renewable energy projects from material, technical entities to ﬁnancial assets.
The proﬁtability of a wind farm in CA is tied to its ability to obtain a PPA, which is an
agreement with a load-serving entity to purchase power at a constant price. One researcher
states that in CA (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021). Hence, it is of interest to examine "the
construction of a predictable income associated with renewable energy", which "results
from a construction that operates on multiple levels" (Nadaï and Cointe, 2020). Once a
PPA is established and a wind farm is deemed proﬁtable, it triggers "investment in the
development of concrete economic activities with simultaneous productive, territorial,
and political dimensions" (Nadaï and Cointe, 2020).
Many of the local stakeholders cited in the previous chapter call for a wind farm lease
area large enough to be proﬁtable – a feature of a project that is central to the question
of assetization, as "proﬁt-making and ﬁnancial values tend to be more prevalent in project
development processes than described here, with less attention and work devoted to the
collective or environmental dimension of the projects" (Nadaï and Cointe, 2020). The
process of assetization can be characterized as "constructing new value out of already
existing objects by engaging them in a future-oriented narrative" (Nadaï and Cointe,
2020). Hence, once a ﬂoating wind farm is materialized, its value as an asset is tied to
its ongoing proﬁtability over its stated lifetime.
The process of obtaining a PPA is pertinent to the emergence of a wind farm, as
"developers need to take into account local conﬁgurations in order to get access to wind
(site)" but "proﬁt-making and ﬁnancial values tend to be more prevalent in project
development processes" (Nadaï and Cointe, 2020). A researcher interviewed emphasizes
this distinction: "the planning process is ultimately diﬀerent as opposed to a load-serving
entity process" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021), specifying that "a load-serving entity is going
to have some sort of baked-in process that they need to adhere to that would be diﬀerent
[from the BOEM planning process]" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021)
4.3
Levelized Cost of Energy: A Techno-economic
Metric
The LCOE is a metric representing the average net present cost of energy generation over
the lifetime of the wind farm. LCOE can be seen as a calculative device that helps to
frame the site as proﬁtable. LCOE is often used as a decision-making tool by researchers,
regulators, and load-serving entities as partially indicative of the projected price of energy
on the electricity market – "it’s not perfect but it’s a good proxy for what the eventual
PPA price is going to be" (Consultant (DNV), 2021). It is noted that LCOE is not the
sole metric for determining a PPA, as "the purchaser will look at not just the power
purchase agreement price of the LCOE, but also the value of oﬀshore winds the broader
electricity system" (LBNL Researcher, 2021).
==PAGE:74==
56
4 Transition from the socio-technical to the techno-economic
In conversation with business developers, LCOE is highlighted as a general indicator
of the technology readiness level of ﬂoating wind farms (Innovation Department Ørsted,
2021). At present, WEAs are not yet ﬁnalized and LCOE presents itself as a useful tool
for approximating wind farm techno-economic performance. Hence, LCOE is employed to
assess both wind farm feasibility in terms of attractiveness to investors and to developers
who track the progress of ﬂoating oﬀshore wind as an industry. A researcher states,
"LCOE is a very comparable metric, both between diﬀerent sites but also diﬀerent
technologies and diﬀerent capital projects of diﬀerent lenders" (ORE Catapult Researcher,
2021).
In the case of wind farm design and optimization, LCOE is a metric that represents
the trade-oﬀbetween ﬁnancial costs and annual energy production (AEP)1. The choice
of LCOE for optimization allows for both of these metrics to be assessed, resulting in
solutions that include both technical and ﬁnancial feasibility. This choice of LCOE as an
optimizer is echoed across researchers in wind farm design: "I think LCOE is deﬁnitely
the best metric to optimize on" (ORE Catapult Researcher, 2021).
4.4
Lost Perspectives in the Formulation of LCOE
The LCOE falls short of success as a socio-economic indicator as it impacts only a select
set of stakeholders in the wind farm development process – those concerned with the
cost of the wind farm and the cost of electricity. These stakeholders include national
governments, research institutions, developers, and electricity purchasers – "if you focus
on who’s engaged with LCOE, you’ll ﬁnd that it’s a very limited number of people.
It’s the Public Service Commission, the CaliforniaEnergy Commission, the National
Laboratories, etc" (Researcher (University of Delaware), 2021).
Many of the stakeholders discussed in the previous chapter are lost in the deﬁnition
of LCOE – "the stakeholder aspects are never calculated in any of these formulas"
(Researcher (University of Delaware), 2021). Externalities include environmental impacts
as inﬂuenced by wind turbine Installation and operations, visual impact to those on
shore, diverting shipping and ﬁshing2 activity.
The PPA price impacts local consumers indirectly through electricity rates, which are
in part tied to the energy source. Conversely, many local stakeholders are not impacted
by the LCOE. For example, "rates are not what a ﬁsherman cares about, so the whole
purchase process are all tied ultimately to state-level rates" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021).
In addition, many researchers agree that LCOE may not an ideal metric to represent
a wind farm’s techno-economic value to a developer (Katherine Dykes et al., 2020). It
has been indicated that lifetime proﬁt (expressed as return on investment (ROI)) may
be a ’better’ indicator, as the objective of the energy industry is not producing the
cheapest possible power, but generating the highest revenue over the lifetime of the
project(Katherine Dykes et al., 2020). Hence, closer to the time of development, ROI
1AEP is the total amount of electricity generated by each wind turbine in a wind farm in one year
given in kilowatt-hour (kWh). The equation for AEP E.5 is found in Section 6.1.5
2Compensation of ﬁshermen for lost revenue might be included in LCOE if pertinent.
==PAGE:75==
4.5 Reconstruction of Socio-technical Perspectives to a Quantitative Model
57
may be deemed a ’better’ indicator, but LCOE is more applicable at California’s current
stage of development.
4.5
Reconstruction of Socio-technical
Perspectives to a Quantitative Model
The developer prioritizes LCOE yet is inﬂuenced by the interests of other actors. To
evaluate LCOE in a way that includes the multidimensional nature of wind farm planning
and development, we reconstruct socio-technical stakeholder perspectives in the form of
a quantitative model.
First, the wind farm site is simpliﬁed and translated to a two-dimensional boundary
and bathymetry map, and a weather proﬁle. In this translation to a technical frame,
some social controversies can be directly mapped to their technical representation. Some
examples include:
• Visual impact of a wind farm is quantiﬁed by the distance to shore.
• The impact on the ﬁshing industry is mapped to the area of the wind farm, which
correlates to ﬁshing ground lost.
• The impact on the shipping industry correlates to the perimeter of the wind farm,
and their proximity to shipping routes.
Once the technical frame is established costing calculations that translate distances,
areas, and depths to economic values are used to evaluate the ﬁnancial viability of the
wind farm.
The concept of ﬁnding a suitable wind farm site, sometimes called "macro-siting" is
tied to the controversy mapped in the previous section through stakeholder "scenarios".
The scenarios explored include splitting of the Call Areas in ways that might inﬂuence
diﬀerent stakeholders involved in the network surrounding the wind farm footprint (Figure
3.6), including the DOD, shipping, ﬁshing, and those concerned with the visual impact
of wind farms. Optimizations within these simpliﬁed wind farm footprints can then be
used to explore the relationship between the relative proﬁtability of a site and its impact
on those with a stake in its location.
Next, the wind farm layout design, also called turbine "micro-siting", is explored
through the application of the wind farm perimeter as well as an introduced spacing
constraint3 that is tied to the depth of the wind farm. The spacing constraint has multiple
implications: the spacing between wind turbines inﬂuences the developers through an
impact on the energy production and costs, but also directly ties into questions of access
to the wind farm – the further turbines are separated, the more accessible the wind farm
is to larger vessels. In addition, the spacing is tied to the length of the mooring lines and
hence to the possibility for (secondary) entanglement with marine species. Finally, the
3From an ANT-perspective, a constraint is a framing device that is constructed to uphold a certain
frame (i.e. to include or exclude aspects of a network). In an optimization context, a constraint is a
numerical term that is enforced as a limit on the design variables in a problem. In optimization, a
solution is only feasible if all constraints are satisﬁed.
==PAGE:76==
58
4 Transition from the socio-technical to the techno-economic
question of layout is explored through a comparison of optimization for a regular layout,
as recommended by the USCG in New England. This anticipated recommendation of a
regular layout is brought into question through comparison with irregular optimization.
4.6
Reﬂections
A wind farm is a hybrid techno-socio-economic network with multidisciplinary impacts
and must be analyzed as such. For a feasible development of a ﬂoating wind farm
project, the ﬁnal site and layout must include both techno-economic elements as well as
the perspectives of those actors who hold a stake in its physical manifestation. In the
following chapters, we explore a means of mapping stakeholder perspectives to preliminary
wind farm design to determine their impact on wind farm cost-competitiveness in the
form of LCOE. The reconstruction of a socio-technical narrative of wind farm siting and
design to a techno-economic space sacriﬁces social dimension, as all stakeholders that are
not involved in either the research and development of ﬂoating oﬀshore wind or those
without a stake in the load-serving entity’s process lose inﬂuence in the formulation of
LCOE. Thereby this analysis allows for cross-disciplinary reﬂection and the incorporation
of impacts that cannot be necessarily quantiﬁed, yet are integral to the emergence of a
wind farm.
==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 5
Floating Wind Farms Technical
Background
As we enter the techno-economic portion of this thesis, this section provides the technical
and theoretical background for ﬂoating wind farm optimization. First, the technical
assumptions made for ﬂoating technology, including the wind turbine, the ﬂoating
substructure, including the station-keeping system, and the electrical system used in
the optimization model are explained. The section concludes with an overview of the
optimization framework and current research in the ﬁeld.
5.1
Systems Engineering in the Wind Industry
A wind energy system is characterized by complexity, heterogeneity, and uncertainty
(Dykes et al., 2011). The design of wind turbines and farms is a multidisciplinary problem,
involving a variety of stakeholders with technical, political, economic, and social interests.
Development, the cost of energy, and market barriers impose heterogeneous challenges in
the realization of wind energy systems.
The ﬁeld of ﬂoating oﬀshore wind is uncertain and novel, as at present only a few
demonstration projects are underway. The industry has yet to stabilize upon a standard
system – there are currently multiple leading designs of ﬂoating substructures, as well as
numerous associated challenges, including the electrical system, mooring, and anchoring,
installation, and operation procedures. Additional uncertainty is anticipated due to
weather, which is made increasingly unpredictable with climate change. Finally, local
political and economic developments aﬀect the design, and thereby the ﬁnancial viability
of wind energy projects.
In this portion of thesis, we take a systems engineering approach to develop and
analyze an optimized ﬂoating wind farm site and layout. Therefore, in the following
sections the diﬀerent techno-economic components of a ﬂoating wind farm system are
introduced.
5.2
Components of a Floating Wind Farm System
The study of a ﬂoating wind farm’s layout necessarily integrates its technical components.
The components, shown in Table 5.1 and their characteristics are further described in
the following sections.
==PAGE:78==
60
5 Floating Wind Farms Technical Background

Key technological assumptions made in this thesis are as follows, and will be explained

Technology Assumptions:

• Semi-submersible substructure
• 3-leg semi-taut hybrid mooring system
