5.2.1
Wind Turbine
The size of commercially-available oﬀshore wind turbines is increasing rapidly to drive
down the cost of energy (Walter Musial, Beiter, and Nunemaker, 2020). As the develop-
ment of ﬂoating wind in CA is at an early stage, we assume that a 15MW wind turbine,
maybe even a larger one, will be commercially available by the time of construction
of a ﬂoating wind farm in CA. Ergo, the IEA 15 MW oﬀshore reference wind turbine
(Gaertner et al., 2020) is employed in this thesis both in the model for costs and energy
production. The reference turbine is designed in a corporation by NREL and DTU
(Gaertner et al., 2020). The technical design parameters of the turbine are presented in
the Appendix D.
==PAGE:79==
5.2 Components of a Floating Wind Farm System
61
5.2.2
Floater Concept
The key component of a ﬂoating wind turbine is the ﬂoater concept, which provides the
static stability of the wind turbine in the water. Floating substructures currently used in
research and industry can be categorized into three main types: Semi-Submersible, Spar
Buoy, and Tension Leg and are presented in Figure 5.1. Moreover, hybrid ﬂoater concepts
are also currently designed and in testing, where diﬀerent features of the three main
ﬂoater designs are combined. The three main ﬂoater concepts are further elaborated on
below. The substructure design is driven by the site conditions including the bathymetry,
soil conditions, local infrastructure, and supply chain capabilities (Ma et al., 2019).
Figure 5.1: Floater Concepts (Walter Musial, Beiter, Tegen, et al., 2016)
5.2.2.1
Semi-Submersible
A semi-submersible substructure can be deployed in water depths from 50 m and
downwards (Arapogianni et al., 2013). The foundation includes large columns that are
connected by steel bars (Ma et al., 2019). The static stability is maintained by buoyancy
and the waterplane area. The ﬂoater platform itself assures enough stability to support
the ﬂoating turbine without mooring lines. This is the key advantage of the design, which
allows to fully assemble the turbines at the quayside and then tow them out to the ﬁnal
oﬀshore site. Moreover, maintenance of the turbines can be performed at the quayside
and prevents the use of expensive vessels for open ocean operations (Walter Musial,
Beiter, Tegen, et al., 2016). However, the disadvantage of the ﬂoater is that waves induce
increased motion, resulting in higher loads on the mooring lines compared to the other
ﬂoaters (Ma et al., 2019).
==PAGE:80==
62
5 Floating Wind Farms Technical Background
5.2.2.2
Spar Buoy
The spar buoy is designed as a steel or concrete cylinder ﬁlled with water and gravel,
which ensures that the center of gravity is kept below the center of buoyancy and allows
the wind turbine to ﬂoat and stay upright in the ocean (Ma et al., 2019). This foundation
is used in the Hywind Scotland ﬂoating wind farm. The stability is ensured by ballast
(Walter Musial, Beiter, Tegen, et al., 2016). Due to the large draft spar buoys can only
be deployed in water depth from 120 m downwards (Arapogianni et al., 2013). For the
design to be fully assembled at quayside a deep-water port is mandatory otherwise the
turbines need to be assembled in waters deeper than the draft of the spar, namely around
120 m, which will increase the commissioning costs (Ma et al., 2019).
5.2.2.3
Tension Leg Platform (TLP)
A TLP substructure can be deployed in water depths from 50 m and downwards (Ara-
pogianni et al., 2013). The TLP foundation cannot ensure stability itself and therefore is
dependent on the mooring-line tension. This is a drawback of the design since the system
is unstable until it is attached to the mooring lines. This can cause some diﬃculties in
the deployment in deep waters (Walter Musial, Beiter, Tegen, et al., 2016). The position
and stability of the platform are ensured by vertical pre-tensioned tethers (Ma et al.,
2019).
5.2.3
Floater Selection
It was found that the ﬂoater choice depends greatly on the site characteristics and
infrastructure, including the port facilities, the route from the port to the ﬁnal oﬀshore
site, and the supply chain facilities. The deep waters in Morro Bay narrow the selection
to a spar-buoy and a semi-submersible ﬂoater, as a TLP ﬂoater requires vertical taut
mooring lines, which pose an installation challenge in such deep waters and thereby
increases the installation costs. Next, the port facilities, namely the draft depth, are
considered. Table 5.2 lists the big ports in CA and their water depth. It is noted that the
choice of the installation port causes a challenge in central CA and thereby introduces
uncertainties because all existing big ports in CA are already running up to their full
capacity.
Table 5.2: Central Coast Port Water Depth (See California, 2021)
Port
Water Depth [m]
Humboldt Bay
15
Long Beach
18
Heuneme
10
Los Angeles
16
Oakland
15
San Francisco
5
==PAGE:81==
5.2 Components of a Floating Wind Farm System
63
It can be observed that none of these ports has a deep enough draft to allow an
assembly of a spar buoy, with a usual draft of 120 m, in the port, which would increase
the installation costs as the wind turbines would then need to be assembled oﬀshore.
Therefore, we choose a semi-submersible ﬂoater to be applied in this thesis. The choice is
also supported by the study of Beiter, Walt Musial, et al. (2020), a NREL researcher and
a wind farm developer, interviewed for this thesis (NREL Researcher 1, 2021)(Developer
2 (Castle Wind), 2021). In future work, it will be necessary to include the hydrodynamic
forces as well as the supply chain options for a better-informed choice.
5.2.4
Station Keeping System
The station keeping system is a coupled system of mooring lines and anchors. Its function
is to keep the wind turbine in position and further maintain stability, where the amount
of stability needed depends on the ﬂoater concept (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020). The
mooring lines are connected to the ﬂoating substructure and to anchors, which are ﬁxed
to the seabed. Hence, once the platform is installed and anchored the system is stable
(Arapogianni et al., 2013).
The design drivers for the station keeping system are (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020):
• Floater type
• Environmental conditions
• Excursion limitations
• Water depth
• Marine growth
• Mooring response and peak load
• Loading at the anchor
• Cost system including Ancillaries
5.2.4.1
Mooring Lines
The two main mooring line designs, illustrated in Figure 6.12b, are catenary mooring
and taut mooring. Depending on the design, commonly used and analyzed mooring
designs for ﬂoating wind turbines are 3-leg, 4-leg, 6-leg, and 9-leg systems, where a leg
represents one mooring line (Ozmutlu, 2020). Moreover, mooring systems are deﬁned as
either spread moorings, where the mooring lines are distributed around the platform, or
a single point, only attached to one point on the platform (Castillo, 2020).
Figure 5.2: Mooring Designs (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020)
==PAGE:82==
64
5 Floating Wind Farms Technical Background
Catenary Mooring System The mooring lines in a catenary design ("free-hanging
lines") are made of either long steel chains or a combination of wires and steel chains
at the lower section with a horizontal connection to the seabed. The weight of the
steel chains in the lower section of the mooring lines resting on the seaﬂoor generates
the restoring force to keep the turbine in position. Therefore, the loads applied on the
anchors are only horizontal. The advantage of the catenary design is the low cost of the
anchor because they only need to withstand horizontal forces. The main drawback is
a larger footprint than other mooring systems with a similar application (Ikhennicheu
et al., 2020).
Taut Mooring System: High-tension mooring lines maintain the stability of the
turbine. Common materials used for taut moorings are synthetic ﬁbers or wires. A
taut mooring system usually has an angle of 30◦to 45◦between the mooring line and the
ﬂoater, or even 0◦if a TLP substructure is used (Toledo Monfort, 2017), which induces
combined vertical and horizontal loads on the anchor, resulting in higher loads than
that of a catenary mooring system. The main advantages of taut mooring systems are a
smaller footprint and shorter mooring lines. The main drawbacks are higher costs for
the anchors and more complex installation procedures (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020).
Semi-taut Mooring System A combination of taut and catenary mooring systems
is known as semi-taut mooring design. The design has the advantage of shorter mooring
lines compared to the length in a catenary mooring design. Moreover, fatigue loading is
reduced due to the allowance of both vertical and horizontal movement of the ﬂoating
sub-structure. This mooring design is suitable for deep-water applications due to its
smaller footprint compared to a catenary system and lighter mooring lines due to material
savings (Xodus Group, 2020).
5.2.4.2
Anchor Types
The selection of the anchors for a ﬂoating wind turbine depends on the substructure
design, mooring system design, soil conditions and holding capacity requirements (Ma
et al., 2019). The diﬀerent anchors suitable for ﬂoating wind turbines are shown in Figure
5.3. Hereafter the features of the main anchor types Dead Weight, Drag Embedment,
Piles and Vertical Load are brieﬂy described:
==PAGE:83==
5.2 Components of a Floating Wind Farm System
65
Figure 5.3: Anchor Types (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020)
Dead Weight Anchor is the oldest (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020) and the simplest
anchor (Toledo Monfort, 2017). The holding capacity is generated by the weight of
the anchor and partly by the friction between the anchor and the soil. It is capable of
resisting vertical and/or horizontal forces. These anchors are suitable for all soil types
(Toledo Monfort, 2017).
Drag Embedment Anchor (DEA) is the most common (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020)
and least expensive anchor currently available (Toledo Monfort, 2017)(Ma et al., 2019).
DEAs penetrate into the seabed, where the holding capacity generated by the resistance
of the soil in front of the anchor, is mainly suitable for large horizontal forces and thereby
the preferred anchor for catenary mooring systems (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020). These
anchors are well applicable in most soil types except for hard rocks (Toledo Monfort,
2017).
Pile Anchors are divided into three categories: driven, suction, and torpedo depend-
ing on their installation. They are capable of withstanding both vertical and horizontal
forces, based on the friction of the soil along with the anchor and the lateral soil resis-
tance, and thereby suitable for taut mooring systems and vertical mooring systems for
TLP ﬂoaters (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020). Piles are the most expensive anchors available.
However, due to their high holding capacity, they are a feasible cost-eﬀective option for
sharing anchors between ﬂoating oﬀshore wind turbines in a wind farm (Ma et al., 2019).
All types of pile anchors are suitable for application in soft and medium clay and driven
anchors are also well suitable in sand (Toledo Monfort, 2017).
A Vertical Load Anchor (VLA) has the same installation procedure as a DEA but
with deeper penetration into the seabed. VLAs are suitable for deep water installations
==PAGE:84==
66
5 Floating Wind Farms Technical Background
and compatible with both catenary and taut mooring systems as they are capable to
withstand both vertical and horizontal forces (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020). The anchors
are expensive but less expensive than piles, which makes them an economically viable
solution for the use of shared anchors. They are applicable in soft and medium clay soil
(Toledo Monfort, 2017).
5.2.4.3
Deep Water Station Keeping System
To determine the station keeping system for ﬂoating wind turbines in deep waters, a
few studies have been reviewed. The design standards for ﬂoating oﬀshore wind turbine
mooring system designs are based on the experience from the oil and gas industry and
may lead to conservative solutions (Ma et al., 2019).
Ikhennicheu et al. (2020) conclude in their study, that for water depths of 250 m
downwards a mooring system based on synthetic ﬁber ropes with either a taut or semi-
taut mooring system would be an auspicious conﬁguration, because of the lightweight of
the ﬁber ropes and their high elasticity. Ma et al. (2019) add the advantage of decreasing
material costs, less dynamic tension due to the lower stiﬀness, and an increasing fatigue
performance. Toledo Monfort (2017) agrees that the most feasible system might include
taut mooring systems, but comments that further research is needed to evaluate costs
because a taut conﬁguration will decrease mooring line lengths and material costs but
increase anchor cost due to the need for suction piles or VLAs.
Gómez et al. (2015)Rhodri et al. (2018) and Xodus Group (2020) propose a hybrid
mooring system design for deep waters, as shown in Figure 5.4, which uses either taut
or semi-taut mooring lines with a combination of synthetic ﬁber ropes and steel chains.
The heavy steel chains are used in the upper part of the mooring line in the connection
with the ﬂoater and in the lower part of the mooring line to reduce the uplift angle on
the ground, which would allow the usage of DEAs.
Figure 5.4: Mooring Types (Reliability in a Sea of Risk, 2016)
==PAGE:85==
5.2 Components of a Floating Wind Farm System
67
Concluding, it was found that further research is needed to evaluate the use of a
catenary vs taut/semi-taut mooring system in deep/ultra-deep waters, evaluating the
cost trade-oﬀof the anchor selection, the material costs, and the smaller footprint in
deep waters. Moreover, the use of shared anchors should be further analyzed in wind
farm design.
As previously described, the station keeping system design depends on diﬀerent
drivers. The optimal design assessment of the station keeping system requires an analysis
of the ﬂoater integrating hydrodynamic and aerodynamic coupling with the mooring
lines because both are aﬀecting the stability of the structure (Ikhennicheu et al., 2020).
However, a mooring system optimization including the dynamic responses from the wind
and wave data is out of the scope of the thesis. Hence, we employ a simpliﬁed mooring
model that evaluates the inﬂuence of the water depth and the ﬂoater selection based on
the literature research above.
5.2.5
Mooring System Design Selection
Based on the literature review of the station keeping system for ﬂoating oﬀshore wind
turbines in deep waters above, it is found that the most suitable option for a mooring
system in water depth ranging between 700-1100 m as in the case of Morro Bay, is a 3-leg
semi-taut conﬁguration with hybrid mooring lines (chain + synthetic ﬁber + chain) and
DEAs for each mooring line. The semi-taut system reduces the line material costs as well
as the footprint. Additionally, the hybrid lines with synthetic ﬁber ropes in the middle
decrease the weight of the mooring lines. The chain part on the ground allows the use of
DEAs, which are the most economically favorable anchor option. Based on the choice of
semi-submersible substructure, a 3-leg mooring system design with a 120◦distribution
(Xu et al., 2021), presented in Figure 5.5 is assumed. The mooring line model deployed
in this thesis is further examined in Section 6.1.4.
Figure 5.5: 3-leg Mooring System Design with a Semi-submersible ﬂoater (Xu et al.,
2021)
This analysis is not including hydrodynamic motions and therefore future work will
be necessary to further improve the mooring design.
==PAGE:86==
68
5 Floating Wind Farms Technical Background
5.2.6
Electrical System
As the industry progresses and wind turbines and farms become larger, the capacity of
subsea cables has increased. As this thesis aims to model wind farms to be built approxi-
mately ﬁve years in the future, it is pertinent to employ newly developed technology. It
is projected by NREL that ﬂoating wind farms will utilize 66 kV array cables, an upgrade
from the commonly-used 33 kV cables (Beiter, Walter Musial, et al., 2016). ORBIT has
sample cable data for conductor cross-sections of 185 mm2 and 630 mm2 and allows for
the optimization of choice of array cable. To allow for this ﬂexibility, both cables are
supplied to ORBIT as options for use in wind farm installation.
All currently commissioned oﬀshore wind farms utilize high voltage AC (HVAC)
cables for export purposes, though in projects currently under development in Europe
and the US high voltage DC (HVDC) cables will be deployed for export purposes. It
remains a question whether HVDC cables will be utilized on the West Coast for ﬂoating
purposes. A 2016 study by NREL determines that the answer depends on the distance
to landfall, comparing 132 kV HVAC, 220 kV HVAC, and 320 kV HVDC export cables
(Beiter, Walter Musial, et al., 2016), concluding that for ﬂoating purposes with export
lengths less than 100 km, a 220 kV HVAC export cable is most cost-eﬀective. Past
this distance, HVDC cables become the most suitable option due to decreased electrical
losses.
In this thesis, a 220 kV HVAC export cable is chosen, as the mean export cable
distance to the Morro Bay substation is near 90 km and sample export cable costing data
is made available in ORBIT. In future work, it will be necessary to consider both HVDC
and HVAC export cable options in this region, especially if the point of interconnection
is further from the site (for example at Diablo Canyon).
Cables used for ﬂoating applications will likely be dynamic stainless steel armored
copper cables. The cables are supported by buoyancy modules and equipped with
cable protection systems form at the contact points with the ocean ﬂoor (Pérez, 2019).
Although the dynamics of these cables are not included in the analysis, through ORBIT
we can estimate their length and corresponding installation requirements.
5.3
Wind Farm Optimization
5.3.1
Multidisciplinary Design Analysis and Optimization
As previously discussed, a wind farm system is a complex multidisciplinary system and
must be addressed as such in design and optimization. As described in this section, a wind
farm consists of aerodynamic, structural, and electrical aspects in its technical design alone.
Hence we apply Multidisciplinary Design Analysis and Optimization (MDAO), a technique
developed to solve design problems, incorporating multiple disciplines simultaneously. In
MDAO the sub-components of a wind farm are coupled and analyzed in a simulation
that encompasses the whole system costs. It also includes an optimization algorithm
that drives the design variables to optimize the entire system (Sanchez Perez-Moreno
et al., 2018).
==PAGE:87==
5.4 Objective Function Selection
69
5.3.2
TopFarm
The optimization of the ﬂoating wind farm in this thesis is performed in TopFarm.
TopFarm, developed by DTU Wind Energy, is a software for wind farm optimization
(DTU Wind Energy 2018). It is built on the open-source Multidisciplinary Design and
Analysis Optimization (openMDAO) framework and is available as a Python package
(DTU Wind Energy 2018). The two main components are PyWake, for the estimation of
the AEP, and TopFarm2, for the problem formulation (Riva et al., 2020).
5.3.3
PyWake
PyWake, an open-source wind farm simulation tool, is capable of calculating the ﬂow
ﬁelds, the power production, and the AEP of a wind farm using diﬀerent wake models
(Pederson et al., 2019). It incorporates the following components (Riva et al., 2020):
• Site: provide the wind proﬁle
• Wake Model: taking into account wake eﬀects, by calculating eﬀective wind
speeds
• Wind Turbine: estimation of the power and thrust curve of the turbines
• AEP Calculator: computing AEP for each turbine and then summarizing for
the whole wind farm
The wake model and the APE calculation applied in this thesis are elaborated in Section
6.1.5.
5.4
Objective Function Selection
As previously introduced in Chapter 4 LCOE is applied as the objective function to be
minimized through optimization in TopFarm. In the research ﬁeld of ﬂoating wind farm
design and optimization, LCOE has been consistently applied as a metric for assessing
cost and wake models (Maienza et al., 2020), (Izquierdo-Pérez et al., 2020), (Serrano
González et al., 2021), (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020). Moreover, the utilization of
this metric allows for the results of this thesis to be comparable to publicly available
studies and to contribute to the current conversation concerning ﬂoating oﬀshore wind
development.
5.5
Layout Optimization of Floating Wind Farms
Wind farm layout design is a highly-research ﬁeld, and many studies can be found for
the optimization of bottom-ﬁxed oﬀshore wind farms. Floating oﬀshore wind is a less
developed ﬁeld, and at present research has focused on smaller-scale demonstration
projects, concentrating on topics including substructure ﬂoater design, mooring line
design in shallow waters, and station-keeping system design with shared anchors. As
==PAGE:88==
70
5 Floating Wind Farms Technical Background
follows. few studies on layout optimization of commercial-scale ﬂoating wind farms have
been published.
Rodrigues et al. (2015) conducted a layout optimization study of movable ﬂoating
wind turbines to reduce losses due to wake eﬀects. They conclude that the eﬃciency of a
wind farm can be increased by simultaneous optimization of the anchor position and the
location of the turbines within the mooring lines for each wind direction, resulting in an
irregular layout.
Another study on layout optimization of ﬂoating hybrid wind-wave energy is found
by Izquierdo-Pérez et al. (2020). They present an optimization tool for grid ﬂoating
hybrid wind-wave farms with the objective function to minimize the LCOE. This study
assumes a regular layout for computational simplicity and visual impact.
Serrano González et al. (2021) present an optimization tool for micro-siting of ﬂoating
wind farms based on a weathervaning technology with the objective function to minimize
the LCOE. In the study, each ﬂoating wind turbine is connected to one mooring line.
Based on the weathervaning technology the turbines self-orientate into the wind direction
by moving around the anchor being the pivot point.
This study expands upon current research in multiple dimensions. First, this thesis
assumes deep water installation (> 200 m), which has not often been included in
ﬂoating oﬀshore wind optimization. Secondly, a variable spacing constraint depending
on the water depths is applied. This thesis is unique in that it integrates two advanced
models for wind farm yield (PyWake) and capital expenditures (ORBIT) in irregular
optimization using TopFarm, resulting in LCOE optimization that reﬂects nuanced
trade-oﬀs between cost and yield. Finally, the inclusion of socio-technical context allows
for a multidisciplinary analysis of wind farm optimization results.
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 6
Model Development
This thesis applies MDAO through nested optimization for the LCOE of a ﬂoating wind
farm. LCOE represents the average cost of the energy produced throughout the project’s
lifetime, taking into account the time value of money (Katherine Dykes et al., 2020)
LCOE =
PLT
t=0 CapExt+OpExt
(1+WACC)t
PLT
t=1
AEPt
(1+WACC)t
(6.1)
CapExt
: Capital Expenditures at year t [USD]
OpEx
: Operational Expenditures at year t [USD]
AEP
: Annual Energy Production at year t [MWh]
WACC
: Weighted Average Cost of Capital
LT
: Lifetime [years]
The lifetime is selected to be 30 years to be consistent with current assumptions
(Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020). The discount rate is taken as the Weighted Average
Cost of Capital (WACC), which considers the weight of all capital investment sources
and gives an adjusted required return. The base WACC value is chosen to be 5.4% as
calculated by NREL with inputs from industry, based on current ﬁnancing of ﬁxed-bottom
technology, and is varied as a sensitivity later in this thesis1.
Hence in LCOE optimization problem, the wind farm AEP, CapEx and OpEx must
be linked in the optimization model. In the following chapter, the development of these
models is explained in further detail.
Oﬀshore wind farm optimization can be divided into two aspects. The ﬁrst phase,
called wind farm macro-siting (Hou et al., 2019), relates to the construction area of the
wind farm. Hou et al. (2019) supports the argumentation in Chapter 3, that in the
macro-siting political and regulatory issues are the main concerns. Once a wind farm
site is deﬁned the layout of the wind turbines inside the site is of interest. Hence, in the
second phase called the micro-siting, the wind turbines positions are optimized (Han and
Nagamune, 2020). In this phase the wake eﬀects are one of the main concerns.
1This value was calculated with a 75% share of debt, 4.4% equity return, 12% debt rate, and 26%
tax rate, as based on literature values and validated by consultation with industry (Beiter, Walt Musial,
et al., 2020). A basic calculation for the WACC is included in Appendix F.

6 Model Development
6.1
Optimization Formulation
The overall optimization problem seeks to minimize the LCOE of a 1 GW wind farm,
using 67 15MW wind turbines, in the 2018 Morro Bay Call Area, subject to constraints
including the boundary of the Call Area, number of wind turbines, the minimum distance
between turbines as constrained by the relationship between mooring line footprint and
seabed depth. The optimization loop is presented in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Optimization Loop
The optimization is classiﬁed as the following:
• Single objective: minimize LCOE
• Constrained: constraints on minimum spacing, wind farm boundary
• Non-linear: cost and AEP components are highly non-linear
• Deterministic: the wind resource and cost models are treated as static
• Continuous: turbine x and y positions are continuous variables
• Single-decision: the problem is considered static and does not take into account
dynamic behavior
• Multi-disciplinary: the problem includes mechanical, aerodynamic, electrical, and
cost components among others
The Table below summarizes the Input data for the base case.

6.1.1
Base Case
In the base case the optimization is constrained by the 2018 Call Area perimeter, split
in two areas, shown in Figure 6.2, and a depth-dependent mooring line constraint. The
entire Call Area has been estimated to have a capacity of 2.4 GW (Beiter, Walt Musial,
et al., 2020). With conservative spacing constraints, the assumption of half the Call Area
as the design space for a 1 GW wind farm allows for the turbines to rearrange without
unreasonable excess areas. The Call Area is split in two roughly equal halves in order to
evaluate the potential and the trade-oﬀs in the diﬀerent parts of the whole Call Area.
The area is split in two ways: along a geographical contour parallel to the CA coast,
dividing the Call area into East and West portions, and horizontally dividing the Call
Area into North and South portions.
==PAGE:92==
74
6 Model Development
(a) Vertical
(b) Horizontal
Figure 6.2: Morro Bay Base case areas
Figure 6.2 shows the four diﬀerent areas evaluated in the base case. The design
variables in this case are the x and y positions of each turbine (constituting 134 design
variables). The position of the OSS is not included, as the exact electrical layout is not
included as part of this optimization.
In the assumed divisions of the Call Area any adjacent wind farm occupying other
parts of the Call Area is neglected. Thereby, some trade-oﬀs are not considered in this
analysis. For example, a wind farm closer to shore might be shielded by another wind
farm further from shore, resulting in increased wake losses.
6.1.2
Stakeholder-motivated Scenarios
As discussed in the previous chapter, the site and layout of this wind farm is a topic of
much speculation and contention among stakeholders. Hence, it is of interest to design
a wind farm that is limited by some of the key stakeholders presented in the previous
sections. Two main stakeholders inﬂuencing the wind farm layout have been chosen to
demonstrate the impact on design from those who play a part in the planning process.
1. First, constraints on wind farm layouts as historically recommended by the USCG,
in the form of a grid layout, are investigated in order to determine an optimal layout.
Finally, the optimized regular boundary is applied in an irregular optimization to
investigate whether signiﬁcant gains can be made with irregular optimization.
2. Second, the sites that are proposed by the DOD, shown in Figure 6.4, are considered,
as it it of interest to examine the impact of limiting the area for development in
the region.
6.1.2.1
Case 1: Coast Guard-recommended Regular grid spacing
As discussed in the previous section, the USCG recommends that all existing wind farms
in the US have at least a 1 NM by 1 NM square grid spacing, namely 1852 m by 1852 m.
==PAGE:93==
6.1 Optimization Formulation
75
In this case, the wind farm is constrained to a regular grid spacing, with design variables
of, demonstrated in Figure 6.3:
• Orientation angle θ: rotation of the wind farm rows about the center coordinate in
degrees
• Position of one turbine (x0, y0), determining position of the wind farm within the
boundary.
• Variable regular spacing (sgrid): consistent grid spacing, limited from 1 NM to 11D
spacing2.
• Number of rows: treated as a continuous variable, rounded up.
Figure 6.3: Grid Diagram
2D [m] is the wind turbine rotor diameter

6 Model Development
In this optimization, the wind farm layout is constrained to a grid – wind turbines
are at right angles from one another and equally spaced, with a minimum spacing of
1 NM. The wind farm is constrained to the entirety of the Morro Bay Call Area and
the number of turbines held constant at 67. As motivated by layouts in New England
(Unites States Coast Guard, 2020), rows are oﬀset by half the distance between turbines,
such that the row and column spacing are calculated as a function of the constant grid
spacing sgrid as follows:
srow = sgrid/
√
2

√
2
(6.2)
In this case, the optimization is achieved through a simple parameter sweep of the ﬁve
design variables. This is not computationally optimal, but allows for a global minimum
to be found without the need for discrete optimization. Two methods were employed in
this optimization.
1. Method 1: The wind farm is a grid of 67 turbines in a rectangular formation, with
variable number of rows. The wind farm is kept within the 2018 Morro Bay Call
Area through a penalty if turbines are placed outside the Call Area.
2. Method 2: The wind farm begins with 2000 turbines, with the location of one
turbine variable. Given the rotation angle and the initial position, turbines are
removed such that only 67 turbines remain in the Call Area. This method results
in a regular layout with portions of the perimeter along the Call Area perimeter.
6.1.2.2
Case 2: Department of Defense Proposed Areas
In this case, the Call Area currently proposed by the DOD is taken to be the wind farm
boundary, shown in Figure 6.4. The proposed north area is 400 km2 in size and the
area south 233 km2. In this optimization problem, the number of turbines is introduced
as a design variable, as the proposed number of 67 15 MW turbines should no longer
feasibly ﬁt in the design space. It is noted that as the number of turbines is discrete, it
is included in not the optimization loop but iterated externally.
Figure 6.4: alternative DOD proposed Areas
==PAGE:95==

6.1.3
Wind Farm Site Boundary
The wind farm site is translated into the model as a wind farm boundary, the border
in which the turbines are allowed to move. The boundary for the Morro Bay Call Area
is adapted from the geographic information system (GIS) data provided by (BOEM,
2021a). The software QGIS is used to process the GIS data into x/y coordinates. For
the boundaries in the diﬀerent optimization cases, the base data is used and adapted for
each case. Finally, the wind farm polygon is constructed from a set of boundary points
for use in TopFarm.
In simulating ﬂoating oﬀshore wind turbines, we must also implement a buﬀer on the
boundary in order to take into account the space occupied by the station keeping system.
A boundary buﬀer of 1000 m is hence implemented in the model.
Figure 6.5: Boundary Buﬀer
6.1.4
Mooring Line Model
Two of the cases of interest include a depth-dependent spacing constraint. The spacing
constraint is dependent on the mooring line system design. As previously examined
in Section 5.2.5 the mooring line model developed in this thesis has the following
characteristics:
• semi-taut / hybrid mooring lines
• 3-leg mooring with 120◦distribution
• line material: chain + synthetic ﬁbre + chain
• DEA anchors
The spacing constraint in TopFarm is based on a required deﬁned radius, that draws
a circle around each wind turbine in which no other wind turbines can be placed. The
spacing constraint varies with the water depth depending on the wind turbines position.
A circular spacing constraint is employed in this thesis, in order to avoid mooring line
crossings of the diﬀerent wind turbines in the wind farm.
The mooring line model developed in this thesis is illustrated in Figure 6.6. The
hybrid mooring lines have a chain part at the top connecting the mooring line to the
==PAGE:96==
78
6 Model Development
ﬂoater then a synthetic ﬁbre follows and a chain part at the seabed at the connection of
the anchor. The propositions of the two diﬀerent materials are based on (Yuan, Incecik,
and Ji, 2014). Yuan, Incecik, and Ji (2014) conducted a study on the motion responses
of a semi-submersible ﬂoater with a hybrid mooring system. The chain length at the
water surface is calculated as a fraction of the ﬂoating mooring length 0.2 ∗ml. The
chain resting on the seabed, eliminating the vertical forces and thereby allowing the use
of DEAs, is assumed to be cg = 150m (Yuan, Incecik, and Ji, 2014).

more accurate presentation of the spacing around the wind turbines would be a ’triangle’
spacing, which still prevents from mooring line crossings of diﬀerent turbines but allows
the turbines to move closer together. Analyzing a triangle spacing is out of scope of the
thesis, but may be of interest for future research.

the Propagate Downwind Model, and a Deﬁcit Wake Model. The Propagate Downwind

any upstream blockage eﬀects and is only valid for far wake (Pederson et al., 2019).
Whereas in the deﬁcit wake model, presented in Figure 6.8b, the wake deﬁcit cause by a

6.1.6
Transmission Losses
The ﬁnal power output of the wind farm is also aﬀected by electrical losses along export
and array cables, in the substation and on all wind turbine generator transformers .
In reality, transmission losses are complex to calculate and depend on many factors:
power input of the transmission lines, temperature, material of the cables, grid frequency,
insulation thickness, rated values and other characteristics (Lundberg, 2003). As many
of these parameters are unknown for this site, the following simpliﬁed loss model is used
to calculate the transmission losses in each cable (Ploss, in W) (Lundberg, 2003). All
constants and cable data are included in Tables F.1 and F.2.
3We chose 8769 hours, because this is widely used in research and thereby in order to be comparable
to other research studies. However, it is noted that some developers use 8766 hours to count for the leap
years occurring every four years

P0, C0: no-load parameters that depend on the cable’s rated voltage
Pk: load parameter that depends on the cable’s rated voltage
lcable: cable length [km]
Sin: cable’s input apparent power [VA]
Sn: cable’s rated apparent power (=

The cable lengths lcable are derived from ORBIT, which assumes a grid layout based
on the average spacing in the layout from TopFarm. This optimization does not take
into account the cable layout of the wind farm, and thus the grid spacing used in ORBIT
is based on the average distance to the four closest turbines. ORBIT then calculates the
according number and length of strings of each cable type accordingly 4. In addition,
ORBIT uses the distance to landfall to calculate the length of the export cables as well
as the number of export cables.
The cable’s input apparent power Sin is the combination of active and reactive power
generated by the wind farm, calculated as Sin = Pgen,cable/cos φ, where cos φ is the power
factor. The presence of reactive power increases transmission losses as well as decreases
grid safety. In order to maintain a high power factor (close to 1), sets of power factor
correction capacitors are often used to maintain the reactive power at low levels. For
wind farms, a power factor > 0.95 is considered high-performance, resulting in power
factors between 0.85 and 0.95 (Alonso and Amarís, 2011). A power factor of 0.85 is
chosen as a conservative estimate.
The generated power is back-calculated from the AEP determined using PyWake by
dividing by the number of hours in a year as Pgen = AEP/8769
The power in each export cable is calculated as the total generated power divided
by the number of export cables Sin,exp = Pgen/(ncables · cos φ).
As the exact array
cable layout is unknown, it is assumed that the shortest cables with a smaller cross
section (185 mm2) (approximately 40 turbines out of 67) carry the load of one turbine
Sin,arr,low = Pgen/(nwt·cos φ) and other array cables carry the load of one sting Sin,arr,low =
Pgen/(nstring · cos φ).
Finally, the total power loss along cables is calculated as the following:

as the sum of the cable losses and this value Lelec = Lelec,cables + Lelec,net.
6.1.7
Other Losses
Other than wake and electrical losses, the losses over the wind farm are assumed to to
be due to environmental losses (Lenv), technical losses (Lt) and availability losses (La).
• Environmental losses include icing, blade soiling, and lightning conditions, although
these are assumed to be rare in CA (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020).
• Technical losses are due to hysteresis (losses due to the control system causing shut
down near cut-out wind speed), parasitic load from onboard equipment and rotor
misalignment.
• Losses due to availability, which are heavily dependent on the the metocean
conditions (wind and wave resource), have been estimated at 5% by NREL for the
Humboldt Call Area and are used for this site (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020).
These three sources of loss are expressed as a percentage of the ﬁnal power output.
Hence the ﬁnal net AEP can be estimated as:
AEPfinal =
AEP
(1 + Lelec + Lenv + Lt + La)
(6.11)
It should be noted that the O&M downtime is omitted from this model as a source
of losses, and should be considered in future iterations.
6.2
Cost Model
LCOE optimization in this thesis is a representation of the trade-oﬀs between CapEx and
AEP. All values in this thesis are expressed in 2019 USD. The NREL ORBIT tool includes
a CapEx model that is integrated with a wind farm layout and allows for optimization.
In the ORBIT model, the following parameters are varied depending on the output from
TopFarm:
• Wind turbine and row spacing: as mentioned above, the average distance to
the four closest turbines is taken as the wind farm spacing in ORBIT, which allows
for calculating array cabling costs.
• Water depth: the average water depth of all turbines in TopFarm.
• Distance to port: calculated as the mean turbine distance to the speciﬁed port
location.
5In this estimation, we assume that array cable lengths are 50% longer than the distance between
turbines. The same cables as used in this thesis are applied.
==PAGE:101==
6.2 Cost Model
83
• Distance to landfall: calculated as the minimum turbine distance to the speciﬁed
point of interconnection, minus the expected distance from landfall to the substation.
The minimum distance is chosen to be representative of the OSS location.
6.2.1
Capital Expenditures
The capital expenditures (CapEx) are calculated using the NREL-developed tool ORBIT.
The balance of system costs, as detailed in the ORBIT documentation, include (National
Renewable Energy Labratory, 2020):
• Installation costs of the wind turbine
• CapEx and installation costs of the substructure (semi-submersible)
• CapEx and installation costs of the array and export cables
• CapEx and installation costs of the mooring system
• CapEx and installation costs of the oﬀshore substation
• Onshore construction costs required to connect the turbine to the grid
• Port fees and commissioning cost
ORBIT is unique in its ability to estimate the costs during the design and installa-
tion phases based on a weather proﬁle and technology choice. This results in a more
integrated CapEx model that reﬂects location and available technology. The CapEx can
be subdivided into the following categories, most of which are calculated in ORBIT:
• Turbine procurement costs: the cost of procuring a turbine (tower and rotor-nacelle
assembly), estimated as 1020 USD per kW for a 15 MW turbine (Musial and Ram,
2010).
• Balance of System CapEx: the costs associated with the wind farm support,
installation, and electrical system.
• Soft CapEx: the costs associated with project insurance, completion, decommis-
sioning, contingency and ﬁnancing.
In integration with TopFarm, the default balance of system values in ORBIT are
used, save for the weather data of Morro Bay, the mooring line system costs, number of
turbines, plant layout (grid), and optimization inputs as described above.
6.2.1.1
Turbine Costs
The turbine costs per kW are estimated from NREL’s 2020 study of the Aqua Ventus
ﬂoating oﬀshore wind turbine and substructure (Musial and Ram, 2010) at 1020 USD/kW
for a 15 MW turbine, a lower cost per kW than lower-rated turbines.
6.2.1.2
Balance of System Costs
The balance of system CapEx is the sum of the System CapEx and the Installation
CapEx, and is one of the outputs of the ORBIT model. The System CapEx is the sum
of the unit costs (either user inputs or results from design phases) multiplied by the
conﬁgured number of turbines, cable section lengths or other appropriate unit. This
==PAGE:102==
84
6 Model Development
output does not change based on the installation simulation. Installation CapEx is a
dynamic result based on the installation simulation and is based on the times associated
with each subsystem installation, day rates of any vessels/ports and accrued weather
delays. Further details and process diagrams for the System and Installation Phases in
ORBIT can be viewed in the Appendix F.
The CapEx associated with the design and installation of a ﬂoating wind farm are
often similar to those associated with ﬁxed bottom wind farms, with some signiﬁcant
diﬀerences.
• Floating platform:
the cost of the semi-submersible, based on the original
Balance of System model from ORBIT (Maness, Maples, and Smith, 2017).
– Cost: The cost of the substructure includes the cost of the stiﬀened column,
truss structure, heavy plate, secondary steel, and towing speed. All of these
values are assumed to be the defaults set in ORBIT, all given in USD/t. The
associated masses are directly calculated from the turbine rating.
– Installation: In ORBIT the moored substructure installation encompasses
both the quayside assembly of the substructure and turbine, as well as the
substructure tow-out and assembly. Most time values, including that for
substructure assembly, turbine assembly steps, mechanical veriﬁcation, and
connection of mooring lines are set to default values (per turbine). The tow-out
speed is calculated based on distance to port and weather conditions.
• Wind turbine installation: in the case of ﬂoating turbines, a single lift instal-
lation is proposed, in which the turbine is assembled onshore and placed on the
ﬂoater.
• Mooring and anchoring systems: The mooring system costs in ORBIT have
been modiﬁed to reﬂect a semi-taut mooring system as described in Section 5.2.5.
– Design: The cost of mooring lines depends on their mass, length, and unit
cost, and the cost of anchors depend on their type and mass. The costs
assumed in this thesis are shown in Table F.3 in the Appendix F.
– Installation:
The cost of installing mooring and anchoring systems, is
imported from oil and gas industry knowledge.
In the case of catenary
mooring lines and drag anchors, the installation procedure includes embedding
anchors, and attaching mooring lines to the semi-submersible
• Oﬀshore Substation: The OSS is sized to the farm capacity, and assumed to be
ﬁxed bottom with monopile foundations. This is clearly an inaccurate assumption,
as the OSS would in reality also need to be constructed with a ﬂoating foundation.
Future iterations of ORBIT promise to include a ﬂoating OSS model. Therefore,
it has to be noted that the CapEx for the OSS in this thesis has no bias but is
introduced with a large uncertainty. Because, by this time no ﬂoating OSS has yet
been commissioned.
==PAGE:103==
6.3 Optimization Algorithm
85
6.2.1.3
Soft CapEx
The Soft CapEx represents additional project level costs associated with commissioning,
decommissioning and ﬁnancing of the project. The default cost factors for these categories
are derived from the 2018 Cost of Wind Energy Review (Stehly and Beiter, 2019).
The decommissioning cost is related to the ﬁnal stage of a wind plant lifetime, in
which all components are dismantled. For ﬂoating wind farms, it is determined that
decommissioning costs are lower than for ﬁxed-bottom oﬀshore wind, due to a lower impact
on the seabed (Walter Musial, Beiter, and Nunemaker, 2020). The decommissioning
costs are included in the ORBIT package as part of the Soft CapEx.
6.2.2
Operation and Maintenance Expenditures
The OpEx for a wind farm are annual costs that occur over the lifetime of the wind farm,
representing the sum of the cost of seabed rental, insurance, and grid access fees. The
maintenance expenditures on a wind farm are the sum of corrective and preventative
maintenance on a wind farm, otherwise thought of as planned and unplanned maintenance.
Optimization for maintenance strategy – i.e. the frequency of preventative maintenance,
might be performed in a further study, as these costs are anticipated to change with
ﬂoating oﬀshore wind. In addition, there are also some indirect costs associated with
maintenance expenditures, including the costs to guarantee repair services.
For the purposes of this thesis, the operation and maintenance (O&M) costs have
been assumed to be the assumptions set by NREL in their 2020 study on the cost of
oﬀshore wind (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020), speciﬁcally the value used for 2022 of
62 USD/kW-year. These values have been selected for their compatibility with results
derived using ORBIT. A uncertainty study is conducted in Section 7.5.0.2 on the LCOE
of the wind farm through adjusting the OpEx cost, demonstrating the impact of OpEx
on the overall LCOE.
6.3
Optimization Algorithm
The aim of the optimization is to ﬁnd a minimized LCOE by moving the wind turbines in
the design space. Depending on the classiﬁcation and thereby computational complexity
of a problem, the optimization algorithm is chosen (Herbert-Acero et al., 2014). Therefore,
before evaluating the choice of optimizer in the diﬀerent cases in this thesis a brief overview
of optimization algorithms is given, followed by the optimization procedure followed for
each case.
6.3.1
Wind Farm Layout Optimization
Common used algorithms for large-scale oﬀshore wind farm optimization as stated by
Hou et al. (2019) are heuristic algorithms like Genetic Algorithms (GA). However, a
new trend shows the adaption of gradient-based or hybrid optimization methods (Hou
et al., 2019). In a hybrid model, a heuristic approach is applied to ﬁnd the initial layout,
==PAGE:104==
86
6 Model Development
followed by a local search algorithm to obtain the optimal solution (Hou et al., 2019).
Tesauro, Réthoré, and Larsen (2012) called this a "two step strategy", ﬁrst applying a
meta-heuristic method for a global exploration of the design space, followed by a heuristic
algorithm reﬁning the solution by a local exploration. Another promising approach is a
"multi-ﬁdelity approach" applying diﬀerent algorithms, proposed by Tesauro, Réthoré,
and Larsen (2012).
6.3.2
Gradient-based and Gradient-free Optimizers
Gradient Based Methods use derivatives of the objective function to explore the
design space and ﬁnd an improved design. Gradient-based techniques are local search
methods, so they can only ﬁnd local minima or maxima. Hence, in a complex search
space the algorithm may converge to quickly in a local optimum but not a global one.
In order to ﬁnd the global optimum, the initial positions must be close to the optimum
(Tesauro, Réthoré, and Larsen, 2012). A possibble solution to overcome the limitations
Herbert-Acero et al. (2014) propose to additionally apply a meta-heuristic method to
ﬁnd the best neighbor solution.
Meta-heuristic Methods are gradient-free algorithms such as the GE or the Particle
Swarm Optimizer (PSO). These algorithms are "nature-inspired" and follow the principle
of the evolution theory, mixing solutions that give birth to new ones or mutating oﬀspring
solutions. An advantage of these is a more global search of the design space. But
this more comprehensive exploration of the design space, also introduces a drawback
namely a much slower convergence, especially as the number of design variables increase
(Herbert-Acero et al., 2014). These types of optimizers were tested in this thesis but
were not eventually used do to eﬃciency reasons.
Gradient-free Heuristic Algorithms are following semi-empirical rules to ﬁnd
near-optimal problem solutions. Random Search (RS) is a basic heuristic algorithm, which
randomly ﬁnds diﬀerent feasible solutions and determines the best possible optimum
from the sample (Herbert-Acero et al., 2014).
6.3.3
Choice of Optimization Procedure
6.3.3.1
Base Case
For the base case, a process that utilizes multiple optimizer types has been chosen in
order to more thoroughly explore the design space.
First, the initial positions are set to a grid in which most turbines are in inside the
boundary. SLSQP, a gradient-based optimizer, is next used. Through iteration, SLSQP
consistently converges once all turbines have moved within the boundary and satisfy the
minimum spacing requirement. Hence, SLSQP is used once to set the initial positions
for all further optimization.
Next, a gradient-free method, namely a RS is utilized to more thoroughly explore
the design space. It should be noted that this algorithm is not run to convergence to
save computational time. It is noted that this gradient-free method often places turbines
outside the boundary in its search.
==PAGE:105==
6.3 Optimization Algorithm
87
Next, SLSQP is utilized to again bring turbines outside the boundary back into feasible
positions. Finally, COBYLA, a gradient-free algorithm, is iterated until convergence for
local optimization of turbines to ﬁnd an optimum layout.
Table 6.2: Final optimization procedure used in the base case.

The process of determining this algorithm was iterative. Multiple optimizers were
tested, including meta-heuristic gradient free methods. These types of optimizers were
not found to converge within any reasonable time window. In addition, a process of
alternating gradient-free and gradient-based methods (RS and SLSQP) was attempted yet
resulting in negligible to non-existent improvements to LCOE (all under 0.1 USD/MWh).
Thereby the method presented above demonstrates a method that results in a minimized
LCOE with reasonable computational time for the scope of this thesis.
6.3.3.2
Scenario 1: Regular Optimization and Constrained
Irregular Optimization
Through consultation with a leading wind energy layout engineer (Wind Farm Designer
(RWE Renewables), 2021), it was determined that the optimizers compatible with
TopFarm are not suﬃcient for a regular optimization, and favor a sweep over the limited
design variables. The two methods share the following design variables: the x and y
position of the southwest-most turbine (x0, y0), variable regular spacing and orientation
angle. The range of each design variable is pre-determined by the Call Area position and
the direction of the prevailing wind speed.

6.3.3.3
Scenario 2: Maximum Number of Turbines in
DOD-proposed Areas
In the second scenario the SLSQP algorithm is applied.
SLSQP, a gradient-based
optimizer, consistently converges once all turbines have moved within the boundary and
satisfy the minimum spacing requirement. In the case it is the only algorithm applied,
as this optimization aims to ﬁnd the maximum number of turbines that can be placed
within the boundary. Therefore, there is no further room for improved and no other
algorithm is applied.
6.4
Model Validation
The ORBIT, PyWake, and LCOE models are then validated with a sensitivity analysis.
In this analysis, 16 turbines are placed in a 4 x 4 grid, oriented towards the prevailing
wind direction. This is achieved through a sweep of orientation angles, demonstrating an
optimal angle of -7.8◦. This value is validated later in the grid optimization.
This section will highlight the sensitivity to depth, which impacts the mooring line
length and thereby the wind turbine spacing. Sensitivities concerning layout, number of
turbines, distance to port and landfall, and weather window are included in Appendix G.
6.4.1
Depth Sensitivities
As discussed above, the mooring line footprint, and thereby the minimum spacing is
dependent on the depth. In this case, the average depth of the wind farm is varied from
600 m to 1300 m, the depth range of Morro Bay. Both row and column spacing are
then varied according to the mooring footprint Equation 6.7. Diﬀerent values of the
attachment angle α are tested in order to view sensitivities. Figure 6.9 demonstrates
the linear relationship between depth and minimum spacing, deﬁned as two times the
mooring footprint .
==PAGE:107==
6.4 Model Validation
89
Figure 6.9: Attachment Angle α Sensitivities
6.4.1.1
AEP, CapEx, LCOE
In this sensitivity, the regular spacing between turbines is set to the minimum spacing as
deﬁned by the mooring footprint. AEP is thus impacted by this spacing. It is observed
that as the grid spacing increases, the AEP increases accordingly, with negative concavity
indicating that eventually as spacing increases, the AEP will reach a steady maximum.
As expected, the AEP increases with α, as footprint increases and thereby wake losses
decrease.
It is observed in the CapEx sensitivities, that as depth increases, the overall CapEx
increases linearly. This behavior is expected as depth corresponds to an increase in
mooring line length and dynamic cable length. In addition, as previously examined,
==PAGE:108==
90
6 Model Development
there is a linear relationship between spacing and CapEx. Hence, the addition of two
linear functions gives this linear relationship. As expected, the CapEx increases with α,
as footprint increases and array cables and mooring lines are longer. In addition, the
slope of the CapEx curve slightly increases, which directly follows from the relationship
between depth and spacing.
Finally, the LCOE plot demonstrates that LCOE is foremost driven by AEP – the
trade-oﬀbetween inter-turbine spacing and depth favors greater spacing over CapEx
savings due to depth. In addition, the curves demonstrates that for each value of α there
is an absolute minimum for LCOE. It is demonstrated that the depth value associated
with this optimum increases as α decreases. This shows that, especially at low depth
(and smaller spacing), LCOE is driven by AEP. Yet at further depths and distances
between turbines, where there are limited wake eﬀects, the LCOE for larger attachment
angle becomes higher than that of lower angles, driven by CapEx.
6.4.1.2
CapEx Breakdown
ORBIT allows for a detailed CapEx analysis into the wind turbine CapEx, Mooring
Installation and System, Substructure Installation and System, Array and Export Cable
Installation and System, and OSS Installation and System CapEx.
As expected, the wind turbine and substructure-related CapEx ﬁgures in Appendix G
are constant on a USD/kW basis, as the depth does not alter the turbine or substructure
design of choice in this model. Similarly, the OSS system CapEx is not impacted by the
depth.
All other costs increase linearly with depth – the Mooring System and Array System
CapEx increasing the most drastically with depth. This is expected, as increases in
depth linearly corresponds to mooring line length (demonstrated above). The cost of
installation of array, export, mooring, and OSS increase with depth, but have less of an
impact on the overall CapEx. In addition, there is a small jump around 1000 m depth
in the mooring installation cost. This is assumed to be due to ORBIT’s installation
assumptions, which impose a limit on the amount of mooring line length that can be
held on an installation vessel. In reality, this curve would be smooth.
6.4.2
Column / Wind Turbine Spacing
The spacing between turbines perpendicular to the wind direction was varied from 5D to
30D spacing. First, the CapEx in USD/kW installed capacity is calculated using ORBIT
based on the distance between wind turbines. It is demonstrated that as column spacing
increases, the CapEx increases linearly with a slope of approximately 5 USD/kW per
multiple of the diameter. This is due directly to the linear increase in array cabling costs,
as well as installation time (a much less signiﬁcant impact).
Next the AEP as a function of wind turbine spacing is simulated using PyWake. It
is demonstrated in the Appendix G that at low spacing, there is noticeable wake loss,
where at high spacing the AEP stabilizes to only include the wake losses of turbines in
alternating rows.
==PAGE:109==
6.4 Model Validation
91
Finally, the LCOE simulation demonstrates a nontrivial global minimum for LCOE
around a spacing of 14D. This trade-oﬀis clearly observed in the AEP graph, which
demonstrates that the wake loss begins to stabilize around the same point. In addition,
as turbine spacing has a signiﬁcant impact on cable losses, longer distances between
turbines result in higher LCOE values. It should be noted that the LCOE range varies
1.2 USD/MWh, a range smaller than many other sensitivities seen here.
Figure 6.10: LCOE Wind Turbine Spacing
6.4.3
Row Spacing
The spacing between turbines perpendicular to the wind direction was varied from 5D to
30D spacing. First, the CapEx in USD/kW installed capacity is calculated using ORBIT
based on the distance between rows. It is demonstrated that as row spacing increases,
the CapEx increases linearly with a slope of approximately 5 USD/kW per multiple of
the diameter. This is due directly to the linear increase in array cabling costs, as well as
installation time (a much less signiﬁcant impact). Row spacing has less of an impact on
CapEx than column spacing, as ORBIT assumes that array strings are connected within
rows. Hence, increasing row spacing only increases the cable length between the OSS
and the ﬁrst turbine in each string. It is noted that there is some non-linearity at low
spacing, indicating that there is a minimum cabling cost.
Next the AEP as a function of row spacing is simulated using PyWake.
It is
demonstrated in the Appendix G that at low spacing, there is noticeable wake loss,
where at higher spacing the AEP stabilizes to only include the wake losses of turbines
in alternating rows. Finally, the LCOE simulation demonstrates a nontrivial global
minimum for LCOE around a spacing of 20D – this trade-oﬀis clearly observed in the
AEP graph, which demonstrates that the wake loss begins to stabilize around the same
point. It should be noted that the LCOE range only varies 1.5 USD/MWh.
==PAGE:110==
92
6 Model Development
Figure 6.11: LCOE Row Spacing
6.5
Distance Sensitivities
Next, the distance to port and landfall are tested in ORBIT. This calculation does not
impact the layout of the wind farm.
6.5.1
Distance to Landfall
The distance to landfall determines the length of the export cable, and drives electrical
losses to the net annual energy production. This impact is seen in the AEP, although
the diﬀerence losses between 50 and 150 km are less than 1% of the overall AEP.
The distance to landfall directly scales with the system and installation costs of the
export cable. Hence, a linear trend is observed with the distance to landfall, increasing
55 USD/kW per m. The two points on interconnection of interest, Morro Bay and Diablo
Canyon power plants are located approximately 92 and 98 km from the port, indicating
that a closer point of interconnection would marginally improve the overall CapEx. As
the electrical losses are proportionally small in the AEP calculation, the LCOE is driven
by the cost of the export cable, demonstrating a fairly linear trend with distance to
landfall – with 0.05 USD/MWh added per km.
6.5.2
Distance to Installation Port
The distance to the installation port drives the installation costs associated with the
wind farm, as longer trips to shore require longer weather windows, corresponding to
longer wait times, as well as higher costs associated with using installation vessels. A
large range of distances to port are tested, as a worst-case scenario would require the use
of a port in Humbolt Bay, 15000 km from the site. As the layout of the wind farm does
not change in this sensitivity, the LCOE scales with CapEx demonstrating linear trend –
with 0.02 USD/MWh added per km.
==PAGE:111==
6.5 Distance Sensitivities
93
6.5.3
Number of Turbines
The number of turbines, which is altered in the third scenario, presents a view into the
assumptions made by ORBIT. The AEP per MW installed decreases with the number of
turbines as strings are added, contributing to wake losses.
A CapEx breakdown in ORBIT is used to examine the costs of adding turbines.
The overall CapEx per MW has a decreasing trend as the number of turbines increase,
which is expected with economies of scale, yet CapEx curve is not smooth, indicating
discreteness in the ORBIT model. ORBIT allows for a detailed CapEx analysis into
the wind turbine CapEx, Mooring Installation and System, Substructure Installation
and System, Array and Export Cable Installation and System, and OSS Installation
and System CapEx. It is observed that the wind turbine, mooring, and substructure
system unit CapEx are constant with the addition of new turbines, as would be expected
with a constant number of mooring lines per turbine. The unit cost associated with the
installation of the substructure, array system and OSS decrease with increased turbine
numbers, as would be expected in economies of scale.
The other CapEx inputs demonstrate discreteness that is examined in the ORBIT
documentation. The export cable system demonstrates a general decreasing trend with
sharp spikes each time an export cable is added. This is calculated in ORBIT using the
capacity of the wind farm and the export cable voltage. Each time an export cable is
added there is a corresponding spike in installation costs, although less dramatic. The
cost of mooring installation shows a general decreasing trend, but smaller increases in
costs each time a new vessel needs to be deployed (this is determined by mooring line
mass). Finally, the array cable installation cost demonstrates an increasing trend, with
spikes according to the addition of new strings as well as switching between types of
array cables (as both 185 mm and 630 mm 66 kV cables are employed).
==PAGE:112==
94
6 Model Development
(a) Export Cable System
(b) Mooring Installation
(c) Array Cable System
Figure 6.12: CapEx sensitivity
It should be noted that the CapEx values associated with the OSS are not accurate –
there is not a capability in ORBIT to model a ﬂoating OSS. Regardless, the associated
system and installation costs demonstrate expected trends with additional turbines.
Finally, the LCOE appears to decrease with number of turbines, with a sharp initial
decrease and lower decreases at higher turbine numbers. This leveling oﬀis likely due to
the wake losses seen in the AEP calculation. Discrete trends present in the CapEx are
still present in the LCOE, as expected.
Figure 6.13: LCOE sensitivity for the number of wind turbines
==PAGE:113==
6.6 Weather Window
95
6.6
Weather Window
ORBIT allows for the input of weather data into the cost model. The model thus
calculates the weather window needed for an installation procedure. In order for a
representative model, wind and wave data from 2011 through 2019 from the National
Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration are averaged on a monthly basis (National
Data Buoy Centre and National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration’s, 2021). It
has to be noted that the wind speeds presented in the ﬁgure below are sampled at the
water surface and are therefore lower as in Figure 2.4a. Which represent the wind speeds
at the turbine hub height. The diﬀerent wind speeds at diﬀerent heights is traced back
to the wind shear.
(a) LCOE
(b) Average Annual Weather
Figure 6.14: Weather Window a) LCOE b) average annual weather
Points throughout the ﬁrst few years of the data set were selected as start points in
order to determine the eﬀect of weather on CapEx. As might be expected, installation
that begins in the spring an summer demonstrate a consistently low CapEx, with autumn
and winter installation resulting in higher CapEx values, shown in Figure 6.14a. The
reason for this trend can be seen in the plot above – low wind speeds and wave heights
in the spring and summer allow for longer weather windows where the turbines can
be installed. It should be noted that if the weather is ignored, the resulting CapEx is
equivalent to the results generated with summer and spring start dates.

CHAPTER 7
Results and Analysis
The following section presents the results and discussion of the optimization for each case
implemented. Major components of LCOE, including CapEx and the net capacity factor,
as well as relative metrics including depth, distance to port, and landfall, are included to
support the analysis and examine their inﬂuence on the LCOE.
7.1
Base Case
The Figures 7.1, 7.2, 7.3 and 7.4 below demonstrate the optimized layouts for each of
the four base cases – the "vertical" (V1 and V2) and "horizontal" partitions (H1 and H2).
Included in these diagrams are the wind farm boundary, including a buﬀer for mooring
line footprint, as well as the depth-dependent spacing constraint, shown as dotted lines
around the colored dots, which represent the wind turbines.

From the Figures 7.1, 7.2, 7.3 and 7.4 above it is observed that in all cases the
spacing and perimeter constraints are successfully implemented and active – minimum
spacing diameter decreases with depth (which decreases in the east), and all turbines
are contained within the wind farm boundary. It should be noted that these layouts are
not tightly packed – there is clearly free space for turbines to rearrange to ﬁnd optimal
positions.
In addition, in all cases, the turbines are more heavily concentrated towards the east,
as might be expected to minimize the distance to landfall and thereby the length of the
export cable.

can be observed, by comparison, that the V1 area has the lowest LCOE, followed by the

7 Results and Analysis
As the wind direction is relatively uniform and all areas have similar power densities
(Table 7.12), the capacity factor for all sites is near uniform. It is noted that the capacity
factor calculated in TopFarm is lower than that assumed by NREL with 46.5%1 (Beiter,
Walt Musial, et al., 2020). It can be viewed in Table 7.1 above that site V1 has the
lowest values for depth as well as distance to landfall, but has a high value for distance to
the port, indicating that changes in CapEx (and thereby LCOE) are more inﬂuenced by
water depth (which drives minimum spacing), as well as the distance to landfall, which
dictates the length of the export cable. As following, site H1 also has a smaller depth
and distance to landfall than V2 and H2, resulting in lower LCOE values.
The calculated CapEx for all sites are compared to the value NREL estimates for
development in 2022 of 3822 Million USD (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020). The lower
values for the CapEx are attributed to the wind turbine CapEx. NREL assumes a 10
MW wind turbine for development in 2022 whereas in this study a 15 MW wind turbine
is assumed. In their study Beiter, Walt Musial, et al. (2020) calculate an LCOE for the
Morro Bay Call Area of 112 USD/MWh (development in 2019) and 90 USD/MWh (for
development in 2022). The resulting LCOE values for the bases cases are positioned
between the NREL values. As follows, are representative for development in 2021.
Table 7.2: LCOE values for Oﬀshore wind farms in the US (Ministry of Foreign Aﬀairs

Table 7.2 lists public available LCOE values for the ﬁrst build oﬀshore wind farm in
the US and tender values for four diﬀerent oﬀshore wind projects awarded in the past. It
is noted that the LCOE for the Block Island wind farm is extremely high compared to the
others, due to it being the ﬁrst oﬀshore wind farm build in the US. Moreover, the supply
chain for oﬀshore wind in the US is yet not fully developed, which drives the LCOE
to increase. (Ministry of Foreign Aﬀairs of Denmark - The Trade Council, 2020). A
comparison of the LCOE values in this thesis with that of the New York tender indicates
higher values due to relative technology immaturity of ﬂoating wind as a technology.

7.1.1.1
Point of Interconnection Location
An optimization is run for an alternate substation location: the Diablo Canyon Substation.
This substation is located 18 km to the southeast of the Morro Bay Substation, resulting
in further distances between the OSS and the point of interconnection (POI), resulting
in a longer export cable, thereby increasing the costs and electrical losses. The distance
to landfall is calculated from the closest wind turbine to the POI – the wind farms
with more southern and eastern portions (V1 and H1) are close to equidistant to both
substations, resulting in similar LCOE values. More northern and western sites (V2 and
H2) see more substantial increases in the distance to landfall.
It is observed that for all cases except for H1 (the southernmost case) the LCOE
increases with the change in the substation location. This is particularly dramatic in the
case of H2, which is the northernmost area chosen.

7.2
Stakeholder-motivated Scenario 1: Regular
grid spacing
Next, we examine the stakeholder-motivated scenarios that allow us to determine the
impact of key players in the construction of a wind farm site and layout.
First, we examine the impact of a regular optimization (as expected to be required
by the USCG) versus the results of an irregular optimization.

7 Results and Analysis
Figure 7.5a above demonstrates that when the grid is constrained to a ’rectangle[,
the spacing is limited by the perimeter of the wind farm – the spacing between turbines
is set to approximately 9D. Figure 7.5b demonstrates that the location that minimizes
LCOE is the southeast, where the depths are limited and the distances to the installation
port and landfall are minimized. In both cases, the spacing is wider than the minimum
spacing previously recommended by the USCG (1 NM = 7.7D).
It is observed in Table 7.6 that the two solutions converge to diﬀerent orientation
angles and spacing values, which is surprising given the uniform wind direction – this
is likely due in the case of Method 1 to the constraints that the wind farm should
be rectangular and ﬁt inside the Call Area perimeter. The second method is not as
constrained, allowing the wind farm to expand its footprint and rotate to a more optimal
orientation angle.
Next, it is of interest to determine whether an irregular layout in these areas brings
signiﬁcant LCOE gains, as "generally, most of the recently published works for WT
micro-siting have been done based on a coordinate model, which has been demonstrated
to be a better model than the grid model. The reason is that a coordinate model allows
the WTs to move within a continuous domain which provides more potential solutions"
(Hou et al., 2019).
Next, we compare the LCOE values for a grid optimization versus an irregular
optimization within the same space as the grid optimizations presented in the previous
section. It is noted that the grid optimizations were completed with a ’poor man’s
optimization’ method, which allows for a brute-force determination of a minimum LCOE
value. This is signiﬁcantly more computationally intensive than optimization algorithms
used in TopFarm and results in perhaps a more global optimum with the given constraints.
The LCOE values for regular optimization cases have been calculated both with the
assumption of 45◦and 30◦mooring line attachment angles to demonstrate the value of
irregular layouts in both cases.

The ﬁrst grid optimization scenario represents a highly constrained layout. In this
scenario, the power density is approximately 3 MW/km2, the benchmark used for most
wind farms in the US (Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020). A signiﬁcant ﬁnding, in this
case, is that with a larger mooring footprint, there is little to no room for the wind farm
to optimize its layout. In the case of irregular optimization, it is highly improbable that
the algorithm will settle upon a uniform layout. In this case, the irregular layout found
that satisﬁed all constraints gave a slightly lower AEP than the regular layout, resulting
in a higher LCOE.
In addition, it can be viewed that when constrained to a smaller footprint, reduction
in the mooring line attachment angle allow for signiﬁcant improvements in both regular
and irregular optimization, as turbines have more freedom to locate to more optimal
positions. It is noted that in this case, there is very little movement of turbines in
irregular optimization, a reﬂection of the highly-constrained nature of the problem.

The second grid optimization scenario oﬀers insights into the value of irregular layouts
in an area with a uniform wind rose. First, it is observed that the irregular layout, which

those in the base case, demonstrating the value of searching the entire Call Area when
determining an optimal site.
==PAGE:125==
7.3 Stakeholder-motivated Scenario 2: DOD-proposed Areas
107
Multiple optimization trials were run in this case with the result that a grid layout is
a close approximation of the optimal layout – layout improvements are only made with
small adjustments to the original grid layout. When turbines rearranged dramatically,
the LCOE demonstrates sizable increases. Hence, it is observed that like the previous
case, a grid layout is a close approximation of the optimal layout – layout improvements
are only made with small adjustments to the original grid layout.
In addition, the introduction of a 30◦mooring line attachment angle allows for
further improvement of the LCOE – in this optimization, turbines move slightly from
the optimized irregular positions to further improve the AEP and hence the LCOE.
7.3
Stakeholder-motivated Scenario 2:
DOD-proposed Areas
Second, we examine stakeholder impacts on the site, namely the location and size of the
wind farm site. In this case, the DOD proposed two alternative areas, shown in Figure
B.2b. The impacts on the potential capacity and the economics are analyzed. The two
alternative sites are optimized for the maximum number of turbines that can be placed
within the site boundaries.
(a) DOD North
(b) DOD South
Figure 7.8: DOD North and South alternative areas
Figure 7.8 above shows the results for the DOD North and South site. The DOD
North site has space 69 wind turbines and the DOD South site only for 52 wind turbines,
resulting in a potential capacity of 1035 MW in the DOD North area and 780 MW in
the South area. Hence, only the alternative DOD North area provides enough space for
a 1 GW wind farm.
However, the DOD North area results in a higher LCOE, shown in Table 7.10,
compared to the once achieved in the diﬀerent base cases, which are optimized for 67
wind turbines. This referred to the longer distance to port as well as interconnection.

Table 7.10: Scenario 2 Overview
Table 7.10 shows that the DOD North area results in a higher LCOE compared to the
once achieved in all base cases. The DOD North scenario includes more turbines, which
might be expected to drive down LCOE (see Section 6.4), but is further from port and
landfall, increasing installation and export cable costs. In addition, higher mean water
depth increases the mooring line lengths and thereby associated costs. The capacity
factors are also slightly lower than the base case, which is expected due to increased wake
losses – although turbines are placed on average closer together, this diﬀerence is not
large due to a high minimum spacing driven by water depth. Hence, it can be concluded
that the LCOE, in this case, is driven by an increase in CapEx and a decrease in AEP.
Conversely, the South site has the lowest mean water depth with 895 m of all the
diﬀerent cases analyzed. The low water depths have an inﬂuence on the CapEx as well
as the inter-turbine spacing. The inter-turbine spacing decreases and thereby allows
the turbines to move closer, increasing the maximum number of turbines feasible in the
site, corresponding to a high power density of 4.0 (presented in Table 7.12). Moreover,
a shorter distance to landfall decreases the CapEx. However, due to the smaller inter-
turbine spacing, higher wake losses cause a slight decrease in capacity factor. In the
DOD South area, fewer turbines, as well as high wake losses, drive the LCOE to higher
values than seen in the base case.
