7.4.1
Wind Farm Site
In constructing a bid for a wind farm lease, the developer must ﬁrst choose a site within
the Call Area. From analysis, sites closer to shore and further southeast (in this case, V1,
H1, and the optimized regular layout) appear desirable both for distance to potential grid
connection as well as for depth. Decreased distance to landfall brings lower export cable
costs and electrical losses, and reduced distance to shore decreases the weather windows
needed for installation. The mean water depth determines the length of mooring lines,
array cable length (in this case), and the minimum spacing.
The trend towards shore is further demonstrated in the proposed DOD area – the
DOD North area demonstrates deep waters and far distances to landfall (resulting in high
LCOE value), whereas the South area is characterized by low depth and close distance
to landfall, reducing the LCOE.
Next, it is observed that the location of the installation port and its distance to the
chosen site plays a role, albeit a limited one with the averaged wind and wave data.
Although our results indicate relatively low sensitivity to port location, for an installation
port to the south such as Heuneme the closest sites such as H1 and V1 are preferable,
and for a northern port like San Francisco V1 and H2 are more preferable.
7.4.2
Wind Farm Layout Design
Results indicate that due to the uniform wind rose, a regular layout may prove comparable
to irregular layouts in terms of LCOE, as seen in the comparison of the V1 base case to
the second regular layout designed2. A regular layout may bring further techno-economic
advantages, such as the use of shared anchors, as well as cooperation with stakeholders.
The success of a simple swept grid optimization thereby demonstrates that irregular
2Base case V1 and the second optimized regular layout are chosen as these sites are similarly located
and have similar power densities 7.12.
==PAGE:129==
7.5 Sensitivities on Grid Optimization Result
111
optimization may not actually need to be applied by a developer in the Case of Morro
Bay, regardless of recommendation by the USCG. It should also be noted that the swept
optimization used in this scenario identiﬁes a global optimum, where likely many wind
turbine positions found in irregular optimization settle in local optimal values. Hence,
regular optimization indicates that the factors then that drive LCOE are the site of the
wind farm, the impact of wind farm spacing and the mooing line footprint.
Regular layouts designed in Scenario 1 demonstrate that the optimal wind farm
spacing results in a power density greater than what is conventional (3 MW/km2). Hence,
wind farm developers may be encouraged to pursue the widest spacing achievable within
the lease area. The inverse eﬀect of spacing on the wind farm LCOE is observed in
scenarios that reﬂect the impact of developing in the sites proposed by the DOD. In
these cases, although the number of turbines is similar, the restricted space drives the
LCOE to higher values than seen for other areas – for example, comparing the LCOE of
DOD North and H2, which are in similar locations/ but with diﬀerent power densities.
The nuanced trade-oﬀbetween LCOE and the number of turbines given a set area is
not explored in-depth in this thesis due to limitations/discrete values in ORBIT. It is
noted that there are many other factors not considered in this thesis that contribute to
limiting the spacing between turbines, including the electrical system layout.
Moreover, it is observed that the impact of the attachment angle α plays a crucial
role in such deep waters, as it inﬂuences both the mooring line material cost and spacing
between turbines.
In such deep waters, an attachment angle change of 15◦has a
signiﬁcant impact. The decreased minimum spacing between wind turbines thereby
increases the available area in which tubines can be located. However, hydrodynamic
forces induced by the waves (out of the scope of this thesis) should be considered for the
optimal mooring design, to evaluate the optimal attachment angle and line length.
As mentioned earlier, it is recognized that wake shadow eﬀects from other farms in
the Call Area, as well as the location of a shared OSS, may play a signiﬁcant role in the
choice of site and layout. These possibilities are outside the scope of this research.
7.5
Sensitivities on Grid Optimization Result
Given the results of optimization, the ﬁnal grid layout using Method 2 demonstrates a
near-optimal LCOE that satisﬁes the expected recommendations of the US Coast Guard.
Hence, this area demonstrates a near-feasible case for the area (it might be argued as the
power density is less than 3 MW/km2 that this case is less than realistic), it is chosen as
a base case for further sensitivity analysis.
7.5.0.1
Cost Breakdown
A breakdown of the total cost understood as the combination of the CapEx and discounted
OpEx, is included in Figure 7.9 below and H.1 in Appendix H.

Figure 7.9: Overall CapEx Breakdown
From this chart, it is observed that the largest expenses are the wind turbines and
the substructures, which provide the most signiﬁcant opportunity for cost reduction with
innovation. Notably, the cost of the mooring system (including installation) constitutes
9% of the expenses, demonstrating the size of the opportunity for optimizing mooring
lines. The Soft CapEx constitutes construction insurance and ﬁnancing, contingency,
commissioning, and decommissioning costs, included in Table H.1.
It is noted that the cost of the oﬀshore substation constitutes only 3% of the overall
cost, demonstrating that modeling the OSS as ﬁxed-bottom does not have a signiﬁcant
impact on the LCOE.
Finally, the discounted OpEx ﬁgure constitutes 19% of the discounted costs, which is
consistent with existing literature in the ﬁeld (Maienza et al., 2020).
7.5.0.2
Uncertainty Analysis
The following sensitivities on key parameters are varied to view their impact on LCOE.
These values are represented Figure 7.10 below.
• CapEx: As demonstrated above, the cost model is highly reliant on projected cost
parameters, especially for novel turbine technology, substructure type, mooring
technology, and dynamic cabling. In addition, there may be large unforeseen costs
associated with port infrastructure that have not yet been included in the model.
Hence, the CapEx was varied 10% to reﬂect these potential savings or expenses.
• OpEx: The OpEx, which is determined using an assumption of 62 USD/kW/year
is explored with a higher sensitivity range of 25% uncertainty. Factors that may
alter the OpEx cost include the location of the operations port, as well as the costs
associated with hiring vessels and crews that can only be broadly estimated.
• Mean wind speed: Often to ensure bankability, P75 and P90 wind speeds
(instead of P50) are used by ﬁnancial institutions to avoid risk. In addition, the
AEP generated by PyWake is tied to signiﬁcant uncertainty. AEP uncertainty from
==PAGE:131==
7.5 Sensitivities on Grid Optimization Result
113
the Global Wind Atlas can be estimated between 9 - 15% uncertainty (Witha et al.,
2019), and the Bastankah Gaussian wake model has been predicted to demonstrate
a RMSE of approximately 4% at a distance of 7D (Schreiber, Balbaa, and Bottasso,
2019). As PyWake calculates the AEP given the Weibull distribution, the average
wind speed is varied 10% in the calculation of AEP.
• WACC: The nominal WACC chosen for this thesis is 5.4%, is based on current
ﬁnancing for ﬁxed bottom technology in the United States. As for those, the
ﬁnancing of ﬂoating oﬀshore wind might be more apprehensive of less proven
technology (Weber, 2020). Hence, a sensitivity range of 10% is chosen, giving a
range of WACC values between 4.9% and 5.9%.
• Lifteime: Most studies assume 25 or 30 years Beiter, Walt Musial, et al., 2020, but
increased or decreased unexpected damage on the wind farm can shorten or extend
its lifetime signiﬁcantly. Hence, a lifetime range of 25 to 35 years is demonstrated
as a sensitivity.
• Installation Time: It is assumed that installation will take between one and
three years (Lacal-Arántegui, Yusta, and Domínguez-Navarro, 2018).
Figure 7.10: LCOE Uncertainties
The Figure 7.10 above demonstrates that even slight changes in the mean wind speed
of the site result in a large shift in a wind farm’s proﬁtability. Next, the CapEx is a
signiﬁcant driver of LCOE, demonstrating that driving down the costs of components
and installation. In addition, the lifetime of the wind farm has a signiﬁcant impact on the
LCOE (with more losses in reduction of the lifetime than gains in increasing the lifetime),
indicating that developments to prolong the years of generation through improving the
design to limit fatigue and improving operations and maintenance procedures. It is
noted that OpEx and installation time have the lowest impact on LCOE of these factors,
indicating that the most gains to LCOE will come from CapEx-driven improvements.
==PAGE:132==
114
==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 8
Social Evaluation of
Techno-economic
Optimization
In this chapter we use the results of techno-economic optimization to explore the developer
as an actor, examining the complex process of translating a wind farm’s site and layout
design to a physical wind farm. If built, the potential wind farm designs presented in
the previous chapter overﬂow from a technical frame and bring unique socio-technical
controversy.
8.1
Wind Farm Site
8.1.1
Reintroducing the Network
The developer plays a central role within the siting network presented in Chapter 3
(Figure 3.8) in translating the wind farm from an intermediary as a site to an actor as a
physical entity. In preparing a bid, the developer is engaging in a nested process of siting
a wind farm within the Call Area, which is likely to contain two to three wind farms. As
stakeholder dialogue largely concerns the entire Morro Bay Call Area, many actors will
be aﬀected regardless of which site is chosen by a single developer within the Call Area.
The choice of wind farm site within the Morro Bay Call Area determines which
stakeholders will be most engaged. Hence, the network examined in this section traces
the boundary of a 1 GW wind farm and discusses the speciﬁc interactions and controversy
tied to its individual location.
8.1.2
Developer’s Perspective: Techno-economic
Optimization
Optimization for LCOE demonstrates the trade-oﬀs that the developer must consider in
terms of siting. The cost of the mooring system and installation encompasses 9% of total
costs, motivating developers to prioritize shallow-water sites, located in the southeast
region of the Call Area. In addition, both the expected installation port and point of
interconnection are located to the southeast of the Call Area, prividing further incentive
for developers to utilize this region.
Figure 4.1 demonstrates, that even within a developer’s bid preparation, the processes
of determining site and layout are often separate – the developer chooses a site to bid
==PAGE:134==
116
8 Social Evaluation of Techno-economic Optimization
on within the Call Area and then optimizes the layout. Yet, advantages can be seen
when the wind farm site is determined by the developer. The USCG scenario includes
the wind farm’s position within the Call Area as a design variable, resulting in LCOE
values lower than cases with pre-determined sites. Lease auctions will likely not allow
this ﬂexibility, as in the past BOEM has pre-partitioned the WEA into distinct lease
areas (Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, 2021a) that are bid on separately.
When mapping one developer’s wind farm site to the stakeholder network we cannot
ignore interaction with other potential wind farms within the Call Area. Given the
wind direction, a wind farm located in the southeast region of the Call Area will likely
experience shadow eﬀects from other wind farms, thereby reducing the AEP and increasing
the LCOE. Such eﬀects are not discussed in this analysis and merit further study.
8.1.3
Regulator: BOEM and the CEC
As the regulators of the oﬀshore wind leasing process, BOEM will be charged with
partitioning the Call Area into two or three areas available for leasing. Through techno-
economic analysis of wind farm sites within the Call Area, it becomes clear that portions
of the Call Area may be more attractive to developers than others – it might be
recommended that BOEM partitions the area to equalize the competition for each lease
area.
It is noted that among the four base cases, the range in LCOE values found is shallow
– the maximum diﬀerence found is 1.7 USD/MWh, approximately a 2% increase from
the lowest LCOE. This indicates that the process of partitioning the Call Area might
be driven by the logistics of access to ports and points of interconnection rather than
proﬁtability.
8.1.4
Environmental Impacts
The areas closer to shore, namely the V1 site and optimized layout in Scenario 1, occupy
the portion of the Call Area that borders the MBNMS. This proximity may result in
increased engagement with the sanctuary oversight that may require additional measures
taken to avoid disturbing this area during the installation and maintenance of the wind
farm.
Environmental groups may favor a southeast position of a wind farm in that it avoids
a large portion of the EFH area (2.5). In contrast, other sites in the west region of
the Morro Bay Call Area (V2) are partially located in an HAPC area. Advocates for
marine mammals concerned with entanglement with mooring lines may take issue with
sites located in the south and west regions of the Call Area (V2 and H1, for example)
where there are higher distributions of Humpback whales and Blue whales (A.2 and A.3).
In addition, a slightly higher distribution of Northern Elephant seals is found in the
southernmost region (H1) (A.5).
==PAGE:135==
8.1 Wind Farm Site
117
8.1.5
Department of Defense
Results for the DOD scenario presented in Chapter 7 indicate that a wind farm placed
in either of the original areas proposed by the DOD decreases its proﬁtability as opposed
to the original Call Area. In addition to proﬁts, the combined DOD-proposed areas allow
for a maximum power output far lower than available in the whole Call Area (1.8 GW
versus 2.4 GW for the entire Call Area).
Through the siting of the ’399 Morro Bay Area’, the DOD has demonstrated its
preference that wind energy be developed in the southern and western parts of the 2018
Call Area. This new area designation demonstrates that H2 and portions of V1 are not
viable sites for a wind farm. Given this consideration, the optimization results from H1
and V2 best reﬂect compromise with the DOD in terms of the wind farm site.
The ’399 Morro Bay Area’, if designated a WEA, will cause the DOD to relocate its
oﬀshore operations in the area. The ﬁnal footprint of the wind farms within that area
will not then interact with the DOD, who have been engaged in and prioritized in the
early stages of the construction of the wind farm site.
8.1.6
Fishing Industry
It is observed that both the V1 and optimal regular layout sites closely resemble the
original Castle Wind site (Figure B.1), which was constructed via a collaborative process
with the ﬁshing community. This may indicate that the ﬁshing community may be more
willing to compromise with a wind farm located closer to shore. One developer comments
that the ﬁshing industry is "not going to lose any revenue because you can still ﬁsh up to
the quota you’re allocated, but it may cost you more because you may need to travel
further away to catch that same amount of ﬁsh” (Shuman, 2021). A wind farm located in
this portion of the Call Area may be met with less resistance from the ﬁshing community,
who might seek to cohabit lease areas further to the west. However, more data is needed
to accurately reﬂect the economic impact on the ﬁshing industry.
Alternately, the ﬁshing industry may not distinguish between wind farms within the
Call Area. One representative objects to the expansion of the Call Area “At one time
the Call Area was 120 square miles and so that’s what we decided on and that’s what
the ﬁshermen and the city thought was going to happen — and it didn’t. Then it grew
immensely after that ... Now they want to take away almost 400 square miles. And so
we’re basically screwed” (Shuman, 2021).
8.1.7
Shipping Industry
The shipping industry, although unlikely to distinguish between sites in the Call Area,
may have a preference for wind farms built closer to shore. Figure B.3 demonstrates that
current shipping patterns intersect with the westerns portions of the Call Area. Thereby,
if a fairway is not established before the installations of wind farms within the lease
areas, those wind farms located in the western regions of the Call Area may come in
conﬂict with those ships that inhabit the area.
==PAGE:136==
118
8 Social Evaluation of Techno-economic Optimization
8.1.8
Visual Impact
The prioritization of the southeast corner of the Call Area calls to question the visual
impact of the wind farm, as this portion is that most visible from Hearst Castle and
Piedras Blancas, historic scenic landmarks. The visual impact of the wind farm is further
brought to question with the DOD South Area, which moves closer to shore. It is also
noted that the ’Morro Bay 399 Area’ includes a portion closer to shore which may also be
contested. BOEM has already begun to address this controversy through the production
of a visual simulation of what the wind farm might look like from such observation points,
included in Appendix C.
8.2
Wind Farm Layout
8.2.1
Re-introducing the Network
The wind farm layout design takes place in two phases: a preliminary layout is estimated
in a bid proposal and later revisited once the lease is won. In ANT analysis, we focus
on the latter stage, where a network concerned with the cohabitation of the wind farm
inﬂuences its ﬁnal design (Figure 3.10). This network, where the USCG plays a central
role as a regulator and translator, forms the navigational guidelines that act as input for
a wind farm’s ﬁnal layout design.
The layouts presented as results of techno-economic optimization simulate a devel-
oper’s perspective in the bid process – a preliminary stage of layout design. In the
construction of stakeholder-motivated scenarios, we anticipate the concerns of stake-
holders in the layout design network. In this section, we analyze how these preliminary
layouts might impact stakeholders if applied as a ﬁnalized layout design.
8.2.2
Developer’s Perspective: Techno-economic
Optimization
Given BOEM’s auction format, a developer’s objective of layout design in the bid stage is
to minimize the cost of energy to be competitive in securing a PPA. Due to this motive,
developers often limit their stakeholder engagement until after the project is won.
From this perspective, a developer might advocate for an irregular layout that allows
for the minimum LCOE. Of all layouts presented in the previous section, the lowest
LCOE values correspond to V1 of the base case as well as the irregular optimization
within the second of the USCG scenarios.
In addition, the techno-economic model demonstrates that mooring line design can
have a signiﬁcant impact on the LCOE – developers might seek out designs with smaller
mooring footprints to minimize costs and to allow for more ﬂexibility in the positioning
of turbines.
Yet as seen in this study, a uniform wind rose makes it such that regular spacing can
allow the developer to arrive at a near-optimal LCOE. This indicates that after the lease
is awarded the developer may not focus their eﬀorts on contesting the USCG’s request
==PAGE:137==
8.2 Wind Farm Layout
119
for regularity, but more on questions of the spacing between turbines and a provision of
an exclusion zone.
An exclusion zone is desirable for developers and it would prevent other actors from
using the wind farm site, thereby reducing the liability associated with the potential for
collisions. In addition, the provision of an Exclusion Zone may allow for irregular layout
designs due to the reduction of cohabitants of the wind farm.
8.2.3
USCG Navigational Guidelines
A regular grid spacing has been deemed compatible with multiple stakeholders present
in the network, as regulated through the USCG (Figure 3.7). In particular, a regular
pattern will allow for Search and Rescue procedures to proceed similarly to the East
Coast of the US. It is noted, though, that SAR procedures will need to be amended to
account for ﬂoating platforms, mooring lines, and dynamic cables, regardless.
Next, the USCG has the authority to designate which vessels can pass through the
wind farm. Currently, the USCG does not have the authority to designate a ﬂoating
wind farm as an exclusion zone. If this remains the case, regular layouts that provide
access lanes for marine users may be required.
In the techno-economic model, the mooring line attachment angle has a signiﬁcant
impact on a wind turbine’s footprint, and thereby the potential for interference with
other marine activities. Hence it may be of interest for the USCG to trace the mooring
footprint of structures in the process of determining the navigational guidelines for the
region.
In addition, the USCG may need to consider the impact of multiple farms in the
Morro Bay WEA. Figure 3.3 demonstrates that in New England, all adjacent wind farm
must place their turbines on the same grid to allow for vessels to have clear pathways
through the area. In the case of Morro Bay, the USCG may be motivated to regulate the
same provisions, such that all wind farms must lie on the same grid. This may further
constrain layout design after the lease is awarded.
8.2.4
Cohabitation: Fishing and Shipping
In the instance that the wind farm is not an exclusion zone, those who may seek to travel
through the wind farm (namely ﬁshing and small shipping vessels) have a vested interest
in the layout of a wind farm. Wider regular spacing may also allow for navigational
advantages – a larger range of vessels may be able to transit through wind farms. As
mentioned in a PARS study on the East Coast, a wider spacing between turbines may
alleviate any need for shipping lanes within the WEA (Unites States Coast Guard, 2020).
The discussion of mooring type and footprint is central to the ﬁshing industry.
Tension-leg platforms or taut mooring systems with small attachment angles introduce
the possibility for certain ﬁshing activities within the wind farm area. Hence optimization
results that include a smaller mooring footprint not only drive down LCOE but also
allow for more space between turbines that can enable cohabitation.
==PAGE:138==
120
8 Social Evaluation of Techno-economic Optimization
Although unlikely, there may be a possibility that there is a shipping lane between
wind farms within the Call Area. This would limit the area that wind farms can occupy,
thereby causing developers to reduce the spacing between turbines. Otherwise, the
shipping industry is unlikely to have a strong stake in a ﬂoating wind farm layout.
8.2.5
Environmental Groups
In addition to decreasing the LCOE, increased spacing between turbines is expected
to bring environmental beneﬁts, including lower chances of secondary entanglement of
marine mammals and collisions with avian species (Fox and Petersen, 2019). In addition,
wind farm designs with shorter mooring lines (driven by lower attachment angles) lessen
the risk of secondary entanglement, simply as shorter mooring lines provide less surface
area on which that marine debris may become entangled.
8.2.6
Visual Impact
In studies closer to shore, regular layouts are prioritized for decreased visual impact from
the wind farm (Pérez, 2019). As predicted in visual simulations (Appendix C), it is
unlikely that this will be an issue in practice, but may prove a point of contention in the
planning phase.
8.3
Reﬂections
A wind farm, if and when it emerges from the siting and layout network, has complex and
compounding impacts on all stakeholders of its network. The developer, when deciding
the site and layout of a wind farm may inevitably prioritize techno-economic feasibility –
determining sites that are the least expensive to install and maintain wind farms and
layouts that maximize the AEP.
The scenarios presented in the previous section allow the developer to anticipate how
potential stakeholders will impact the eventual cost of energy. As discussed in Chapter 4,
the cost of energy might be used to estimate a PPA price that will allow for a project to
be ﬁnancially viable. Therefore, by including stakeholder perspectives in the preliminary
site and layout design, the developer might increase the feasibility of a bid.
From techno-economic optimization including stakeholder perspectives, sites and
layouts emerge that may prove both proﬁtable to a developer as well as address stakeholder
concerns. First, siting close to shore avoids impacts with ﬁshing and shipping communities,
as well as avoids the migration paths of whale populations. In addition, in this site
regular optimization appears not only compatible with other marine users but also does
not sacriﬁce LCOE extensively due to a uniform wind rose. Finally, the mooring line
design is highlighted as a means to both drive down the LCOE of a project as well as to
reduce the risk of a project in terms of cohabitation with marine users and sea life.
Analyzing the impact of proposed layouts on local communities gives a road map for
stakeholder engagement after a lease is awarded. For example, if a site close to shore
is chosen, the developer may be compelled to further engage with the NMS and those
==PAGE:139==
8.3 Reﬂections
121
concerned with visible impact. In addition, the relatively low economic gains made in
irregular layouts suggest that developers might focus their priorities in negotiation with
the USCG on spacing and the creation of exclusion zones. Hence, if CA is to reach its
energy goals, the stakeholder network must be incorporated early on in the planning
process, such that the ﬁnal design and implementation of a constructed wind farm takes
into account all the concerns of the stakeholders in the communities it powers.
==PAGE:140==
122
==PAGE:141==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusions
9.1
Conclusions
California’s coastal waters have 588 GW of potential oﬀshore wind energy that can only
be captured using ﬂoating wind technology. This thesis uses a case study of Morro
Bay, the largest of California’s proposed areas for wind energy development. We take a
cross-disciplinary lens of socio-technical analysis and techno-economic optimization, to
enable an integrated exploration of the inﬂuence of local context on the site and layout of
a ﬂoating wind farm to determine the site’s ability to be cost-competitive in California’s
future energy mix.
The application of ANT to the case study of Morro Bay allows us to construct and
examine the socio-technical networks that give rise to a wind farm’s site and layout. In
investigating the controversy surrounding the siting of WEAs, it is found that the process
of constructing a wind farm site exists in a techno-centric frame, with key decision-makers
on a Task Force largely made up of government and research entities. The actors who
deﬁne the lease area are hence in stark contrast to those who will eventually interact
with an operational wind farm. In mapping the network that deﬁnes a wind farm layout,
we shed light on how its design has the potential be shaped collaboratively by those
actors that seek to navigate within its perimeter. Stakeholder interviews reveal new and
complex challenges in accessing ﬂoating farms oﬀthe coast of CA: mooring lines, dynamic
cables, and moving platforms introduce uncertainly for those that seek to cohabit the
space. Finally, we identify paradoxes that stall the development of Morro Bay as a WEA
– cycles of inaction between government and industry actors that must be broken to
enable the emergence of ﬂoating wind in the region.
The second half of this thesis allows for the exploration of the perspective of a wind
farm developer through layout optimization for the LCOE of a ﬂoating wind farm. The
LCOE values around 100 USD/MWh calculated in this thesis are in keeping with current
LCOE values seen in the US, with increases due to novel substructure and mooring
systems. The analysis demonstrates the signiﬁcant impact of the mooring system design in
deep waters (> 600 m), which inﬂuences both the cost and the distances between turbines
due to their footprint on the seabed. Furthermore, layout optimization demonstrates
that of the parameters varied, the LCOE is most sensitive to water depth and distance
to landfall.
Stakeholder-motivated scenarios allow the developer to anticipate how potential
stakeholders will impact the eventual cost of energy. A signiﬁcant ﬁnding of the USCG-
motivated scenario is that in the case of a uniform wind rose irregular optimization yields
only marginally improved results when compared to a regular layout of similar location
and power density. Sites proposed by the DOD limit the number of turbines that can be
==PAGE:142==
124
9 Conclusions
placed in each area, resulting in an increased LCOE.
Optimized layouts, in combination with the stakeholder networks generated in ANT
illustrate the interplay between the wind farm design and those stakeholders it impacts.
The similar performance of regular and irregular layouts demonstrate that in this case
stakeholder perspectives, initially viewed as limiting the proﬁtability of a wind farm,
may be synergistic with the minimization of the LCOE. This phenomenon is echoed in
the case of the mooring line design, where smaller footprints both improve LCOE and
allow for cohabitation. Exploring potential sites and layouts for their relationship with
local stakeholders may not only alleviate a developer’s risk of meeting road blocks after
the lease is awarded, but also focus their stakeholder engagement eﬀorts on those groups
most impacted.
The crux of this thesis is the interplay between socio-technical analysis and techno-
economic optimization. In research settings, these two ﬁelds are classically kept apart
and are framed with separate aims and problem formulations. This thesis demonstrates
that the construction of a wind farm necessarily causes these two lines of analysis
to converge when wind farms are placed in a local context. Unique insights can be
found at the intersection of disciplines: wind farm design becomes more viable when
those who are impacted are included in the problem formulation. The need for a link
between stakeholders and those designing wind farms must be bi-directional – interviews
demonstrate a widespread lack of knowledge and trust in ﬂoating oﬀshore wind technology
– a gap which can only be closed by transparency and engagement on the part of the
developers and those engaged in the techno-economic design of wind farms.
This thesis oﬀers a snapshot into today’s socio-techno-economic paradigm to inform
and motivate further inquiry into the complex, novel, and interdisciplinary ﬁeld of ﬂoating
oﬀshore wind development. A coordinated eﬀort between a socio-technical analysis
and techno-economic optimization allows for a viable and economical development of
ﬂoating wind energy. As ﬂoating oﬀshore wind grows across the globe, we encourage
planners, researchers, and developers to adopt a multidisciplinary approach to ensure
that the new industry is socially, technically, and economically feasible from conception
to decommissioning.
==PAGE:143==
9.2 Future Work
125
9.2
Future Work
This thesis spans two ﬁelds and their intersection, thereby future work can be conducted
in multiple directions. In the following, ideas for future work are presented, to further
inform and improve the analysis of this thesis. First, a further socio-technical analysis
might include:
• More stakeholder interviews, spanning more local stakeholders and industries for a
fuller picture. These stakeholders may include the shipping, ﬁshing, and tourism
industry (eg. Hearst Castle) as well as the local government.
Next, the integration of TopFarm and ORBIT for a basic quantitative model of
ﬂoating oﬀshore wind opens the door for further improvement in this ﬁeld:
• Inclusion of a more sophisticated (dynamic) wake model for ﬂoating turbines.
• Including hydrodynamic motions in the mooring system design, for a more sophis-
ticated mooring line model in deep waters (> 600 m).
• Further investigation of the relationship between mooring line footprint and spacing
constraints (eg a triangle spacing constraint as used in Hywind Scotland).
• Electrical system optimization, taking into account dynamic array and export
cables. Might include updated costing models and transmission losses.
• Research on cohabitation with ﬂoating wind farms: mooring line and array cable
interactions with marine environment and ﬁshing.
• Inclusion of positioning and costing of ﬂoating OSS in a model.
• Improved grid optimization techniques for computational eﬃciency.
Finally, there is signiﬁcant room for growth in the integration of socio-technical and
techno-economic models.
• Metric for optimization: LCOE as a metric has been critiqued on multiple fronts
relevant to this thesis.
– A future model might "consider not only the cost side of the equation but
also the value side of the equation" (LBNL Researcher, 2021), which may
be considered by load serving entities that grant PPAs. For example, some
wind farm layouts add value with metrics such as persistence in energy output
and complementary load proﬁles to other sources of energy. These types of
metrics allow for further connection of wind farms and their layouts to those
stakeholders who interact with them.
– LCOE (or a PPA price) does not take into account social impact (as noted
in Chapter 4). It may be of interest to include indicators of social cost in
optimization, such as the minimization of externalities, such as environmental
impact and noise pollution.
• Further integration of stakeholder constraints into a model, including phenomena
such as exclusion zones and shipping lanes.
