APPENDIX B
ANT Appendix
B.1
Morro Bay pre-2018
Figure B.1: Morro Bay proposed potential wind energy before the call in 2018 (LLC,
2016)
B.2
Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
To frame the discussion of the Morro Bay case, it is of interest to examine the parties that
are relevant in the discussion of the placement of a wind farm in the area and navigation
within the area.
B.2.1
Federal and State-level Government Stakeholders
BOEM, USCG, and CEC are chieﬂy responsible for siting and regulating the lease of
federal land oﬀthe coast of CA for oﬀshore wind use. These stakeholders often hold
public forums for local industries and communities and are the chief interface with the
developer.
==PAGE:164==
146
B ANT Appendix
B.2.1.1
The Bureau of Ocean Management
BOEM is responsible for oﬀshore renewable energy development in federal waters and is
also the main party responsible for local engagement in the leasing process.
BOEM has currently set the Morro Bay Call Area, with the caveat that the area or
part of the area may be available for commercial leasing, given interest. In their Call
for Information and Nominations for Commercial Leasing for Wind Power Development
on the OCS oﬀshore CA, BOEM requests nominations and comments on the leasing
areas, which garnered eleven nominations. These nominations serve to demonstrate a
competitive interest in the region (Cruickshank, 2018).
In BOEM’s process of determining the Call Area, the following areas are excluded:
• Areas with water depths of 1100 meters, as advised by NREL (Arent et al., 2012)
• National Marine Sanctuaries
• Areas with mean wind speeds less than 7 meters per second
• Nearshore areas with high levels of ﬁshing activity. This stipulation invites ﬁsheries
to comment on the lease areas and commits to continued engagement with industry
stakeholders.
• Areas with a high concentration of undersea cables. This includes a portion of
Morro Bay.
• The Santa Barbara Channel, which is characterized by high vessel traﬃc
Conversely, "BOEM gave preference to areas within proximity to existing transmission
infrastructure that could provide for potential integration into CA’s existing electrical
grid" (Cruickshank, 2018).
BOEM has been largely responsible for the initiation of the leasing process and has
conducted outreach to local communities, industries, and non-governmental organizations
(NGOs). Before issuing leases, BOEM will prepare an environmental assessment (EA)
and conduct consultations for public involvement, including federal agencies, the state
and local government, tribes, and other interested parties.
BOEM’s regulations require lessees to provide a Navigational Safety Risk Assessment
(NSRA) for a project. In addition, the leasing agency is required to provide a site
assessment and construction and operation plan to cooperating agencies (Cruickshank,
2018).
B.2.1.2
The US Coast Guard, District 11
The USCG is the federal agency concerned with maintaining safe vessel navigation and
traﬃc near areas occupied by an oﬀshore wind farm. USCG District 11 participates in
the oﬀshore wind CA Task Force. The USCG is responsible for enforcing navigational
safety requirements, such as markings and control mechanisms, which are present in
the wind farm. In addition, the USCG liaises with industry stakeholders to determine
applicable vessel corridors in the wind farm. In stakeholder meetings, the Coast Guard
==PAGE:165==
B.2 Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
147
acts as a subject matter expert for maritime safety, security, and mobility, as well as
natural defense and protection of natural resources.
As necessitated by the Ports and Waterways Safety Act, the Coast Guard is required
to conduct studies to "provide safe access routes for vessel traﬃc in the waters under the
jurisdiction of the United States" (Unites States Coast Guard, 2020).
The USCG reviews the NSRA for the wind farm, which represents the authority to
prohibit vessel entry through wind farms. A NSRA requires evaluation of marine traﬃc
information based on an analysis of vessel movement data and consultation with Coast
Guard oﬃcials, pilots’ associations, the maritime industry, local ﬁsherman, recreational
boat users, and others, and includes analysis of changes that will occur as a result of
the placement of the wind farm including potentially cumulative eﬀects associated with
adjacent wind farms (Papavizas, 2021).
As mentioned in their initial response to the Call for Information and Nominations,
the Coast Guard is concerned with the potential for increased vessel density and collision
risk due to re-routing vessels around or through wind farms, as well as the cumulative
impacts of multiple WEAs close to each other. It should be noted that the Coast Guard
does not have the authority to approve, disapprove, permit nor in any way authorize an
Oﬀshore Renewable Energy Installation (Unites States Coast Guard, 2020).
B.2.1.3
California Energy Commission and California Public Utilities
Commission
The CEC is the governing body overseeing all energy development in the state. The CEC
has been heavily involved in the oﬀshore WEA siting and leasing processes as a partner
with BOEM and has initiated requests for research proposals to better understand the
WEAs.
The CA Public Utilities Commission (CPUC) regulates services and utilities in CA
and will play a central role in the commissioning of oﬀshore wind in the state. The CPUC
will evaluate potential procurement of oﬀshore wind in the State’s Integrated Resource
Planning (IRP) process, which uses modeling to optimize the energy infrastructure
to meet the State’s greenhouse gas reduction goals. It should be noted that to date,
modeling has not included oﬀshore wind, but the CPUC "expect to use newly available
oﬀshore wind data in the 2021-2022 IRP planning cycle" (Harland, 2020).
As the CEC and CPUC are key organizers of CA oﬀshore wind, both have expressed
the need for an extensive assessment of the area and the impacts that might come with
oﬀshore wind development. Notable is a call for more data surrounding marine birds
and mammals, an evaluation of commercial ﬁshing, and a preliminary visual eﬀects
assessment.
B.2.1.4
The US Department of Defense
The US DOD has a vested interest in the Call Area, as there are current military training
and testing operations near oﬀshore, the activities in the area are shown in Figure B.2.
This stakeholder may aﬀect the boundary of the Call Area, as well as interact with the
==PAGE:166==
148
B ANT Appendix
layout of turbines within the Call Area through the need for ﬂight paths. The Department
of Defense issued an initial Mission Compatibility Assessment in 2017 evaluating the
compatibility of oﬀshore wind turbines with current and proposed military uses oﬀCA.
In 2020, the DOD proposed an amended Morro Bay Call Area that is signiﬁcantly smaller
than the BOEM proposed area, shown in Figure B.2b.
BOEM list stipulations that the DOD might make in wind farm operation, including
"hold and save harmless agreements; mandatory coordination with DOD on speciﬁed
activities; restrictions on electromagnetic emissions; curtailment of wind farm operations
during speciﬁc DOD events; and evacuation procedures from the lease area for safety
reasons when notiﬁed by the DOD."(Cruickshank, 2018)
(a) DOD activity
(b) DOD proposed sites
Figure B.2: DOD activity (Flint, 2021) & alternative DOD proposed areas (Newsom,
2020)
B.2.1.5
The California Coastal and State Lands Commissions
The CA Coastal Commission has submitted direct comments to the DOD proposed Call
Area sites and "recommend that BOEM, the State of CA, industry, and other interested
parties pursue oﬀshore wind development as far oﬀshore as is possible to ensure that
impacts to coastal resources are avoided, minimized, and mitigated appropriately. The
DOD submission, which includes land inside the inland Coastal Zone boundary (within
the Monterey Bay National Marine Sanctuary), will be under the jurisdiction of the
Coastal Commission. The Commission has voiced concerns that "wind development in
these areas is likely to result in increased impacts to marine resources, public views and
ﬁshing as compared to sites within the BOEM Call Areas or other sites further oﬀshore"
(Huckelbridge and Carl, 2020).
Particular emphasis is placed on visual impact, as the Coastal Act protects public
views as “as a resource of public importance”(Huckelbridge and Carl, 2020), and the "Big
Sur Land Use Plan viewshed policies protect all public coastal views within the ’critical
viewshed’".
==PAGE:167==
B.2 Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
149
The CA State Lands Commission has jurisdiction and management authority over
the City of Morrow Bay and the Port San Luis Harbor District. Hence, the development
of onshore wind facilities will "almost certainly require a lease from the Commission,
which in turn will require the Commission to carefully consider and balance competing
Public Trust uses and values consistent with and in furtherance of its Environmental
Justice and Tribal Consultation policies" (Lucchesi, 2019).
The CA Department of Recreation and Parks has submitted requests for more
information, especially with regards to visual impacts, mammals, and shorebirds, and
other environmental concerns, as well as project oversight and contingency planning
(Bachman, 2020).
B.2.1.6
National Marine Sanctuaries
The BOEM-proposed Morro Bay Call Area is bordered to the east by the Monterey Bay
National Marine Sanctuary. In addition, there is a proposed Chumash Heritage National
Marine Sanctuary (CHNMS), which would encompass the entirety of the Morro Bay Call
Area. National marine sanctuaries must be considered separately, as BOEM does not
have the authority to govern the use of sanctuary land.
The Monterey National Marine Sanctuary Foundation, the largest of thirteen federal
sanctuaries, has expressed opposition to oﬀshore wind farm development within or near
the sanctuary, stating that the sanctuary "is not a place for large industrial development
like a wind farm" (Panetta and Bryant, 2019). Although the Call Area speciﬁed by
BOEM does not include the sanctuary lands, the revised Call Areas set by the DOD
include areas within the sanctuary. In their letter of opposition, the Foundation cites
unforeseen environmental consequences, visual impact, and conﬂicts with shipping and
ﬁshing activities in the region (Panetta and Bryant, 2019).
The Oﬃce of National Marine Sanctuaries, an oﬃce within the National Ocean
Service, NOAA, U.S. Department of Commerce, is the federal agency responsible for
oversight and management of the National Marine Sanctuary System (NMSS). They have
determined the CHNMS nomination to have "successfully met the national signiﬁcance
criteria and management considerations described in the sanctuary nomination process"
(Edmondson and Dourus, 2019). If the proposal were to move forward, "all aspects of
the proposed [Wind Energy Area] would be under consideration, including proposed
boundaries, management focus of the sanctuary, and prohibitions and exemptions." If
this were to occur, an extensive environmental analysis will need to be carried out to
assess the cumulative impacts that oﬀshore wind might have on the CHNMS site.
B.2.2
Local Stakeholders
In CA, stakeholders in the area hold the key to the emergence of this new technology in
the region. A recent study by BOEM presents the ﬁndings for 79 public meetings with
various interested parties in CA (Kearns&West, 2018a), and received over one hundred
comments on the Call for Information and Nominations. Views from representative
bodies of local stakeholders are expressed in the following subsections.
==PAGE:168==
150
B ANT Appendix
B.2.2.1
Fishing Industry
The local ﬁshing industry is represented by the Morro Bay Commercial Fishermen’s
Organization, Morro Bay Harbor District, Central Coast Cable Committee, Morro Bay
Harbor Advisory Board. The ﬁshing industry in the Morro Bay area has stated in
response to the announcement of the wind energy lease areas that "we are not opposed
to the concept of oﬀshore renewable energy projects and express desire for "inclusive and
meaningful ﬁshing industry collaboration" (Johnston and Hawkins, 2020).
The Paciﬁc Coast Federal Fishermen’s Associations state "the loss of 1,073 square
miles of ﬁshing grounds would have devastating impacts to the ﬁshing communities for
whom those seemingly small areas represent a large portion of their most accessible
and productive ﬁshing grounds"(Conroy, 2020). The ﬁshing community has expressed
interest in compensation for the reduction of accessible ﬁshing grounds. Furthermore, in
industry states that "The designation of wind energy areas will also impact ﬁshery science
surveys and management that both enable and constrain commercial ﬁsheries," citing
that the addition of a wind farm area to the already protected zones might severely limit
surveillance opportunities for the industry. In the case that the wind farm area could be
ﬁshed, these groups have also expressed concerns about the potential entanglement of
gear.
The Paciﬁc Coast Federation of Fisherman’s Associations reiterates that "BOEM’s
Call Areas are situated in and/or enclose Essential Fish Habitats (EFH) and Habitat
Areas of Particular Concern (HAPC) for commercially important ﬁsh and shellﬁsh species"
(Conroy, 2020).
In addition, there is some hesitation regarding the CADEMO project by the Cierco
Corporation, citing "the relative proximity to the Morro Bay Call Area. This highlights
the need to consider and evaluate the cumulative impacts of oﬀshore development
projects."
Speciﬁcally, the ﬁshing industry demands an impact statement that is "comprehensive
with respect to the harvested species that occur, and gear types that are used, both in
these proposed Call Areas and along cable transmission lanes proposed to deliver energy
from oﬀshore to the mainland."
Notably, the Morro Bay Commercial Fishermen’s Organization has reached a consen-
sus with the Castle Wind proposed project, ﬁnalized in 2018, which agreed upon an area
that would have the least impact on the ﬁsheries "and would mitigate the displacement
of our ﬁshermen with this operation" (Hafer, 2019). This work demonstrates that local
ﬁshing communities if engaged in the project from an early stage in siting, may be able
to reach a consensus with the Morro Bay site. Similarly, the Alliance of Communities for
Sustainable Fisheries states "a smaller Morro Bay Call Area may be supportable under
certain circumstances". This conditional acceptance of wind development in the area is
contingent upon further examination of environmental and structural concerns, as well
as displaced work.
Representatives have expressed concern over the large role that the DOD has played
in the siting process without other stakeholder involvement, and logging "signiﬁcant
concerns about the Expansion Areas" (Okoniewski, 2020).
==PAGE:169==
B.2 Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
151
In their ﬁrst response to the Call Area announcement, representatives of the ﬁshing
community strongly state "We disagree with BOEM’s assertion that it has chosen Call
Areas that are not high-value ﬁshing grounds. As seafood industry organizations and
businesses, we are aware the proposed wind energy areas are situated within productive
ﬁshing grounds, constituting an important part of the ‘human environment’ of the coast
of California."(Oppenheim et al., 2019)
CA’s ﬁshermen are concerned that large-scale oﬀshore wind development could strip
them of important ﬁshing grounds and habitat. The loss of ﬁshing grounds has been
estimated with the assumption that "Fishermen will have to stay at least 2 miles away
from the edge of the area to avoid having their gear either drift into the area on the
way down to the bottom in the current or drift into the area while pulling the gear up.
This will eﬀectively increase the non-ﬁshable area by at least 2 miles times the perimeter
length of the wind farm area which will almost certainly double the area lost to ﬁshermen"
(Hafer, 2019).
The National Marine Fisheries Service demonstrates a signiﬁcant concern with dis-
turbance to marine life in EFHs, noting that "direct siting of the FOWTs elements and
indirect eﬀects of operations of the FOWTs may adversely aﬀect both the quantity and
quality of EFH supporting managed species"(Oppenheim et al., 2019). In addition, the
organization expresses concern for the entanglement and collision of ﬁsh with substruc-
tures, mooring lines, and dynamic cables. The Paciﬁc Fishery Management Council
reiterates these concerns and adds that "oﬀshore wind energy facilities may interfere
with long-standing scientiﬁc surveys that are critical to the eﬀective management of
ﬁsheries by this Council"(Tracy, 2019), recommending that the CEC and BOEM work to
minimize this interference.
The industry has expressed concerns over the ﬁnancial viability of ﬁsheries if wind
farms are to be built in the Morro Bay Call Area, citing the proﬁts of the Dungeness
Crab industries in the region. Apprehension of the long-term economic impacts to this
industry is cited as a chief concern. Fisherman cited the value of their industry in local
tourism: "Commercial ﬁshing is embedded in the fabric of the community and its working
waterfront is an attraction. How will a large-scale commercial oﬀshore wind industry
change the character of Morro Bay and will tourist dollars still come to the working
waterfront if the ﬁshing industry is displaced?"(Hafer, 2021)
B.2.2.2
Maritime Sector
The maritime sector, including commercial vessel operators, port authorities and per-
sonnel, and Harbor Safety committees, would be one of the industries most displaced
by oﬀshore wind development on the Central Coast. In discussion with BOEM, The
maritime sector expressed interest in oﬀshore wind technologies, surface and subsurface
occupiable area, types of mooring/anchoring systems, and requirements within the project
boundaries (i.e., would vessels be allowed to transit between turbines, types of structural
lighting, etc.).
As the BOEM Call Areas are in federal waters, nationwide and global maritime
organizations have a stake in the location and size of wind energy areas. The National
==PAGE:170==
152
B ANT Appendix
Ocean Industries Association (NOIA) is a proponent of oﬀshore wind, stating that
"NOIA’s oil and gas industry members have decades of experience in the Gulf of Mexico,
Alaska and oﬀthe coast of California showing that oil and natural gas development and
other ocean industries can co-exist and thrive alongside each other"(Charters, 2019).
A prominent voice in the maritime sector is the shipping industry, as represented in
the comments submitted to BOEM by the World Shipping Council, American Waterways
Operator, and the Paciﬁc Merchant Shipping Operation. The American Waterways
Operator stresses the importance of collaboration with the US Coast Guard, as they
consider the necessary sea space for vessels to maneuver safely and contain recommended
minimum separation distances for the siting of oﬀshore structures near shipping routes
(Costanzo, 2019).
Other voices are not as neutral – the World Shipping Council and Paciﬁc Merchant
Shipping Association argue that the majority of the Morro Bay Call Area lies within a
major shipping corridor, claiming" the vessels that currently operate in the traﬃc routes
with which those Call Areas conﬂict would be forced to move further oﬀshore, where they
could create potential navigational safety conﬂicts with other oceangoing commercial
vessels already operating there"(World Shipping Council, 2019) and should be removed
in near entirety from consideration. These groups state that the Call Area conﬂict
with the following US Coast Guard Marine Planning Guidelines concerning coastwise or
coastal shipping routes, navigation safety corridors, and port approaches, and propose
the elimination of the (large) portion of the Morro Bay Call Area that intersects the
easternmost lane of the Monterey Bay National Marine Sanctuary tracks.
Figure B.3: Shipping and other use Call Area CA Oﬀshore Wind Energy: Existing
Infrastructure and Industrial Uses | Maps | Data Basin 2021
==PAGE:171==
B.2 Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
153
B.2.2.3
Morro Bay Community and Government
Perhaps the strongest proponents of oﬀshore wind development in Morro Bay are its
local municipal government, who are motivated by the opportunity for oﬀshore wind
development to spur job creation and to replace retiring nuclear energy facilities in Diablo
Canyon. Put explicitly, the City of Morro Bay states "The success of an oﬀshore wind
project within the Morro Bay WEA is of critical importance to the City. San Luis Obispo
County and the City are in substantial need for local economic development, and we
view oﬀshore wind as a critical part of the revitalization of the Morro Bay and county
economies."
In past years, the community has suﬀered an economic downturn – the Economic
Vitality Corporation of San Luis Obispo county states "Our region continues to face
two challenges: one of the highest costs of living in the nation and a persistent lack of
head-of-household jobs. Our underemployment is several times worse than the state and
national average, and the cost of living is the sixth highest in the United States, leaving
many to work more than two jobs to support their households".
The San Luis Obispo County Community Stakeholders articulate the impact of the
nuclear power facility closure: "Our communities now face the planned closure of the
Diablo Canyon Power Plant, the last nuclear power plant on the west coast and our
county’s largest private employer, thereby losing an economic impact of approximately
$1 Billion to our region annually"(Peshong, 2019) The organization supports oﬀshore
wind as "We have a highly-skilled workforce that is well suited to the needs of the wind
energy industry, along with a community that embraces the creation of such jobs."
The City of Morro Bay recognizes the importance of shipping and tourism, which
both may be impacted by oﬀshore wind development, yet supports wind initiatives as
"we struggle to fund the infrastructure and other services needed to support these key
industries" (Headding, 2019). Hence, local governments are unanimous in their support
of oﬀshore wind and push for a minimum project size for cost-competitiveness.
Although support for oﬀshore wind is unanimous among municipal governments, there
is also an overarching desire for a multi-factor auction format that prioritizes community
beneﬁts agreements, arguing that such agreements will "maximize the probability of
success and the minimization of public opposition to the development of the WEA
areas"(Headding, 2019). In addition, these communities seek to prioritize proposals that
seek to rely upon existing onshore support facilities rather than new construction.
It should be mentioned that many of these statements use very similar, if not identical
language to that used by Castle Wind LLC, which has shown signiﬁcant stakeholder
engagement in the area.
B.2.2.4
Indigenous Communities
Indigenous communities, most speciﬁcally the Chumash tribe have commented on
BOEM’s activities. In the BOEM report (Cruickshank, 2018), the issues most im-
portant to tribes are preserving cultural resources including on the seabed, protecting
local marine life, and maintaining certain ocean and horizon viewsheds. In addition,
==PAGE:172==
154
B ANT Appendix
there have been requests to be involved in the project construction and decommissioning
phases.
The most signiﬁcant conﬂict in the area is tied to the proposed Chumash Heritage
Natural Marine Sanctuary, which encompasses both the Morro Bay and Diablo Canyon
WEAs. The Chumash tribe in the area is naturally opposed to wind energy development
(Kearns&West, 2018a), which may impede the designation of the proposed sanctuary.
Other indigenous tribes that comment include: Bear River Band of the Rohnerville
Rancheria, Blue Lake Rancheria, Cher-Ae Heights Indian Community of the Trinidad
Rancheria, Coyote Valley Band of Pomo Indians, Santa Ynez Band of Chumash Indians,
and Tolowa Dee-ni’ Nation.
It should be noted that renewable energy development has existed in tribal sanctuaries
in the past. One example is the Arrow Canyon Solar Project, located on the Moapa Band
of Paiutes Indian Reservation in Nevada. A combination of 200 MW solar capacity and
75 MW battery storage will be commissioned in 2022 (Institute for Energy Economics
and Financial Analysis (IEEFA), 2019).
Figure B.4: Proposed Chumash Marine Sanctuaries (Northern Chumash Tribal Councill,
2020)
B.2.2.5
Tourism
Local tourism businesses are less than enthusiastic about oﬀshore wind development
in CA. Notable is the Hearst Corporation, which owns the Hearst Ranch and manages
==PAGE:173==
B.2 Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
155
the Hearst Castle, which was donated to the State in 1958. "The Hearst Castle is the
most visited State Park in California, with an average of 720,000 visitors per year, with
revenue to the State of $12 million dollars a year" (O’Toole, 2020). The Corporation
expresses concerns about impacts to viewshed of the Hearst Castle, as wind turbines
placed in the lease area as currently planned will be visible from the Hearst Castle, stating
that "the Castle’s expansive views of the Paciﬁc Ocean and the Coast, a panoramic
landscape that Hearst entrusted to the State in 1958 and further protected in 2005, is
one of its major draws" (O’Toole, 2020). Finally, the Hearst Corporation urges BOEM
"to consider revising the boundaries of the lease site and Call Area to a size and location
that eliminates any potential view of the project from Hearst Castle or anywhere along
the protected California coast and coastal range" (O’Toole, 2020).
B.2.2.6
Environmental NGOs
In CA, numerous organizations have expressed concern for the environmental impact
of oﬀshore wind development, and submitted comments on the lease areas proposed by
BOEM.
As a general note many organizations are in favor of oﬀshore wind development,
but seek to address concerns on Environmental Impact. The National Resources De-
fense Council (NRDC) states "we need to embrace clean industries such as oﬀshore
wind while incorporating protections that will help defend marine life that is already
stressed"(Aylesworth et al., 2019). The NGOs collaborating with the NRDC represented
express faith in federal marine protections already in place in CA, clarifying that "given
the commitment the federal government and state of California have made to protecting
California’s marine environment, we believe oﬀshore wind development can only proceed
in a manner that safeguards these protected ocean habitats and species"(Murock et al.,
2020).
These groups cite studies that mooring and transmission cables from ﬂoating turbine
platforms pose potential scouring issues for habitat and entanglement or displacement
risks for marine life like whales and propose baseline studies to understand these risks
and to develop best management practices to mitigate impacts. Of particular concern is
the benthic impact of catenary mooring lines and drag anchors, which in high sea states
may move across the seaﬂoor and aﬀect habitats. In addition, signiﬁcant concern and
demand for further research are placed on the entanglement of marine mammals with
mooring lines and dynamic cables as well as collisions with local birds (Flick and Kelly,
2020).
Many environmental groups make the recommendation that BOEM engages more ex-
tensively with experts and stakeholders to "avoid and minimize both local and cumulative
ecological impacts and user conﬂicts" in the Call Areas (Ziegler, 2019).
These NGOs have also expressed concerns as to the weight that the DOD is being
given in siting oﬀshore wind farms, stating "Department of Defense should not be the de
facto siting agency for oﬀshore wind development in California"(Aylesworth et al., 2019),
claiming that DOD’s role is superseding the priorities of other stakeholders. "By engaging
in private negotiations with oﬀshore wind developers to discover areas of potential
==PAGE:174==
156
B ANT Appendix
compatibility with oﬀshore wind development on the Central Coast, BOEM, DOD, and
industry become the sole parties to privileged and conﬁdential information"(Aylesworth
et al., 2019).
The Defenders of Wildlife urge BOEM to "include the identiﬁcation and generation of
data in both the marine and terrestrial environments"(Flick and Kelly, 2020) in oﬀshore
wind planning.
The NRDC makes the following proposals for the WEA:
1. Exclude Habitats of Particular Concern
2. Exclude Essential Fish Habitat Conservation Areas
3. Give Important Bird Areas a higher priority of avoidance over other parts of the
Call Areas
4. Carefully consider Biologically Important Areas when making siting decisions
5. Prohibit energy leasing within Outer Continental Shelf Lands Act
The groups highlight the following research priorities in environmental assessment:
benthic habitat, ﬁsh, seabirds, and marine mammals.
The American Bird Conservancy is more stringent with its recommendations, re-
questing "that the Morro Bay North wind energy area be removed from the map com-
pletely"(Goyart and Nevnis, 2019), as it is a region of high abundance of migratory and
resident seabirds. The Center for Biological Diversity also highlights the "migratory
Sooty Shearwaters which are listed as near threatened and declining on the ICUN Red
List" (Belenky, 2019) as important species to protect.
B.2.3
Wind Industry Stakeholders
There are numerous wind energy innovators, developers, and advocates that have a
vested interest in the development of wind energy in the region. Representatives of these
communities were interviewed for this project, and their comments will be discussed in
further detail in the following sections.
B.2.3.1
Wind Farm Developers
Commercial wind farm developers by deﬁnition have the most to gain in the wind
energy leasing process and have an interest in expediting the leasing process and limiting
the regulation on the wind farm site.
In their submission to the auction process,
developers must include the entire wind farm plan, ﬁnancing, securing permitting,
running environmental studies, and stakeholder management.
With regards to the wind farm layout, developers are responsible for conducting
a Navigation Safety Risk Assessment to evaluate navigation impacts associated with
siting, construction, establishment, operation, maintenance, and/or decommissioning of
a structure. Developers are also responsible for assessing the risk associated with vessels
deviating from proscribed routes through the wind farm.
==PAGE:175==
B.2 Relevant Stakeholders and their Interests
157
In the recent Call for Information and Nomination, numerous developers submitted
comments, including Castle Wind LLC, Equinor Wind US LLC, RWE Renewables,
Cierco, EDP Renewables, and Aker Solutions.
Castle Wind LLC, formerly Trident Wind, submitted an independent lease request
in January 2016 for an area representing a portion of the Morro Bay site. Since this
lease request, the developer has made signiﬁcant progress in stakeholder acceptance
in the form of Community Beneﬁts Agreements (CBAs) with the City of Morro Bay
and the local ﬁshing industry. In addition, they have secured the support of multiple
local stakeholders, as mentioned in other sections. It follows that in their comments to
BOEM and the CEC in 2018 and 2020, Castle Wind stresses the importance of local
acceptance and requirement for CBAs in the leasing process, supporting the "use of
a multi-factor bidding type that acknowledges legally binding agreements"(Weinstein,
2019) with key stakeholders. In addition, Castle Wind, like many other developers
stresses "that any oﬀshore wind project in California be sized appropriately in order
to be cost-competitive with other sources of renewable energy available in California’s
merchant electric market"(Weinstein, 2019).
As might be expected, most developers urge BOEM to consider a larger Call Area than
that proposed by the DOD. Equinor (EWUS) claims that "the development of oﬀshore
wind resources can be highly compatible with other uses of maritime resources. EWUS
cautions BOEM against precluding wind energy development at an early stage because
of potential conﬂicting uses"(Geijerstam, 2019). EWUS also recommends that BOEM
not yet consider the WEAs as automatic exclusion zones, maintaining the possibility for
stakeholders other than the Coast Guard to have equal access to the wind farm before
the lease is issued.
Concerning the DOD areas proposed in 2020, RWE Renewables urges BOEM to
identify a "larger planning area – including the Morro Bay Call Area – that represents
the limited space oﬀthe Central California coast suitable for future leasing of oﬀshore
wind, given the legal and physical constraints on oﬀshore wind development outside that
area."(McKeever, 2020)
Similarly, Cierco, who are proposing a demonstration project in the Morro Bay
Call Area, state that the DOD-proposed "modiﬁcations to the Morro Bay Call Area, if
accompanied by removal of the Diablo Canyon Call Area, would not provide suﬃcient
area for more than 1 GW and thus would not enable the economies of scale and develop-
ment pathway for a full-scale oﬀshore wind industry on the Central Coast."(Jakobsson,
2020). The company also suggest the Morro Bay Call Area be split into four 250 MW
lease areas, with the opportunity to encourage "collaboration between developers in
areas such as stakeholder engagement, wind measurements, surveys, grid connection
access, and integration post lease award." Cierco also believes that "stakeholder, special
Interest or local authority group’s views on the acceptability or preference for particular
projects, consortiums and/or technologies should not be a consideration within the leasing
process."(Jakobsson, 2020)
EDP Renewables, which is developing an independent 100-150 MW ﬂoating project
with Aker Solutions and Principle Power in the Humbolt Call Area, also submit a
nomination for the Morro Bay Call Area to develop multiple ﬂoating oﬀshore wind
==PAGE:176==
158
B ANT Appendix
projects. "Aker Solutions is therefore stressing the importance that multiple oﬀshore lease
areas are made available to the industry both in the upcoming lease process"(Margulis,
2019)
B.2.3.2
Wind Technology Manufacturers
The growth of wind energy oﬀthe coast of CA will result in a market opportunity for
Original Equipment Manufacturers (OEMs) in the ﬂoating wind industry. Notable OEMs
are ﬂoating substructure manufacturers, such as Principle Power, which is developing a
semi-submersible design and has participated in demonstration projects oﬀthe coast of
Portugal. These companies have not yet submitted oﬃcial comments.
B.2.3.3
Oﬀshore Wind Energy Advocates
The American Clean Power Association (ACPA), (formerly the American Wind Energy
Association) represents the interests of wind energy professionals and encourages BOEM
to conduct an auction and lease sale of multiple sites in the existing Morro Bay Call
Area during 2021. In general, ACPA caution BOEM from excluding areas for oﬀshore
wind development at this stage based on the various factors and potentially conﬂicting
uses outlined in the Notice" (Bachtel and Sopko, 2019), as an insuﬃciently large lease
area may cause a "project may fail before it could even be completed". For example,
"with regards to vessel traﬃc routes, ACPA strongly discourages BOEM from generically
excluding oﬀshore wind development from vessel traﬃc routes because the projects can
be planned and sited to be generally harmonious with marine navigation."
Oﬀshore Wind CA is a coalition of wind energy professionals, including multiple
developers, substructure designers, and others that have joined to advocate for ﬂoating
wind in CA. This group acts as a lobbying organization for wind energy in the region
intending to spread awareness and accelerate the growth of the industry. Some of their
main points in favor of oﬀshore wind point to electricity reliability and job creation as
favorable outcomes of industry growth.
B.2.4
Potential Oﬀtakers
B.2.4.1
California Community Choice Aggregations (CCAs)
CA’s energy system is unique due to the presence of Community Choice Aggregations,
which allow cities or counties to buy or generate electricity for residents within their
communities.
In the area of Morro Bay, the primary CCA is Central Coast Community Energy
(3CE), formerly Monterey Bay Community Power, a joint power authority that serves
19 jurisdictions in Monterey, Santa Cruz, San Benito, San Luis Obispo, and Morro Bay
Counties. The CA CCAs are generally enthusiastic about renewable energy development,
claiming "Central Coast Community Energy is committed to reducing greenhouse gas
emissions through local control of utility-scale renewable electricity generation provided
at competitive rates and the implementation of innovative energy programs that facilitate
==PAGE:177==
B.3 Stakeholder Interviews: Additional Comments
159
the electriﬁcation of the transportation and built environments" (Habashi, 2019). For
example, 3CE partnered with Silicon Valley Clean Energy (SVCE) to contract 200 MW
of onshore wind energy, 278 MW of solar, and 80 MW of storage.
3CE is a proponent of Community Beneﬁts Agreements and multi-factor auction
formats that prioritize local support and needs in the lease criteria. Speciﬁcally, 3CE
lauded Castle Wind for the formation of CBAs. As expected by their support of oﬀshore
wind development, 3CE supports the designation of lease areas no smaller than 200
square miles in size.
B.3
Stakeholder Interviews: Additional Comments
B.3.1
National Marine Sanctuaries
A representative from the BOEM Task Force outlines three potential ways that the
timeline of the proposed sanctuary might overlap with oﬀshore wind development.
"[If the lease comes ﬁrst, the wind farm] would be grandfathered in, because
it would be preexisting. You could have technically an oﬀshore wind farm
and a sanctuary. I don’t know if it’d be completely outside the sanctuary or
if it would be incorporated into the sanctuary and just have an extra layer of,
I’ll call it bureaucracy.
The second scenario is that it happened simultaneously. So, the National
Marine Sanctuaries start going through the approval process for their envi-
ronmental review at the same time, we go through our environmental review.
So then you have environmental reviews happening at the same time.
The last scenario, which would be the sanctuary gets approved before voting
completes its process. And if that occurred, it would be probably on the
sanctuary side, whether or not they would want to continue with having wind
energy in it so they would have an option to integrate wind energy into the
sanctuary or not."(Member of the BOEM Task Force, 2021)
In the case of the third scenario, developers also express uncertainty "The Sanctuaries
Act is silent on anything but prohibited activities... So while you the sanctuary oﬃce can
issue a permit to conduct prohibited activities. You don’t have the authority to install
or to authorize" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021).
B.4
California Electricity Market
The CA Independent System Operator (CAISO) is responsible for the state’s electricity
grid. They manage to match the total energy demand on a day with the total energy
supply by the diﬀerent electricity generation types in CA (Gong, 2020). To balance the
grid CAISO needs to be able to track and predict the volume of energy generated from the
state’s sources (Gong, 2020). The duck curve, illustrated in Figure B.5, (introduced by
==PAGE:178==
160
B ANT Appendix
California Independent System Operator (CAISO) (2016)) represents a 24-hour timeline
of the netload. The characteristic of the curve is a mid-day drop followed by an increase
between 3 and 7 pm. A high solar penetration during the day when the sun is the
strongest explains the mid-day drop, in which period a high volume of solar-generated
energy is fed into the grid. The increase in the evening is referred to as high electricity
demand and a lack of solar generating energy (Gong, 2020).
For a "normalization" of the duck curve other energy sources then solar power are
needed. Due to constant wind speeds far oﬀshore and high wind speeds in the afternoon
throughout the morning, oﬀshore wind energy could be a feasible solution in CA to
normalize the duck curve (Collier, 2020).
Figure B.5: CA Duck Curve(Gong, 2020)
Figure B.6 shows the total electricity generation in the whole state of CA by generation
type in 2019 (Nyberg, 2021). The generation is heavily driven by Natural Gas followed
by Hydro Power and Solar Power.
==PAGE:179==
B.4 California Electricity Market
161
Figure B.6: CA Total Energy Generation in 2019(Nyberg, 2021)
The electricity generation in the county of San Luis Obispo (SLO) is currently heavily
driven by nuclear power from the Diablo Canyon Power Plant, operated by Paciﬁc Gas
and Electric. The power plant produces, 18,000 GWh, constituting 89.9% of the county’s
generation in 2018. The rest of SLO’s electrical generation is from renewable sources.
This nuclear reactor is set to retire in 2025, creating a signiﬁcant vacuum of generation
that must be met with sustainable resources.
The County of Monterey is similarly reliant on natural gas, with only a small fraction
of generation deriving from renewable. San Luis Obispo currently mainly renewable solar
and Moneterey solar and a small portion of wind (Yolo et al., 2019).
The Paciﬁc Gas & Electric Company (PG&E) is one of the largest natural gas and
electric energy companies in the US. It is regulated by CA Public Utilities Commission
(CPUC). The company is in charge of the transmission and delivery of energy in the
service areas of northern and central CA (Paciﬁc Gas and Electric Company, 2021).
The Los Angeles Department of Water and Power (LADWP) is an independent
operator from CAISO serving the area of Los Angeles County. Currently, Los Angeles
has a goal to operate with 100% renewable energy by 2035 entitled the LA100, and is
currently conducting a scenario analysis with NREL to explore potential pathways to
reach this target.
B.4.1
Supply Chain and Commercialization
On the road to deployment, those interviewed place focus on the need for the development
of a supply chain on the west coast. Substructure manufacturers cite the development of
a supply chain as a second step in the growth of an oﬀshore wind energy industry on
the West Coast, "initially support is needed, and as projects get deployed, then comes
infrastructure and investment and growth, you see supply chain development, then the
industry really comes together to bring the costs down, like we have seen in Europe"
(Developer 1 (PPL), 2021).
==PAGE:180==
162
B ANT Appendix
Some interviewed discuss the paradoxical nature of the need for supply chain devel-
opment "you need that project pipeline to get the investment in the supply chain, so
it’s at that stage where it’s a bit of a vicious circle where do you need the supply chain
before you can get the project, you need the project willing to invest in a facility" (ORE
Catapult Researcher, 2021).
Many of those interviewed comment on the topic of project scale, weighing into the
debate "does California start like many other nations have in terms of starting with
smaller-scale pilot projects, and then move to commercial scale?" (Developer 1 (PPL),
2021). Researchers add, "demo projects are harder to justify because they’re more
expensive" despite having "a smaller set of problems to solve if you could get somebody
to subsidize the cost" (NREL Researcher 1, 2021). A substructure manufacturer further
justiﬁes the trend toward commercialization, claiming "there’s strong industry momentum
that it doesn’t need to go through that. So why not grow to commercial-scale projects
which will unlock the border beneﬁts of upgrading infrastructure creating jobs and driving
the regeneration of those coastal communities as well as bringing energy supply to those
areas as well" (Developer 1 (PPL), 2021).
B.4.2
Procurement Landscape
Among those interviewed, the viability of oﬀshore wind in CA is tied to its "market
acceptance, where oﬀ-takers are comfortable procuring power that price" (LBNL Re-
searcher, 2021). As CA seeks to reach its energy targets, the cost-competitiveness of
oﬀshore wind in CA comes to head with other renewable technology, but "not just with
solar and storage. That’s the principal competitor, but potentially also land-based wind,
the shift into the state from other states" (LBNL Researcher, 2021). This competition
with other states is tied to interstate transmission.
The cost of oﬀshore wind "can be dramatically inﬂuenced by state procurement
policies and state-required procurement policies mean they are" (LBNL Researcher 2,
2021). For example, "if a state is mandating a certain percentage of renewable energy
needs to be purchased by load-serving entities, they’re going to have to meet those goals
to be able to sell power in that state, and so they’re going to just bear the burden of the
cost and then pass it on to ratepayers" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021).
Many researchers agree that an oﬀshore wind project would "need to have a competitive
PPA for a project to proceed" that will likely have to "come from either a CCA or a
group of CPAs than it is from the major electric utilities" (LBNL Researcher, 2021) as
these load-serving entities have higher larger energy appetites at present. For Castle
Wind, this process has already begun "The CCAs are very interested in oﬀshore wind and
Castle has a memorandum of understanding already with what’s now called the Central
Coast Community Energy CCA" (Developer 2 (Castle Wind), 2021). Some researchers
express interest if the process of securing a PPA in CA might be "tied to some sort of
stakeholder engagement process" (LBNL Researcher 2, 2021), which will be seen further
in the process.
==PAGE:181==

APPENDIX E
IEA37 Simple Bastankhah
Gaussian Wake Model
The wake model applied in this thesis, computing the AEP, is the IEA37 Simple Bas-
tankhah Gaussian Wake Model. The computation methodology is based on the following
order:(IEA37, 2019)
1. Import of the turbine and wind data
• Turbine positions (x,y)
• Turbine technical parameters (vcut−in, vcut −out, vrated and Prated)
• wind bins θi, wind frequency in the bin f(θ)
2. Calculation of the power production for the whole wind farm in each wind direction
a) Calculation of the power production for each turbine including wake losses:
• wake deﬁcit calculation between each turbine pair
• calculate total wake loss with:

(E.5)
Where f is the wind frequency and P the wind farm power, which is then summed
over each binned wind speed direction. The AEP output is then passed to the LCOE
computation.

WACC calculation
A simple calculation of the weighted average cost of capital (WACC) is given by Equation
F.1, where E = market value of equity, D = market value of debt, re = equity return, rd
= debt rate and t= corporate tax rate.
WACC =
E
D + E (re) +
D
D + E (rd)(1 −t)
(F.1)

F.5 ORBIT Details
177
F.5
ORBIT Details
F.5.1
ORBIT Processes
• Transmission system
Array System Design and Installation
– Design: The cost of the array system takes into account the array cable
properties, the wind farm capacity, layout, and depth, and the OSS location to
determine the cable layout, lengths of each cable type, and ultimately, costs.
The maximum number of turbines per cable is calculated as the ratio of the
cable power to the turbine rating. Strings are constructed by adding turbines
to strings starting from the smallest capacity cable available and repeated
the second cable type until both cable types have reached their maximum
capacity.
A grid layout is assumed, with the substation being located on one side of
the grid, located centrally to the strings. The maximum number of strings is
calculated by the cable capacity (it is noted that this is not representative of
the number of strings assumed in the sensitivities below).
There is currently no diﬀerence in the array system cost that accounts for
dynamic cabling – hence the costs associated with the cable type, cable
protection systems, and cable lengths must all be updated in future iterations
of this model.
– Installation: ORBIT calculates the cost of installation of array cables based
on the cable lengths derived in the array system design and the vessel properties
provided. Processes include cable lay and burial (dynamic cables will not be
buried), positioning onsite, cable preparation, lowering, pull-in, and testing.
The costs associated with cable installation are most tied to the number
of trips that a cable lay vessel will have to take to lay the cable, which is
determined by the capacity of the vessel and mass of the cable.
Export System Design and Installation
– Design: The export system takes into account the export cable speciﬁcations
(in this case those associated with the 220 kV 1000 mm2 HVAC) as well as
the wind farm power output and location, including depth and distance from
landfall, to calculate the total export cable length, mass, and the number of
sections. The cost is then calculated on a USD/m basis.
– Installation: The export system installation includes both onshore and
oﬀshore processes. Oﬀshore, the cable lay vessel must lay and bury cable.
These processes vary based on the distance to shore, cable, and vessel properties.
Onshore processes include digging the trench, tow plowing, pull in the winch
and cable, and test the cable. Cable pull-in and testing have set times and all
other properties depend on the length of the cable.
==PAGE:196==
178
F Modeling Assumptions
• Oﬀshore Substation:
– Design: The design of the OSS takes into account the wind farm depth,
number, and rating of turbines to determine the size and cost of the oﬀshore
substation. The main variables calculated are the cost of the substructure,
the topside, shunt reactor, switchgear, ancillary system, as an assembly.
– Installation: The OSS installation includes the installation of the monopile
and topside of the OSS, both of which are pre-set within ORBIT. The time
set to lift the topside and the jack down is calculated based on the results
from the OSS design phase.
